Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Dispute on Judgment by Oath with One Witness
Summarizing madhhab positions on al-yamin ma'a al-shahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and jumhur positions on al-yamin ma'a al-shahid, apply the principle that a khabar ahad cannot override a qat'i text, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A solitary hadith cannot override a clear Quranic text in Hanafi usul.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute: can a judge rule based on a single witness plus the plaintiff's oath? The Hanafi school says no, citing the Quran's clear requirement of two witnesses or one man and two women. The majority (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) say yes, relying on a Prophetic hadith. The disagreement hinges on the authority of the hadith relative to the Quranic text — a fundamental usul question about the hierarchy of proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: Abu Hanifa and his companions hold that judgment by oath with one witness is absolutely impermissible. Note the usul basis: the Quranic verse (2:282) is amm and qat'i; the hadith is a khabar ahad and cannot override it.
The Hanafis do not deny the hadith's existence; they question its authority to specify the Quran.
2
Identify the jumhur position and their proof
The Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis accept the procedure. Their main proof is the hadith: 'The Prophet judged by the oath with one witness.' They classify this hadith as mashhur (well-known), not merely ahad, and thus capable of specifying the Quran.
The term 'mashhur' is crucial: for the jumhur, it raises the hadith's authority above a solitary report.
3
Analyse the Hanafi objection
Hanafis argue that even if the hadith is authentic, it is khabar ahad (zanni al-thubut) and cannot oppose a qat'i text like the Quran. The Quranic requirement is general, and a solitary report cannot restrict it.
This reflects the Hanafi principle: 'Al-amm yufidu al-wujub wa la yukhassu bi khabar ahad.' (A general command indicates obligation and is not specified by a solitary report.)
4
Examine the jumhur response
The jumhur counter that the hadith is transmitted by multiple Companions (e.g., Ibn Abbas, Abu Hurayra, Jabir), making it mashhur. For them, a mashhur hadith has higher authority than a single ahad, and can specify the general Quranic text. They also note that this practice was accepted by many early scholars.
The dispute thus shifts to the classification of the hadith: ahad vs mashhur, and the epistemological weight each school assigns.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis will not accept a case with one witness and an oath; the judge must require two witnesses (or one man and two women). The jumhur will accept the procedure in financial and property cases, allowing the judge to rule based on the oath plus one witness.
This shows that usul differences directly affect fiqh rulings.

Chapter Two / Disagreement in the Text (Matn) ... [The view of judgment by oath with one witness is held by] al-Baqir, Rabi'a al-Ra'y, Abu al-Zinad, Ibn Abi Layla, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, Abu 'Ubayd, and Dawud ibn 'Ali. And it is a narration from 'Urwa ibn al-Zubayr and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, and it is narrated from Ibn Sirin and Yahya ibn Ya'mar, and al-Zuhri. And the Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis adopted it. One of the common proofs among these three schools is the hadith: 'The Prophet judged by the oath with one witness.' Discussion about it will come. Fourth: It is narrated that judgment by oath with one witness is absolutely not accepted in anything. That is attributed to al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, 'Ata', al-Thawri, al-Awza'i, Ibn Shubruma, Ibrahim, and al-Hakam ibn 'Utayba. And it is a narration from 'Urwa ibn al-Zubayr, al-Zuhri, and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz. And Abu Hanifa and his companions held this view. Ibn 'Abd al-Barr mentioned that this opinion was not narrated from any Companion. They responded to the hadith by saying it contradicts the text of the Noble Qur'an, which is His statement: 'And call to witness two witnesses from among your men. And if there are not two men, then a man and two women from among those whom you accept as witnesses' (2:282). The impediment to acting on this report is that it is ahad (solitary) contradicting the Great Book. The majority responded to this objection by saying that this hadith, at the very least, is mashhur (well-known). It has been narrated from several Companions: 1. 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas: recorded by al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Abu Ya'la, Ibn al-Jarud, al-Tahawi, al-Tabarani, and al-Bayhaqi. 2. Abu Hurayra: recorded by al-Shafi'i, al-Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and al-Tahawi. 3. Jabir ibn 'Abdullah: recorded by Ahmad, Ibn Majah, Ibn al-Jarud, and al-Bayhaqi. 4. Surraq: recorded by Ibn Majah and al-Bayhaqi. And it has also been narrated from the hadith of 'Umar, 'Ali, Ibn 'Umar, and Abu Sa'id al-Khudri.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the main positions on the issue of ruling by an oath along with one witness (al-yamin ma'a al-shahid). It first lists the scholars and schools that accept this practice: from the early generations (al-Baqir, etc.) and then the three major sunni madhhabs—Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali. Their common proof is a hadith stating that the Prophet himself judged by an oath along with one witness. The passage then presents the fourth view, which is the absolute rejection of this practice under any circumstances. This view is attributed to a group of early scholars including al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, and importantly to Abu Hanifa and his companions. The Hanafi position is that such a ruling is not allowed, and they offer two responses. First, they argue that the hadith contradicts the clear Quranic verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:282) which requires two male witnesses or one man and two women. Second, they argue that the hadith is a khabar ahad (solitary report) which cannot override the decisive text of the Quran. The majority (jumhur) responds to this by asserting that the hadith is not a solitary report but rather mashhur (well-known), as it has been narrated through many Companions. They list several Companions from whom it is narrated, including Ibn 'Abbas, Abu Hurayra, Jabir, Surraq, and others. This dispute is a classic example of how the authenticity and authority of a hadith (thubut and dalala) interact with the Quran in Hanafi usul. The Hanafis prioritize the Quran's clear ruling and consider the ahad hadith insufficient to abrogate or specify it, while the majority consider the hadith to be of higher transmission grade (mashhur) and thus acceptable.

Key Terms
اليمين مع الشاهد (al-yamin ma'a al-shahid): A judicial procedure where the judge rules based on the plaintiff's oath along with the testimony of a single witness.
مشهور (mashhur): A hadith transmitted by at least two Companions and then by multiple chains, but not reaching tawatur.
خبر آحاد (khabar ahad): A hadith that does not meet the conditions of tawatur; transmitted by one or a few chains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why can the hadith 'the Prophet judged by oath with one witness' not be applied?
A) It is fabricated.
B) It contradicts Quran 2:282.
C) It is weak in its chain of transmission.
D) It is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over al-yamin ma'a al-shahid illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that a khabar ahad cannot specify or override a qat'i amm text from the Quran.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Three Methods to Resolve Apparent Contradictions in Evidences
Explaining the three methods to resolve apparent contradiction: jam', naskh, tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three methods of resolving apparent contradictions in legal evidences and apply them in the correct order according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Reconciliation (jam') is always the first duty when evidences seem to conflict.

This passage introduces the foundational principle that legal rulings cannot truly contradict each other. When two evidences appear to conflict, the jurist must first attempt to reconcile them, then consider abrogation, and only then resort to preponderance. This hierarchy ensures that as many evidences as possible remain operative.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that apparent contradiction is not real
The text establishes that contradiction is only apparent; true conflict is impossible because all rulings come from one source, Allah.
Imam Ibn Khuzaymah's statement supports this.
2
Ensure the two evidences are equal in strength
If one evidence is weak (e.g., a weak hadith) and the other is authentic, the contradiction is ignored; the weak evidence cannot oppose the strong one.
Contradiction is only relevant between equivalent evidences.
3
Attempt reconciliation (jam')
This is the primary and obligatory first step. The mujtahid must try to reconcile the two evidences in a reasonable way to give effect to both.
Reconciliation is preferred over discarding any evidence.
4
If reconciliation fails, check for abrogation (naskh)
If reconciliation is not possible and the historical chronology is known, the later evidence abrogates the earlier one.
This requires knowledge of the dates of revelation or narration.
5
If neither works, apply preponderance (tarjih)
When jam' and naskh are both impossible, the mujtahid prefers one evidence over the other using valid grounds such as the number of narrators or corroborating evidence.
This is the last resort.

Chapter Two / Disagreement in the Text
As is established in their principles.
Third Section
Contradiction of a Hadith by a Stronger Hadith
161
There is no doubt that the sources of legal rulings are one: Allah, Blessed and Exalted. If that is the case, many scholars have held that it is impossible for two equivalent evidences to come in legislation regarding the same matter, such that neither has a preference while they conflict in every respect (2).
But despite that, we find a number of legal evidences that appear to the observer—at first glance—to be contradictory in the apparent sense, but in reality there is no contradiction between them; hence Imam Ibn Khuzaymah said: 'I do not know that two hadiths with two authentic chains contradictory to each other have been narrated from the Prophet—so whoever has such, let him bring it to me so that I may reconcile between them' (3).
The hadith scholars and usul scholars have shared interest in this aspect and devoted a considerable portion of their intellectual efforts to it by thoroughly researching it in their works. The usul scholars devoted a chapter to it called 'Contradiction and Preference,' while the hadith scholars assigned it a type of hadith science called 'Mukhtalif al-Hadith,' discussed in books of terminology, and some of them wrote independent works on it.
Both groups have taken three approaches to this apparent contradiction:
1. Reconciliation (al-Jam').
2. Abrogation (al-Naskh).
3. Preponderance (al-Tarjih).
These approaches are not optional for the mujtahid; rather they are obligatory in their order. The mujtahid seeks reconciliation in any possible way without distortion because reconciliation gives effect to both evidences, and giving effect to both is preferable to discarding one or discarding them all (4).
(1) Fawatih al-Rahmut, 2/128; and see: Masa'il min al-Fiqh al-Muqaran, 2/199-208.
(2) Tawjih al-Nazar, 1/523.
(3) See: Ma'rifat Anwa' 'Ilm al-Hadith: 258, and our print 391; and Sharh al-Tabsira, 2/302, al-'Ilmiyya ed., our print 2/109.
(4) Mukhtalif al-Hadith bayna al-Muhaddithin wa al-Usuliyyin al-Fuqaha': 28.
If he does not find a way to reconcile, then if he knows the date of the earlier and later, he says abrogation; if not, then he resorts to preponderance by one of its valid aspects. (Sair)
Then indeed this contradiction is only relevant when the two evidences are equal in strength. But if one is authentic and the other weak, then the contradiction by the weak is not considered, for the weak is not acceptable in itself, so how can it stand in opposition to what is stronger?
The methodologies of jurists and legal schools have differed in adopting approaches to remove contradiction between apparently conflicting equivalent legal evidences. Some find a way to reconcile them, while others see reconciliation as forced and thus resort to abrogation, and so on. This has led to differences among jurists in deriving the rulings indicated by those evidences. This is clarified by the following examples:
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a general usul rule on how to handle apparent contradictions between legal evidences. The author begins by affirming that legal rulings originate from one source—Allah—so it is impossible for two truly equivalent evidences to conflict in reality. However, some evidences may appear contradictory at first glance. The hadith scholar Ibn Khuzaymah boldly claimed he knew of no two authentic contradictory hadiths, implying that any apparent contradiction can be reconciled. The scholars have three methods in a fixed order: first, reconciliation (jam'), giving effect to both evidences; second, abrogation (naskh) if one is earlier; third, preponderance (tarjih) if no reconciliation or date known. These methods are not optional; the mujtahid must follow this sequence. The contradiction is only considered if the evidences are equal in strength; a weak evidence cannot oppose a strong one. Differences among jurists arise due to varying applications of these methods.

Key Terms
التعارض (al-ta'āruḍ): Apparent conflict between two legal evidences that are equal in strength, requiring resolution.
الجمع (al-jam'): The method of reconciling two apparently contradictory evidences to give effect to both.
النسخ (al-naskh): The method of abrogation, where one evidence is known to have come later and abrogates the earlier.
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): The method of giving preponderance to one evidence over another based on valid grounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first method a mujtahid must attempt when faced with two apparently contradictory evidences of equal strength?
A) Abrogation (naskh)
B) Preponderance (tarjih)
C) Reconciliation (jam')
D) Ignoring both evidences

Key Takeaway
The fixed hierarchy of jam', naskh, and tarjih ensures that apparent contradictions between evidences are resolved without discarding valid proofs.

The First Madhhab on Shuf'a: Right of the Co-Owner
Introducing the first madhhab on shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first madhhab on shuf'a and explain its basis in co-ownership (khulta).

Core Idea
Shuf'a is restricted to the co-owner of the very property sold.

This passage introduces the first of two madhhabs regarding who has the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). The first madhhab, held by the majority of jurists, ties shuf'a exclusively to co-ownership in the specific property (khulta). Understanding this position is essential for grasping how differences in legal interpretation arise from the same sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
The issue is: who is entitled to the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) when a co-owned property is sold to an outsider?
2
State the First Madhhab
The first madhhab holds that shuf'a is established by co-ownership in the property itself (khulta). Only the partner whose ownership in the specific property remains has the right.
This excludes partners in other ventures or mere neighbors.
3
Note the Supporters
The text lists many Companions (e.g., 'Umar, 'Ali, Ibn 'Abbas) and later scholars who held this view, indicating its strength.
This is considered the majority view (jumhūr).
4
Define Key Terms
Understand 'shuf'a' (pre-emption) and 'khulta' (co-ownership in the specific property). The right is tied to the property itself, not any other relationship.
This definition is critical for distinguishing between the two madhhabs.
5
Anticipate the Second Madhhab
The text mentions there is a second madhhab (not detailed here). This sets up a comparison: why would some scholars differ?
Differences often stem from interpreting the same hadith differently or from different principles of istinbāt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النموذج الأول:
من يثبت له حق الشفعة:
اختلف الفقهاء فيمن يثبت لَهُ حق الشفعة عَلَى مذهبين:
المذهب الأول: تثبت الشفعة بالخلطة، أي: أن الذي يستحق الشفعة هُوَ الشريك الَّذِي لا تزال شركته قائمة وَهُوَ المسمى: الشريك في عين المبيع فقط. وبهذا قَالَ جمهور الفقهاء، روي هَذَا عن عمر وعثمان () وعلي وابن عَبَّاس وجابر وعمر بن عبد العزيز وسعيد بن المسيب وسليمان بن يسار والزهري ويحيى بن سعيد الأنصاري وربيعة بن أبي عَبْد الرَّحْمن وأبي الزناد والمغيرة بن عَبْد الرَّحْمن (۳) والأوزاعي وإسحاق وأبي ثور وابن المنذر (4).
(۱) نزهة النظر: ۱۰۳ فما بعدها. (۲) عثمان بن عفان بن أبي العاص الأموي، أمير المؤمنين أحد الخلفاء الأربعة والعشرة المبشرة استشهد سنة (٣٥) هـ).
معجم الصَّحَابَة ٣٩٤٥/١١ ، وتهذيب الكمال ١٢٦/٥ (٤٤٣٦)، والتقريب (٤٥٠٣).
(۳) المغيرة بن عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن عَبْد الله القرشي أبو هاشم ثقة لَهُ ،غرائب، توفي في حدود سنة (۱۸۰هـ).
:انظر تهذيب الكمال ۱۹۹/۷ (٦٧۳۲)، وسير أعلام النبلاء ١٦٦/٨ و ١٦٧، والتقريب (٦٨٤٥). (٤) الجامع الكبير ٤٧/٣ عقب (۱۳۷۰)، والإشراف عَلَى مذاهب أهل العِلم ٥/٢، والتهذيب ٣٣٧/٤، والمغني ٤٦١/٥.
First Model:
Who has the right of pre-emption (shuf'a)?
The jurists differed over who has the right of shuf'a according to two madhhabs:
The first madhhab: shuf'a is established by co-ownership (khulta), meaning that the one entitled to shuf'a is the partner whose partnership remains, and he is called: the partner in the very thing sold only. This is the view of the majority of jurists. This was narrated from 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali, Ibn 'Abbas, Jabir, 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, Sulayman ibn Yasar, al-Zuhri, Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Ansari, Rabi'a ibn Abi 'Abd al-Rahman, Abu al-Zinad, al-Mughira ibn 'Abd al-Rahman (3), al-Awza'i, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, and Ibn al-Mundhir (4).
(1) Nuzhat al-Nazar: 103 and following. (2) 'Uthman ibn 'Affan ibn Abi al-'As al-Umawi, Commander of the Faithful, one of the four caliphs and the ten promised Paradise, martyred in 35 AH.
Mu'jam al-Sahaba 11/3945, Tahdhib al-Kamal 5/126 (4436), al-Taqrib (4503).
(3) Al-Mughira ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Abd Allah al-Qurashi Abu Hashim: trustworthy with some odd narrations, died around 180 AH.
See: Tahdhib al-Kamal 7/199 (6732), Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' 8/166-167, al-Taqrib (6845). (4) Al-Jami' al-Kabir 3/47 after (1370), al-Ishraf 'ala Madhahib Ahl al-'Ilm 2/5, al-Tahdhib 4/337, al-Mughni 5/461.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage introduces the first of two madhhabs (schools) regarding who has the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). Pre-emption is a legal right allowing a co-owner to buy the share sold to a third party. The first madhhab, held by the majority of jurists including many Companions and early scholars, states that shuf'a is established only by 'khulta'—meaning the right belongs exclusively to the partner whose ownership in the property itself remains. This is the partner who owns a share of the specific property being sold, not a mere partner in other rights. The passage lists many supporters of this view. The second madhhab is not detailed here but is mentioned as being a different view. This example illustrates how differences in interpreting evidences can lead to divergent legal rulings.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption: the right of a co-owner to purchase the share of a property being sold to an outsider.
الخلطة (al-khulta): Co-ownership in the specific property itself, which gives the right of pre-emption.
الشريك (al-sharīk): The partner in the same property, as opposed to a partner in other rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first madhhab mentioned, who is entitled to the right of pre-emption (shuf'a)?
A) Any neighbor
B) Any partner in any venture
C) The partner whose co-ownership in the specific property remains
D) The buyer of the property

Key Takeaway
The first madhhab on shuf'a restricts the right to the co-owner of the specific property, based on the principle of khulta.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Dispute Over Shuf'a: Hanafis vs Majority
Presents two madhhabs on who has the right of pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how differing acceptance and interpretation of a hadith leads the Hanafi and majority schools to different rulings on shuf'a, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Textual evidence shapes law: Hanafis include neighbors in shuf'a based on a hadith; the majority restrict to co-owners.

This lesson examines a classical dispute over the pre-emption right. By comparing the Hanafi and majority positions, we see how different approaches to hadith evidence—whether a specific narration is accepted and how it is interpreted—lead to divergent rulings. This is a concrete example of the impact of ikhtilaf al-hadith on fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main views
From the passage, list the Hanafi position and the majority position on who is entitled to shuf'a. Note that the passage also mentions a nuance within the Hanbali school.
The majority includes Malikis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, and Imamis.
2
Locate the evidence cited for the Hanafi view
The passage states that the Hanafis relied on a hadith narrated by 'Abd al-Malik ibn Abi Sulayman. Identify the chain reference. The text of the hadith is not given, but this citation is key.
No evidence is cited for the majority in this passage.
3
Trace the usul implication
Consider why the schools differ: the Hanafis accept and interpret this hadith to include neighbors; the majority either do not accept the hadith or interpret 'partner' strictly. This reflects differences in the rules of hadith criticism and textual interpretation.
This is a classic example of ikhtilaf arising from conflicting or differently evaluated hadiths.
4
Apply the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In a Hanafi jurisdiction, a neighbor can claim pre-emption; in a Shafi'i or Maliki jurisdiction, he cannot. This has real-world impact on property rights.
The passage does not discuss priority rules among multiple claimants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النموذج الأول:
من يثبت له حق الشفعة:
اختلف الفقهاء فيمن يثبت لَهُ حق الشفعة عَلَى مذهبين:
المذهب الأول: تثبت الشفعة بالخلطة، أي: أن الذي يستحق الشفعة هُوَ الشريك الَّذِي لا تزال شركته قائمة وَهُوَ المسمى: الشريك في عين المبيع فقط. وبهذا قَالَ جمهور الفقهاء، روي هَذَا عن عمر وعثمان وعلي وابن عَبَّاس وجابر وعمر بن عبد العزيز وسعيد بن المسيب وسليمان بن يسار والزهري ويحيى بن سعيد الأنصاري وربيعة بن أبي عَبْد الرَّحْمن وأبي الزناد والمغيرة بن عَبْد الرَّحْمن والأوزاعي وإسحاق وأبي ثور وابن المنذر. وإليه ذهب المالكية والشافعية والحنابلة والإمامية. وهناك من أثبت حق الشفعة - إضافة للشريك في عين المبيع – للشريك في حق المبيع. وَهُوَ رِوَايَة عن الإمام أحمد، واختارها ابن تيمية وابن القيم من الحنابلة، وبنحوه قال ابن حزم؛ إلا أنَّهُ لَمْ يجعلها للشريك في حق المبيع مطلقاً، وإنما خصها بكونه شريكاً في الطريق فَقَط.
المذهب الثاني: أثبتوا حق الشفعة للجار والشريك عَلَى تفاصيل لَهُمْ في تعيين من هُوَ أولى بِهَا. وبهذا قَالَ: ابن شبرمة والثوري وابن المبارك وابن أبي ليلى وإليه ذهب أبو حَنِيفَةَ وأصحابه. وبنحوه قال الزيدية.
واستدل أصحاب المذهب الثاني بِمَا رَوَاهُ عَبْد الملك بن أبي سليمان
Model One: Who is entitled to the right of pre-emption? Jurists differed on who is entitled to shuf'a into two schools. The first school: shuf'a is established by mixing (khalṭa), i.e., the one entitled is the partner whose partnership remains, called the partner in the specific property (sharīk fī 'ayn al-mabī') only. This is the view of the majority, reported from 'Umar, 'Uthmān, 'Alī, Ibn 'Abbās, Jābir, 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Azīz, and others. The Mālikīs, Shāfi'īs, Ḥanbalīs, and Imāmīs also adhere to this. There is also one who established shuf'a - in addition to the partner in the specific property - for the partner in the right of the property (sharīk fī ḥaqq al-mabī'). This is a narration from Imam Aḥmad, chosen by Ibn Taymiyya and Ibn Qayyim from the Ḥanbalīs, and similar is said by Ibn Ḥazm, except that he did not make it absolute for the partner in the right, but restricted it to being a partner in the pathway only. The second school: they established shuf'a for the neighbor and the partner with their details in determining who is more deserving. This was said by Ibn Shubruma, al-Thawrī, Ibn al-Mubārak, Ibn Abī Laylā, and Abū Ḥanīfa and his companions. Similar is said by the Zaydīs. The proponents of the second school adduced what 'Abd al-Malik ibn Abī Sulaymān narrated.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is the first illustrative example of how differing approaches to conflicting texts lead to juristic differences. It presents a complete dispute on the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). The majority (including Mālik, Shāfi'ī, Aḥmad) restrict shuf'a to the actual co-owner in the property being sold (sharīk fī 'ayn al-mabī'). The Ḥanafī school, represented by Abū Ḥanīfa and his companions, extends shuf'a to the neighbor (jār) as well, based on a hadith (not fully quoted here). The text also mentions a middle position within the Ḥanbalī school, where some include the partner in the right (e.g., right of way). The existence of these differing positions demonstrates the practical impact of the methodological differences in dealing with evidences.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption; the right to take over a sold property from the buyer by paying the price.
الشريك في عين المبيع (al-sharīk fī 'ayn al-mabī'): The co-owner in the specific property being sold; the majority restricts shuf'a to this person.
الشريك في حق المبيع (al-sharīk fī ḥaqq al-mabī'): A partner in a right connected to the property, e.g., right of way; some Ḥanbalīs include this.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The Hanafī school's evidence for including the neighbor in shuf'a is:
A) The Qur'an
B) A hadith narrated by 'Abd al-Malik ibn Abī Sulaymān
C) Ijmā'
D) Qiyās

Key Takeaway
The difference over shuf'a shows how divergent acceptance and interpretation of hadith leads to distinct rulings in Islamic law.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Shuf'a Dispute: Conflicting Hadith Texts and Their Effect on Legal Rulings
Presents two madhhab positions and their hadith proofs on shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how two conflicting hadith texts lead to different rulings on shuf'a between the Hanafi school and the majority, and evaluate the Hanafi response to the opposing proof.

Core Idea
Two authentic hadith on shuf'a conflict; the Hanafi school privileges the hadith giving the neighbor the right, while the majority restricts it to partners.

This lesson examines a classic ikhtilaf between the Hanafi school and the majority over the scope of shuf'a (preemption). The disagreement arises from two different hadith narrated from Jabir ibn 'Abd Allah. By analyzing each hadith's text and the schools' interpretations, we see how differences in transmitted texts directly produce differences in legal rulings. This illustrates a key usul al-fiqh principle: the weight given to one proof over another determines the madhhab position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Hanafi position (neighbor has shuf'a) and the majority position (only partner in the property). Note the intra-madhhab variations among Hanbalis.
The minority among Hanbalis also extend shuf'a to a partner in the right.
2
Examine each school's proof
Locate the specific hadith cited by each side. The Hanafis cite: 'al-jar ahaqqu bi shuf'ati jarihi...' The majority cite: 'al-shuf'atu fima lam yuqsam...'
Both hadith are from Jabir, but through different chains.
3
Compare the texts and conditions
Analyze the operative phrases: Hanafi hadith mentions 'neighbor' and 'shared road'; majority hadith restricts to 'undivided property' and mentions 'boundaries and roads'.
The Hanafi hadith explicitly gives precedence to the neighbor, while the majority hadith seems to negate shuf'a after division.
4
Apply Hanafi tarjih methodology
Consider how Hanafi usul would reconcile or prefer one hadith over the other. Note that both are ahad, so tarjih may be based on textual specificity or contextual factors.
The Hanafi response is not detailed in this passage, but the precedent of preferring the neighbor hadith is evident.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: In Hanafi law, a neighbor with a shared road can claim shuf'a; in majority schools, only partners in the property can.
This difference affects property transactions and rights of adjoining owners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في المتن
وإليه ذهب المالكية والشافعية والحنابلة والإمامية.
وهناك من أثبت حق الشفعة - إضافة للشريك في عين المبيع – للشريك في حق المبيع.
وَهُوَ رِوَايَة عن الإمام أحمد، واختارها ابن تيمية وابن القيم من الحنابلة، وبنحوه قال ابن حزم؛ إلا أنَّهُ لَمْ يجعلها للشريك في حق المبيع مطلقاً، وإنما خصها بكونه شريكاً في الطريق فَقَط.
المذهب الثاني: أثبتوا حق الشفعة للجار والشريك عَلَى تفاصيل لَهُمْ في تعيين من هُوَ أولى بِهَا. وبهذا قَالَ: ابن شبرمة والثوري وابن المبارك وابن أبي ليلى وإليه ذهب أبو حَنِيفَةَ وأصحابه. وبنحوه قال الزيدية.
واستدل أصحاب المذهب الثاني بِمَا رَوَاهُ عَبْد الملك بن أبي سليمان العرزمي، عن عطاء، عن جابر، مرفوعاً: «الجار أحق بشفعة جاره، ينتظر بها، وإن كَانَ غائباً، إذا كَانَ طريقهما واحداً».
رَوَاهُ الطيالسي وعبد الرزاق وابن أبي شيبة وأحمد والدارمي وأبو داود وابن ماجه والترمذي وفي العلل الكبير والنسائي والطحاوي والطبراني والبيهقي وابن عبد البر.
واستدل أصحاب المذهب الأول بما رواه أبو سلمة بن عَبْد الرَّحْمن، عن جابر مرفوعاً: «الشفعة فِيْمَا لَمْ يقسم، فإذا وقعت الحدود، وصرفت الطرق، فَلا شفعة».
رَوَاهُ الشافعي وعبد الرزاق وأحمد وعبد بن حميد والطيالسي والبخاري وأبو داود وابن ماجه والترمذي وابن الجارود والدولابي.
Chapter Two / Difference in the Text. The Malikis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, and Imamis went to it. And there are those who affirmed the right of preemption – in addition to the partner in the very property – for the partner in the right of the property. And it is a narration from Imam Ahmad, and Ibn Taymiyya and Ibn al-Qayyim from the Hanbalis chose it, and Ibn Hazm said similarly; except that he did not make it absolute for the partner in the right of the property, but rather restricted it to being a partner in the road only. The second school: They affirmed the right of preemption for the neighbor and the partner with details for them in determining who is more entitled to it. Ibn Shubruma, al-Thawri, Ibn al-Mubarak, Ibn Abi Layla said this, and Abu Hanifa and his companions went to it. And the Zaydis said similarly. The proponents of the second school argued with what 'Abd al-Malik ibn Abi Sulayman al-'Arzami narrated, from 'Ata', from Jabir, raised: 'The neighbor is more entitled to the preemption of his neighbor, he is waited for, even if he is absent, when their road is one.' Narrated by al-Tayalisi, 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ibn Abi Shayba, Ahmad, al-Darimi, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi, in al-'Ilal al-Kabir, al-Nasa'i, al-Tahawi, al-Tabarani, al-Bayhaqi, and Ibn 'Abd al-Barr. The proponents of the first school argued with what Abu Salama ibn 'Abd al-Rahman narrated, from Jabir, raised: 'Preemption is only in what has not been divided; when boundaries are fixed and roads are laid out, there is no preemption.' Narrated by al-Shafi'i, 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, 'Abd ibn Humayd, al-Tayalisi, al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn al-Jarud, and al-Dulabi.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a classic ikhtilaf (disagreement) in Hanafi fiqh concerning the scope of shuf'a (preemption or right of first refusal). The author first outlines the majority position (Malikis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, Imamis), which restricts shuf'a to a partner in the property itself. A minority among the Hanbalis, including Ibn Taymiyya and Ibn al-Qayyim, extend it to a partner in the right (such as a right of way). Ibn Hazm further limits it only to a partner in the road. The second position, adopted by Abu Hanifa and his companions, grants shuf'a to both the neighbor and the partner, with details on precedence. This is the Hanafi view. The evidence for each side is two conflicting hadiths from Jabir ibn 'Abd Allah. The Hanafis rely on the hadith: 'The neighbor is more entitled to the preemption of his neighbor... when their road is one.' The majority rely on the hadith: 'Preemption is only in what has not been divided; when boundaries are fixed and roads are laid out, there is no preemption.' The passage demonstrates how differences in hadith texts (mutun) directly lead to differences in legal rulings among the schools.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): حق التملك الجبري للشريك أو الجار في العقار المباع بثمنه.
شريك (sharik): من يشارك في ملكية العقار أو في حق من حقوقه.
جار (jar): الجار الملاصق أو القريب في الطريق.
الاختلاف في المتن (al-ikhtilaf fi al-matan): الاختلاف بين الروايات الحديثية من حيث النص اللفظي.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) حديث «الشفعة فيما لم يقسم»
B) حديث «الجار أحق بشفعة جاره»
C) حديث «لا ضرر ولا ضرار»
D) حديث «من باع بيعاً فلا شفعة»

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school extends shuf'a to a neighbor sharing a road based on a specific hadith, while the majority restricts it to partners in the property based on another hadith, illustrating how textual differences in hadith lead to divergent legal rulings.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Narrator's Practice Contradicting His Narration: Hanafi Condition vs. Majority View
Explains the condition that narrator's practice must not contradict his narration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi condition for accepting khabar al-ahad regarding the narrator's practice, distinguish al-Jassas's two-case analysis, and evaluate the majority's counter-argument.

Core Idea
If a narrator acts contrary to his narration, Hanafis reject the hadith; the majority upholds the text.

This lesson examines a critical usul al-fiqh dispute: does a narrator's own practice affect the reliability of a solitary hadith? The Hanafis argue that contradiction indicates certain knowledge overriding the text, while the majority insists the text remains primary. We will explore the reasoning on both sides and a key refinement by Abu Bakr al-Jassas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested condition
What is the condition that Hanafis add for accepting khabar al-ahad? Find the phrase in the passage that states the narrator must not act contrary to his narration.
This is a unique Hanafi requirement not shared by the majority.
2
Understand the Hanafi reasoning
Why do Hanafis impose this condition? According to the passage, acting contrary implies certainty of abrogation, interpretation, or specification, gained from direct observation, hearing, or ijma.
The reasoning is based on the idea that the narrator would not contradict unless he had definitive knowledge.
3
Analyze al-Jassas's distinction
Abu Bakr al-Razi al-Jassas divides cases into two: (a) the hadith is open to interpretation – here the narrator's interpretation is not adopted without evidence; (b) the hadith is not open to interpretation – here the hadith is suspended. Identify these two cases in the text.
Al-Jassas provides a nuanced middle position within the Hanafi school.
4
Examine the majority's critique
The majority (including Shafi'i and Malikis) argue that the narrator's action does not necessarily indicate abrogation; the apparent wording of the text remains the effective cause for ruling. What is their main argument?
They hold that the Companion's ijtihad does not override a clear text.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this dispute? In practice, Hanafis may reject a hadith if the narrator contradicts, while the majority accept it as long as the chain is sound. This affects which hadiths are used in Hanafi fiqh.
This difference can lead to divergent rulings on the same issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
مخالفة الحديث لفتوى راويه أو عمله
وضع الحنفية شروطاً للعمل بخبر الآحاد يمكن أن تكون عاضداً للظن الَّذِي
yوجبه خبر الواحد (۹).
ومن بَيْن تِلْكَ الشروط أن لا يعمل الرَّاوِي بخلاف روايته)، ووافقهم عَلَى هَذَا بغض المالكية (۲) ؛ لأنَّه ما عمل بخلافه إلا وَقَدْ تيقن من طريق صحيحة نسخه، أو صرفه عن ظاهره بتأويله أو تخصيصه، سواء كَانَ هَذَا من معاينة حال رَسُول الله ، أو سَمَاع نص جلي صريح مِنْهُ، أو علم إجماع الصَّحَابَة عَلَى خلاف مضمونه، فأوجب هَذَا عَلَيْهِ القول بمقتضى المتأخر من حَيْثُ علمه (۳).
وفصل أبو بكر الرازي الجصاص من الحنفية، فرأى أن الخبر المروي عَلَى هَذِهِ
الصورة لا يخلو عن حالتين:
الأولى: أن يَكُون الخبر محتملاً للتأويل، فعند ذَلِكَ لا يؤخذ بتأويل الصَّحَابِي فمن دونه، ويبقى الخبر عَلَى ظاهره معمولاً بمنطوقه، إلا عِنْدَ قيام دلالة على وجوب صرفه إلى ما يؤوله الرَّاوِي. الثانية: أن لا يحتمل الخبر تأويلاً، ولا يمكن أن يَكُون لفظ الْحَدِيث تعبيراً من
الصَّحَابِي، فهذا الَّذِي يتوقف في قبوله والعمل به).
وجمهور الفقهاء والأصوليين عَلَى خلافه، إذ لا يلزم من مخالفة الصَّحَابِي للحديث الذي يرويه، أن يَكُون قد اطلع عَلَى ناسخ لَهُ، أو بدا لَهُ وجه تأويله)، ثُمَّ إِن المقتضي للحكم هُوَ ظاهر اللفظ في الخبر وَهُوَ قائم وما عارضه من فعل الرَّاوِي لا يصلح أن يَكُون معارضاً؛ وذلك لأن احتمال تمسكه بما ظنه دليلاً - مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ - قائم، وتَدَيّن الصَّحَابِي وإحسان الظن به يمنعه من تعمد الخطأ، أما السهو والغلط فممكن عَلَيْهِ، كَمَا هُوَ ممكن عَلَى غيره (٦) .
وقول الصَّحَابِي - مهما كَانَتْ مكانته - لا تقاوم الوقوف بوجه النص، لا سيما إذا كَانَ النص لا يحتمل التأويل، وإنما يعدُّ هَذَا من اجتهادات ذَلِكَ الصَّحَابِي، والأمة ملزمة بالعمل بالنص، وغير ملزمة بالعمل باجتهادات الصَّحَابَة، قَالَ الشَّافِعِي – رَحِمَهُ
Fourth Topic:
Contradiction of the hadith by the fatwa or practice of its narrator.
The Hanafis set conditions for acting upon khabar al-ahad that can support the speculation that the solitary report necessitates (9).
Among those conditions is that the narrator does not act contrary to his narration (1). Some Malikis agreed with them on this (2), because he did not act contrary to it except that he had become certain through an authentic path of its abrogation, or he turned it away from its apparent meaning by his interpretation or specification, whether this was from direct observation of the Messenger of Allah, or hearing a clear explicit text from him, or knowledge of consensus of the Companions contrary to its purport, so this obligated him to speak according to the later [knowledge] from his part (3).
Abu Bakr al-Razi al-Jassas from the Hanafis made a distinction: he considered that the narrated report in this situation is not free from two cases:
First: that the report is susceptible to interpretation, then in that case the interpretation of the Companion or those below him is not taken, and the report remains on its apparent meaning acted upon by its explicit text, except when evidence arises for the obligation of turning it to what the narrator interpreted. Second: that the report is not susceptible to interpretation, and it is not possible that the wording of the hadith was an expression from the Companion, so this is the one that is suspended in acceptance and acting upon it (4).
The majority of jurists and usul scholars are contrary to him, since it does not necessarily follow from the Companion contradicting the hadith he narrates that he had come to know of an abrogator for it, or had a way of its interpretation (5). Then the effective cause for the ruling is the apparent wording of the report, and it is established, and what opposes it from the narrator's action is not suitable to be an opposition; because the possibility that he adhered to what he thought was evidence—while it is not so—is present, and the religiosity of the Companion and good opinion of him prevents him from intentional error, but forgetfulness and mistake are possible upon him, just as they are possible upon others (6).
The statement of the Companion—whatever his status—does not resist standing in the face of the text, especially if the text does not allow interpretation. Rather this is considered among the ijtihad of that Companion, and the community is obligated to act by the text, and not obligated to act by the ijtihad of the Companions. Al-Shafi'i said—may Allah have mercy on him:
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul principle regarding the condition that a narrator's practice (or fatwa) must not contradict his own narration. The Hanafis, as mentioned, set conditions for accepting khabar al-ahad, including that the narrator does not act contrary to his narration. They argue that if a narrator acts contrary, it indicates he knew something that overrides the apparent meaning. Abu Bakr al-Razi al-Jassas distinguishes two cases: if the hadith can be interpreted, the narrator's interpretation is not necessarily adopted; if it cannot be interpreted, the hadith is suspended. The majority of jurists and usul scholars disagree, stating that the narrator's action does not necessarily indicate abrogation or interpretation; rather, the apparent wording of the hadith remains authoritative, and the narrator's mistake is possible. The Companions' ijtihad does not override a clear text.

Key Terms
مخالفة الحديث لفتوى راويه (mukhalafat al-hadith li fatwa rawiyhi): Situation where a narrator's legal opinion or practice contradicts the hadith he narrates
خبر الآحاد (khabar al-ahad): A solitary hadith that does not reach the level of tawatur
المقتضي للحكم (al-muqtaqi al-hukm): The effective cause or basis for the ruling, here the apparent wording of the hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Bakr al-Razi al-Jassas, what is the ruling on a hadith that is not susceptible to interpretation and whose wording cannot be an expression from the Companion?
A) It is accepted and acted upon
B) It is suspended in acceptance and acting upon
C) It is rejected outright
D) It is interpreted metaphorically

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the narrator's practice illustrates a fundamental tension in usul al-fiqh between prioritizing the apparent meaning of the text and considering the narrator's actions as evidence of abrogation or interpretation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
The Authority of a Sahih Hadith Over All Opinions
Presenting usul principle with fiqh example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the usul principle that a sahih hadith with clear indication is binding over any contrary opinion, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf in the wali dispute as an application of this principle.

Core Idea
A sahih hadith binds the entire Ummah, including the Sahabi; no human opinion can override it.

This lesson examines a foundational usul al-fiqh principle: a sahih hadith with a sound chain and clear meaning is a hujjah (proof) that must be followed, even if it contradicts the practice or fatwa of a Sahabi or any other scholar. Ibn al-Qayyim elaborates on why a narrator might act against his own narration but that does not weaken the hadith's authority. The passage then applies this principle to the fiqh dispute on the requirement of a wali (guardian) in marriage, showing how differences in interpreting and prioritizing hadiths lead to divergent rulings between the Hanafi school and the majority.

Guided Path
1
State the Usul Principle
Identify the core claim: a sahih hadith with clear indication is binding on the entire Ummah, including the Sahabi. Note the conditions: the hadith must be authentic (sahih) and its meaning clear, and there must be no abrogating hadith.
The principle prioritizes the Prophet's words over any human opinion, regardless of the person's status.
2
Analyze Ibn al-Qayyim's Argument
Break down his rationale: he lists several reasons why a narrator might act contrary to a hadith (forgetfulness, misinterpretation, taqlid, etc.) but insists none justify abandoning the hadith. This reinforces the absolute authority of the sahih hadith.
Ibn al-Qayyim's points highlight human fallibility while preserving the hadith's integrity.
3
Apply the Principle to the Wali Dispute
Observe how the usul principle underlies the ikhtilaf: the majority (jumhoor) rely on hadiths requiring a wali, while the Hanafi school relies on hadiths giving a woman authority to marry herself. Both sides follow the same principle of prioritizing hadith, but they differ in which hadiths they consider stronger or more applicable.
The dispute is not about the principle itself but about the interpretation and authenticity of specific narrations.
4
Identify the Hanafi Position and Its Basis
State the Hanafi view: a woman may marry herself without a wali. Note that this is based on narrations from al-Zuhri and al-Sha'bi and the school of Abu Hanifah and Zufar. Recognize that the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold a middle view: wali is required but his obstruction can be overridden by a judge.
The Hanafi position aligns with the principle of taking hadiths that grant women autonomy, even though they are fewer in number according to the majority.
5
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a woman's self-marriage is valid without a wali; under the majority view, such a marriage is invalid. This difference directly impacts the validity of countless contracts.
The principle from this lesson shows that both sides claim to follow the same usul rule, but their different readings of the evidence lead to opposite rulings.

عَنْهُ
والحديث - إذا صح سنده واتضحت دلالته - حجة عَلَى الأمة، بِمَا فِيْهَا الصَّحَابِي (٢) ؛ لذا قَالَ ابن القيم: والذي ندين الله بهِ ولا يسعنا غيره – وَهُوَ القصد في هَذَا الباب - أن الْحَدِيث إذا صَحَّ عن رَسُوْلَ اللهِ ، وَلَمْ يصح حَدِيث آخر ينسخه: أن الفرض علينا وعلى الأمة الأخذ بحديثه وترك كُلّ ما خالفه، ولا نتركه لخلاف أحد من الناس كائناً من كَانَ لا راويه ولا غيره، إذ من الممكن أن ينسى الرَّاوِي الْحَدِيث، أو لا يحضره وقت الفتيا، أو لا يتفطن لدلالته عَلَى تِلْكَ المسألة، أو يتأول فيه تأويلاً مرجوحاً، أو يقوم في ظنه ما يعارضه، ولا يَكُون معارضاً في نفس الأمر، أو يقلّد غيره في فتواه بخلافه لاعتقاده أنَّهُ أعلم مِنْهُ، وإنه إنما خالفه لما هُوَ أقوى مِنْهُ، وَلَوْ قُدّر انتفاء ذَلِكَ كله، ولا سبيل إلى العِلْم بانتفائه ولا ظنه، لَمْ يَكُنْ الرَّاوِي معصوماً، وَلَمْ توجب مخالفته لما رَوَاهُ سقوط عدالته حَتَّى تغلب سيئاته حسناته، وبخلاف هذا الْحَدِيث الواحد لا يحصل لَهُ ذَلِكَ»(٣).
ومهما يَكُن الأمر فإن هَذَا التأصيل قد انعكس على المجال الفقهي، فوجدت خلافات بَيْنَ الفقهاء، كَانَ مرجعها إِلَى هَذَا الأصل، ونلمس هَذَا جلياً من خلال الأمثلة الآتية:

اختلف الفقهاء في اشتراط إذن الولي لصحة عقد النكاح على قولين: الأول: لا عقد النكاح من غَيْر ولي وَهُوَ شرط في صحة العقد. يصح وبهذا قَالَ الجُمْهُور، وَهُوَ مروي عن عمر وعلي وابن مسعود وابن عباس وأبي هُرَيْرَةَ وعائشة. وبه :قَالَ سعيد بن المسيب والحسن وعمر بن عَبْد العزيز وجابر بن زيد ) والثوري وابن أبي ليلى وابن شبرمة وابن المبارك وعبيد الله العنبري وإسحاق بن راهويه وأبو عبيد. وقد روي عن ابن سيرين والقاسم بن مُحَمَّد والحسن بن صالح). وإليه ذهب الشافعية (٣) والمالكية (٣) والحنابلة (4) والظاهرية) والزيدية). وَقَالَ :الصاحبان لا يصح النكاح إلا بولي، فإذا رضي الولي جاز، وإن أبى – والزوج كفوء - أجازه الْقَاضِي ().
الثاني: يجوز للمرأة أن تزوج نفسها مِمَّنْ تشاء، وَلَيْسَ للولي أن يعترض عَلَيْهَا، إذا وضعت نفسها حَيْثُ ينبغي أن تضعها. وَهُوَ مروي عن الزهري والشعبي وإليه ذهب أبو حَنِيْفَةَ وزفر (3)
وأما الإمامية ففصلوا بَيْنَ الثيب والبكر، فإن كَانَتْ بكراً رشيدة فقد اختلف فقهاؤهم فِيْهَا عَلَى أقوال:
١. ثبوت الولاية لنفسها في العقد الدائم والمؤقت.
ثبوت الولاية لنفسها في العقد الدائم دُونَ المنقطع.
.٣ عكس الَّذِي ،قبله، أي: ثبوت الولاية لنفسها في العقد المؤقت دُونَ الدائم. ٤. لَيْسَ لها ولاية عَلَى نفسها سواء كَانَ العقد دائماً أو منقطعاً، إذا كَانَ الولي الأب أو الجد للأب.
ه. الكل شركاء في حق الولاية، فلا يمضي العقد إلا برضا الْجَمِيع. فإن عضلها الولي، وَكَانَ المتقدّم كفوءاً، وكانت راغبة في الزواج مِنْهُ، فلها أن تُزوّج نفسها إجماعاً في المذهب (١). أما الصغيرة فتثبت ولاية الأب والجد للأب عَلَيْهَا بكراً كَانَتْ أو ثيباً، وإذا زوجها
Chapter Two: Differences in the Text. Allah -: How can I leave the hadith for an action of someone whom, if I had lived contemporaneously with him, I would have argued with him? (1). From him. And the hadith - if its chain is authentic and its indication is clear - is a proof upon the Ummah, including the Sahabi (2). Hence Ibn al-Qayyim said: That which we profess allegiance to Allah by and which alone suffices us - and it is the intended meaning in this chapter - is that if a hadith is authentic from the Messenger of Allah, and there is no other hadith that abrogates it, then the obligation upon us and upon the Ummah is to take his hadith and abandon everything that contradicts it, and we do not abandon it due to the opposition of anyone among the people, whether the narrator or anyone else, because it is possible that the narrator forgot the hadith, or it was not present in his mind at the time of issuing a fatwa, or he did not pay attention to its indication concerning that issue, or he interpreted it with a weak interpretation, or something that he thinks contradicts it arose in his mind while in reality it does not contradict it, or he followed someone else in his fatwa contrary to it because he believed that person was more knowledgeable than him, and that his opposition was only because of what he considered stronger. Even if all of that were absent, and there is no way to know of their absence or even to assume it, the narrator is not infallible, and his contradiction of what he narrated does not necessitate the loss of his integrity unless his evil deeds outweigh his good deeds. And contrary to this single hadith does not cause that for him (3). Whatever the case, this fundamental principle has been reflected in the field of fiqh, and differences among jurists were found, the origin of which goes back to this principle, and we notice this clearly in the following examples. The first example: Requiring the guardian in marriage. The jurists differed regarding the requirement of the guardian's permission for the validity of the marriage contract into two opinions: First: There is no marriage contract without a guardian, and it is a condition for the validity of the contract, and this is the opinion of the majority, and it is narrated from Umar, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Abu Hurairah, and Aisha. And it was stated by Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, al-Hasan, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, Jabir ibn Zaid, al-Thawri, Ibn Abi Layla, Ibn Shubruma, Ibn al-Mubarak, Ubaydullah al-Anbari, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and Abu Ubayd. And it has been narrated from Ibn Sirin, al-Qasim ibn Muhammad, and al-Hasan ibn Salih. And to it went the Shafi'is, Malikis, Hanbalis, Zahiris, and Zaydis. And the two companions said: The marriage contract is not valid except with a guardian; if the guardian agrees, it is permissible, and if he refuses - and the husband is suitable - then the judge permits it. Second: It is permissible for a woman to marry herself to whomever she wishes, and the guardian has no right to object to her, if she places herself where she should be placed. This is narrated from al-Zuhri and al-Sha'bi, and to it went Abu Hanifah and Zufar. As for the Imamis, they differentiated between the thayyib and the bikr; if she is an adult virgin, their jurists differed on five opinions: 1. Guardianship is proven for herself in both permanent and temporary contracts. 2. Guardianship is proven for herself in permanent but not temporary contracts. 3. Opposite of the previous: guardianship is proven for herself in temporary but not permanent contracts. 4. She has no guardianship over herself whether the contract is permanent or temporary, if the guardian is the father or paternal grandfather. 5. All are partners in the right of guardianship, so the contract is not executed except with the consent of all. If the guardian prevents her and the prospective husband is suitable and she desires marriage with him, then she may marry herself by consensus in the madhhab. As for the minor, the guardianship of the father and paternal grandfather over her is established whether she is a virgin or thayyib, and if he marries her
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental usul al-fiqh principle: a hadith with a sound chain and clear indication is binding on the entire Ummah, including the Sahabah themselves. The author begins with a statement (likely from a Sahabi) questioning how one could abandon a hadith for the action of someone whom one would have argued with if alive. Then he states the principle and quotes Ibn al-Qayyim (d. 751 AH) who elaborates that when a sahih hadith is established and not abrogated, it must be followed over any contrary opinion, whether from a Sahabi or anyone else. Ibn al-Qayyim gives several reasons why a narrator might act contrary to a hadith he narrated: he may have forgotten it, not recalled it at the time, misinterpreted it, or followed someone he considered more knowledgeable. None of these justify abandoning the hadith. The author then applies this principle to a specific fiqh dispute: the requirement of a guardian (wali) in the marriage contract. He presents two main positions: the majority (including Shafi'is, Malikis, Hanbalis, Zahiris, Zaydis) requiring a wali, and the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifah and Zufar) allowing a woman to marry without a wali. The text also mentions that the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold a middle position: the marriage is not valid without a wali, but if the wali refuses an appropriate match, the judge can intervene. The passage then lists the various Imami Shi'i positions on the issue. The lesson demonstrates how differences in hadith interpretation and the usul principle of prioritizing hadith lead to real fiqh differences. The Hanafi position is based on hadiths that give women authority over themselves, while the majority rely on hadiths requiring wali. The passage does not delve into the specific proofs, but the usul principle is the lens through which the disagreement is understood.

Key Terms
الحديث (al-hadith): A report of the Prophet's words, actions, or approvals; here it specifically refers to a sahih hadith that is a hujjah.
الصحابي (al-sahabi): A companion of the Prophet; the passage asserts that a sahih hadith takes precedence even over a Sahabi's opinion or practice.
الولي (al-wali): The marriage guardian, typically a male relative; the dispute is whether his consent is required for a valid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the usul principle presented in the passage, what should a jurist do when a sahih hadith contradicts the practice of a Sahabi?
A) Follow the Sahabi because he is more knowledgeable.
B) Follow the hadith, as it is binding on the entire Ummah.
C) Reconcile them by assuming the hadith is abrogated.
D) Discard both and use qiyas instead.

Key Takeaway
A sahih hadith with clear indication is binding and must be followed over any contrary opinion, even from a Sahabi, unless abrogated.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Evidence for Wali Requirement: A Usul al-Fiqh Analysis of the Hadith of Aisha
Presents juristic ikhtilaf on marriage without wali with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hadith used by the majority to require a wali in marriage, and explain the Hanafi response based on the epistemological weakness (zanni thubut) of the hadith.

Core Idea
A disputed ahad hadith cannot override the general permissibility of marriage from the Quran; the Hanafi school prioritizes the Quranic dalil.

This lesson examines one of the classic usul al-fiqh disputes: the requirement of a marriage guardian (wali). The majority of schools cite a hadith from Aisha (ra) declaring a marriage without wali invalid. However, the Hanafi school—including internal differences—does not accept this hadith as sufficiently strong to overturn the general permission for women to contract their own marriage. We will analyze the hadith's transmission, its epistemological weight, and the Hanafi reasoning that leads to a different ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the core question being debated? Is a wali a condition for marriage validity?
The divergence stems from different interpretations of the same sources.
2
State the majority position and its evidence
The majority (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, Zahiri, Zaydi) require a wali. Their primary evidence is the hadith of Aisha (ra): 'Any woman who marries without her guardian's permission, her marriage is invalid.'
The hadith is narrated by many compilers, but it remains ahad.
3
State the Hanafi position and internal ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that a woman may marry herself without a wali. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require a wali but allow the judge to authorize if the guardian unjustly refuses a suitable match.
This internal difference shows the range of Hanafi reasoning.
4
Classify the hadith epistemologically
In Hanafi usul, ahad hadith are zanni al-thubut (uncertain transmission) and cannot override qat'i dalil from the Quran. The word 'any woman' is general ('amm), but the hadith is not accepted as definitive.
Hanafis may also consider the hadith as advisory rather than obligatory.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school does not accept the hadith as sufficient to make wali a condition. They may engage in jarh (criticizing narrators) or ta'wil (interpretive reading), or they prioritize Quranic verses (e.g., 2:232, 4:25) that seem to permit women to contract their own marriage.
This illustrates the principle that a zanni dalil cannot abrogate a qat'i one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في المتن
راهويه وأبو عبيد. وقد روي عن ابن سيرين والقاسم بن مُحَمَّد والحسن بن صالح).
وإليه ذهب الشافعية (۳) والمالكية (۳) والحنابلة (4) والظاهرية) والزيدية). وَقَالَ :الصاحبان لا يصح النكاح إلا بولي، فإذا رضي الولي جاز، وإن أبى – والزوج كفوء - أجازه الْقَاضِي ().
الثاني: يجوز للمرأة أن تزوج نفسها مِمَّنْ تشاء، وَلَيْسَ للولي أن يعترض عَلَيْهَا،
إذا وضعت نفسها حَيْثُ ينبغي أن تضعها.
وَهُوَ مروي عن الزهري والشعبي وإليه ذهب أبو حَنِيْفَةَ وزفر (3) (۸)
وأما الإمامية ففصلوا بَيْنَ الثيب والبكر، فإن كَانَتْ بكراً رشيدة فقد اختلف فقهاؤهم فِيْهَا عَلَى أقوال:
۱. ثبوت الولاية لنفسها في العقد الدائم والمؤقت.
ثبوت الولاية لنفسها في العقد الدائم دُونَ المنقطع.
.٣ عكس الَّذِي ،قبله، أي: ثبوت الولاية لنفسها في العقد المؤقت دُونَ الدائم. ٤. لَيْسَ لها ولاية عَلَى نفسها سواء كَانَ العقد دائماً أو منقطعاً، إذا كَانَ الولي الأب أو الجد للأب.
ه. الكل شركاء في حق الولاية، فلا يمضي العقد إلا برضا الْجَمِيع. فإن عضلها الولي، وَكَانَ المتقدّم كفوءاً، وكانت راغبة في الزواج مِنْهُ، فلها أن تُزوّج نفسها إجماعاً في المذهب (۱). أما الصغيرة فتثبت ولاية الأب والجد للأب عَلَيْهَا بكراً كَانَتْ أو ثيباً، وإذا زوجها أحدهما وَهِيَ صغيرة لزمها عقده، ولا خيار لها إذا بلغت عَلَى الأشهر عندهم ) . وإذا كَانَتْ ثيباً بالغةً فليس لأحد ولاية عَلَيْهَا (٢).
واستدل القائلون بالاشتراط بحديث عَائِشَة رضي الله عَنْهَا عَن رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها ،باطل ثلاث مرات، فإن دخل بها فلها المهر بما أصاب مِنْهَا، فإن تشاجروا فالسلطان ولي من لا ولي لَهُ».
رَوَاهُ الشَّافِعِي)، والطيالسي ()، وعبد الرزاق )، والحميدي)، وسعيـد ابــن مَنْصُور ) ، وأحمد (۸)، والدارمي (9) ، وأبو داود (۱) ، وابن ماجه (۱۱)، والترمذي (۱۲) ، والنسائي (۱۳)، وأبو يعلى(١٤) ، وابن الجارود (١٥) ، والطحاوي (١٦) ، وابن حبان (۱) ، وابن عدي (۱۸)، والدارقطني (۱۹)، والحاكم (۲۰)، والسهمي (۲۱)، وأبو نعيم (۲۲) ، والبيهقي (۲۳) ، والخطيب (٢٤) ، وابن عَبْد البر (۲۹) ، والبغوي (٢٦) .
Chapter Two / Variation in the Text. ... And to it went the Shafi'is, Malikis, Hanbalis, Zahiris, and Zaydis. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Marriage is not valid except with a guardian, so if the guardian consents it is permitted, and if he refuses – while the husband is suitable – the judge may authorize it. Second: It is permissible for a woman to marry herself to whomever she wishes, and the guardian has no right to object to her, if she places herself where she ought to be placed. And it is narrated from al-Zuhri and al-Sha'bi, and to it went Abu Hanifa and Zufar. As for the Imamiyya, they differentiated between a non-virgin and a virgin... And those who require (the guardian) adduced the hadith of Aisha from the Messenger of God: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her guardian, her marriage is invalid, three times...'
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a juristic dispute (ikhtilaf) on the requirement of a wali (guardian) in marriage. The author presents the ruling positions of various schools. The majority (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, Zahiri, Zaydi) require a wali; within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Hanifa and Zufar allow a woman to marry herself, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require a wali but allow the judge to override an unjust refusal. The Imami Shi'a position is detailed with multiple opinions. The evidence for the requirement is the hadith of Aisha (ra) narrated by many authorities. The author lists the narrators to show the hadith's widespread transmission, implying its strength as evidence.

Key Terms
ولي (walī): Guardian for marriage, typically the father or closest male relative.
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has been married before (non-virgin).
كفوء (kufu'): Suitable match in terms of lineage, religion, and socio-economic status.

D) The two companions (al-Sahibayn)

Key Takeaway
The hadith on wali is ahad; the Hanafi school does not accept it as overriding the general Quranic permission, leading to a different ruling on marriage without guardian.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Aisha's Action and the Wali Requirement: Reconciling Text and Practice
Debate on whether Aisha's marriage of her niece contradicts her own narration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a textual dispute between a narrator's action and their narration, applying Hanafi usul principles of naskh and ta'wil, and evaluate the jumhur's counter-argument using additional evidence.

Core Idea
When a narrator's action contradicts their hadith, Hanafi usul considers naskh or ta'wil; jumhur resolve by clarifying the action's scope.

In this lesson, we examine a classic usul issue: Aisha (ra) narrated that a wali is required for a valid marriage, yet she herself married her niece without the girl's father present. Does her action contradict her narration? The Hanafi school argues yes, indicating abrogation or reinterpretation. The jumhur, however, provide a key clarification that resolves the apparent conflict. This passage teaches you how to handle such textual tensions using usul methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Identify the hadith of Aisha requiring a wali and her action of marrying her niece without a wali.
The contradiction is between her narrated statement and her action.
2
Understand the Hanafi (second school) argument
The Hanafis argue that Aisha's action shows she did not require a wali, suggesting naskh or ta'wil of her earlier narration.
They cite al-Tahawi and the narration via Malik of Aisha marrying her niece.
3
Examine the jumhur's refutation
The jumhur counter by providing a more detailed narration from Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Qasim that shows Aisha only arranged the marriage, not performed the contract.
This narration includes Aisha's explicit statement that a woman should not perform the contract.
4
Apply usul principles
Recognize that when a narrator's action is ambiguous, the stronger evidence is their explicit statement. The principle 'al-ibra bi al-nass la bi al-fi'l' guides reconciliation.
The jumhur use this to resolve the conflict in favor of the narrated hadith.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis permit a woman of sound mind to contract her own marriage without a wali; jumhur require a wali for validity.
This shows how usul analysis directly impacts fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ أجاب أصحاب المذهب الثاني عن هَذَا الْحَدِيث، بأنه قد عارضه فعلها، وأنها فعلت خلاف ما روت فَقَالَ الطحاوي: «ثُمَّ لَوْ ثبت ما رووا من ذَلِكَ عن الزهري، لكان قد روي عن عَائِشَة - رضي الله عَنْهَا – ما يخالف ذَلِكَ». ثُمَّ رَوَى من طريق مالك، أن عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن القاسم أخبره، عن أبيه، عن عَائِشَة زوج النبي الله أنها زوجت حفصة بنت عَبْد الرَّحْمن المنذر بن الزبير، وعبد الرَّحْمن غائب بالشام. فلما قدم عَبْد الرَّحْمَن قَالَ: أمثلي يصنع بِهِ هَذَا، ويفتات عَلَيْهِ؟ فَكُلِّمَتْ عَائِشَة عن المنذر، فَقَالَ المنذر: إن ذَلِكَ بيد عَبْد الرَّحْمن، فَقَالَ عَبْد الرَّحْمن: ما كنت أرد أمراً قضيته، فقرت حفصة عنده، وَلَمْ يَكُنْ ذَلِكَ طلاقاً. فلولا أنها كَانَتْ ترى عدم اشتراط الولي لصحة عقد النكاح، لما فعلته مع ابنة أخيها، وهذا يدل على وجود ناسخ أو تأويل لما روته من اشتراطه. ورد الجُمهُور هَذَا الاستدلال: بأنه لَيْسَ في خبر عَائِشَة هَذَا التصريح بأنها باشرت العقد بنفسها، فَقَدْ تَكُون مهدت لأسبابه، فإذا جاء العقد أحالته إلى الولي بدليل ما روي عن عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن القاسم، قَالَ: كنت عِنْدَ عَائِشَة يخطب إليها المرأة من أهلها فتشهد فإذا بقيت عقدة النكاح، قالت لبعض أهلها: زَوّج فإن المرأة لا تلي عقد النكاح. فإذا علمنا أن مذهبها هَذَا الَّذِي رَوَاهُ عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن القاسم عَنْهَا، اتضح أن مراد الرَّاوِي بقوله: زوجت حفصة، أي هيأت الأسباب، فانتفت المخالفة المظنونة، لما روت عن رَسُول الله.
And the proponents of the second school answered concerning this hadith, saying that her action contradicted it, and that she did contrary to what she narrated. So al-Tahawi said: 'Then if what they narrated from al-Zuhri is established, it would be that Aisha narrated something contradictory to that.' Then he narrated via Malik: Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Qasim informed him from his father that Aisha, wife of the Prophet, married Hafsa bint Abd al-Rahman to al-Mundhir ibn al-Zubayr while Abd al-Rahman was absent in Syria. When Abd al-Rahman arrived, he said: 'Is someone like me treated thus and decided upon without consultation?' Aisha was spoken to about al-Mundhir, and al-Mundhir said: 'That is in Abd al-Rahman's hands.' So Abd al-Rahman said: 'I would not refuse a matter you have concluded,' so Hafsa remained with him, and that was not divorce. If she [Aisha] had not held that a wali is not necessary for the validity of the marriage contract, she would not have done this with her brother's daughter. This indicates the existence of an abrogator or an interpretation of what she narrated about its requirement. And the majority refuted this deduction: that in Aisha's report there is no explicit statement that she herself performed the contract; perhaps she arranged its causes, and when the contract came, she referred it to the wali, as indicated by what was narrated from Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Qasim. He said: I was with Aisha when a woman from her family was being proposed to; she would testify, and when the knot of marriage remained, she would say to some of her family: 'Marry (her), for a woman does not perform the marriage contract.' So when we know that this is her opinion, which Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Qasim narrated from her, it becomes clear that the narrator's statement 'she married Hafsa' means she arranged the causes, so the presumed contradiction is removed concerning what she narrated from the Messenger of Allah.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a dispute centered on a hadith of Aisha regarding the requirement of a wali (guardian) for marriage. The second school (likely the Hanafis) uses Aisha's own action—marrying her niece while her brother was absent—as evidence that she did not consider a wali necessary. They claim this action contradicts her narration that a wali is required, thus indicating abrogation or reinterpretation of the hadith. The majority (jumhur) responds by arguing that Aisha did not actually perform the contract herself; she merely facilitated it. They provide additional evidence from Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Qasim that Aisha explicitly said a woman should not perform the marriage contract. Therefore, the apparent contradiction is resolved. The Hanafi position relies on usul principles such as the possibility of naskh (abrogation) or ta'wil (interpretation) when a narrator's action seems to conflict with their report.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): A guardian, specifically the male relative who has authority to give a woman in marriage.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a previous ruling by a later one.
تأويل (ta'wil): Interpretation that reconciles apparent contradiction by reinterpreting the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the jumhur, why does Aisha's marriage of Hafsa not contradict her narration requiring a wali?
A) Because the hadith was abrogated.
B) Because she did not perform the contract herself; she arranged it.
C) Because the wali later approved.
D) Because Hafsa was an adult.

Key Takeaway
When a narrator's action seems to contradict their hadith, careful scrutiny of the action's nature and additional evidence from the same narrator can often resolve the conflict without resorting to abrogation.

Parsing Ikhtilaf: The Purification of Vessel from Dog's Licking
Summary of differing opinions on washing a vessel after a dog drinks from it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differing rulings on washing a vessel after a dog drinks and classify the underlying usul principle (ta'abbud vs. rational cause) for each school.

Core Idea
Differing hadith interpretations lead to four distinct rulings on the same act.

This passage presents a comparative overview of how various schools derive rulings from the hadith on dog-licked vessels. Understanding each school's treatment of the dog's saliva, number of washes, and use of soil requires us to examine their underlying usul principle: whether the command is devotional (ta'abbud) or rationally explained. We will parse the key differences and trace them back to interpretive principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Schools and Their Positions
List each school mentioned (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, Zahiri, Zaydi) and extract their specific ruling on: (a) impurity of the dog's leftover water (su'r), (b) number of washes, (c) requirement of soil (tatrib), and (d) the rationale (ta'abbud vs. cleansing).
The Hanafi position is absent from this list but is also relevant.
2
Classify the Rationale
For each school, determine whether they view the seven washes as ta'abbud (purely devotional) or as a rational means to remove impurity. Note that both Shafi'is and Malikis consider it ta'abbud, but they differ on the impurity of su'r.
The Maliki view is unique: su'r is pure yet washing is still required as worship.
3
Apply Usul Principles
Consider how each school might derive its position from the same hadith (e.g., the command 'wash seven times'). Is the number seen as a fixed limit (ta'abbud) or as a guideline to achieve purification? The Hanafi school, for instance, argues the illah is removal of impurity, so three washes suffice.
This step connects the rulings to the usul concepts of 'ibadah ta'abbudiyyah vs. mu'allalah.
4
Synthesize the Ikhtilaf
Summarize the main points of disagreement: (1) impurity of su'r, (2) number of washes, (3) necessity of soil, and (4) underlying rationale. Understand that differing interpretations of the Prophet's words lead to these variations.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate and based on valid usul differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النموذج الثاني: طهارة الإناء من ولوغ الكلب
اختلف الفقهاء في عدد الغسلات التي يحصل بها التطهير من ولوغ الكلب عَلَى قولين: الأول: ذهب جمهور الفقهاء إلى أنَّ الإناء يغسل سبع مرات من ولوغ الكلب، واختلفوا في نجاسة سؤره واشتراط التتريب، وهل الأمر بالغسل للنجاسة أم هُوَ للتعبد ؟ على النحو الآتي: ١ ذهب الشافعية إلى أن سؤر الكلب نجس، ويغسل الإناء سبعاً أولاهـن بالتراب والأمر بالغسل سبعاً للتعبد. ٢. ذهب مالك إلى أن الأمر بإراقة سؤر الكلب وغسل الإناء مِنْهُ، عبادة غَيْر مدركة العلة، والماء الذي ولغ فِيْهِ لَيْسَ بنجس، وَلَمْ يرَ إراقة ما سوى الماء في أشهر الروايات عَنْهُ. قَالَ المازري: اختلف في غسل الإناء من ولوغ الكلب، هَلْ هُوَ تعبد أو سبع مرات: أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَتْ العلة لنجاسته ؟ فعندنا أنَّهُ تعبد، واحتج أصحابنا بتحديد غسله سبع مرات : أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَتْ العلة للنجاسة لكان المطلوب الإنقاء، وَقَدْ يحصل في مرة واحدة. ٣. ذهب الحنابلة إلى أن سؤر الكلب نجس، ويجب غسل الإناء مِنْهُ سبعاً، إحداهن بالتراب من غَيْر تحديد لمكانها من السبع. ٤ قَالَ الظاهرية: سؤر الكلب طاهر، وغسل الإناء مِنْهُ سبعاً إذا ولغ فيه فرض، وما في الإناء من طعام وشراب وماء فَهُوَ طاهر. قَالَ الزيدية: التسبيع في غسل الإناء وتتريبه واجب، من غير تعيين لغسل بعينه.
Second model: Purification of the vessel from the licking of a dog. The jurists differed on the number of washes by which purification from the licking of a dog is achieved, into two opinions: First: The majority of jurists went to that the vessel is washed seven times from the licking of the dog, and they differed on the impurity of its remaining water and the condition of using soil, and whether the command to wash is due to impurity or for worship, as follows: 1. The Shafi'is went to that the remaining water of the dog is impure, and the vessel is washed seven times, the first of them with soil, and the command to wash seven times is for worship. 2. Malik went to that the command to discard the remaining water of the dog and to wash the vessel from it is a worship whose cause is not rationally perceived, and the water into which it licked is not impure, and he did not require discarding anything other than water in the most famous narrations from him. Al-Mazari said: There is disagreement on washing the vessel from the licking of a dog, whether it is worship or due to impurity? According to us it is worship, and our companions argued that limiting its wash to seven times: if the cause were impurity, the required would be cleansing, and that might be achieved in one time. 3. The Hanbalis went to that the remaining water of the dog is impure, and it is obligatory to wash the vessel from it seven times, one of them with soil, without specifying which of the seven. 4. The Zahiris said: The remaining water of the dog is pure, and washing the vessel from it seven times if it licked in it is obligatory, and whatever food, drink, and water is in it, it is pure. The Zaydis said: Washing seven times and using soil in washing the vessel is obligatory, without specifying a particular wash.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a textual analysis of the differing juristic opinions regarding the purification of a vessel after a dog drinks from it. The core issue is whether the washing is for removing impurity or for ritual worship. The majority agree on seven washes, but they differ on the need for soil, the impurity of the dog's saliva, and the underlying rationale. The Shafi'is require seven washes with the first containing soil, and they consider the saliva impure and the washing as worship (ta'abbud). Malik views the entire matter as worship with no rational cause; the water remains pure. The Hanbalis also require seven washes with one containing soil, without specifying which. The Zahiris hold that the saliva is pure, yet washing the vessel seven times is still obligatory. The Zaydis similarly obligate seven washes with soil. Notably, the Hanafi position is absent from this listing; the Hanafi school typically holds that the vessel must be washed three times and that the dog's saliva is impure (though a lighter impurity). This absence suggests the text focuses on the positions of other schools for comparative analysis.

Key Terms
ولوغ (wulugh): The act of a dog drinking by lapping up water with its tongue.
سؤر (su'r): The leftover water or liquid after the dog has drunk.
تتريب (tatrib): The use of soil (earth) in the washing process.
تعبد (ta'abbud): A ruling based on divine command without a rationally perceived cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which school of thought holds that the dog's remaining water (su'r) is pure but still requires washing the vessel seven times?
A) Shafi'i
B) Maliki
C) Hanbali
D) Zahiri

Key Takeaway
The number of washes and use of soil required for a dog-licked vessel vary among schools, primarily based on whether the command is interpreted as ta'abbud (devotional) or rational (mu'allal).
