Commentary
This passage presents a concise summary of the juristic dispute regarding the 'musarrah' animal - an animal whose udder is left unmilked for days to appear full, deceiving the buyer. The text states there are two opinions: the Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and in one narration Abu Yusuf) that the buyer cannot return the animal based on this defect; and the majority view (including Shafi'is) that the buyer may return it due to the defect of tasriyah. The Hanafi position likely relies on the principle that the buyer has the option to return if the defect existed at sale, but here the defect is not permanent (the milk can be milked) and the seller may argue that the buyer had the right to examine. However, the text does not elaborate the Hanafi argument. It simply records the ikhtilaf. The footnotes cite various sources for both positions. This is an example of how differences in understanding hadith (like the hadith on musarrah) lead to divergent rulings.

Key Terms
الشاة المصراة (ash-shaat al-musarrah): A milk-gorged animal: an animal that has not been milked for some time to make its udder appear full, misleading the buyer.
التصرية (at-tasriyah): The act of leaving an animal unmilked to deceive the buyer; considered a defect by most schools.

Dispute over Khiyar al-Majlis: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Presentation of two opinions on the option of the session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two scholarly positions on khiyar al-majlis, explain their respective proofs from hadith interpretation, and evaluate the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
A hadith on khiyar al-majlis is interpreted literally by Shafi'i and contextually by Hanafi—resulting in opposite rulings on contract bindingness.

This passage introduces one of the most famous disputes in Islamic contract law: the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis). The disagreement arises from how different schools interpret the same Prophetic hadith. Understanding this dispute reveals how usul al-fiqh principles—like handling a general text versus a specific context—shape actual fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define the term
Read the definition of khiyar al-majlis: the right of both parties to confirm or rescind a sale from its formation until they physically separate or mutually choose to confirm.
Focus on the temporal scope: from offer and acceptance until separation.
2
Identify the two opinions
List the first opinion (Hanafi, Maliki, etc.): contract is binding immediately upon offer and acceptance unless a stipulation is made. List the second opinion (Shafi'i, etc.): option remains until the parties separate.
Note which scholars support each view.
3
Locate the proof source
The key proof is the hadith: 'The two parties have the option as long as they have not separated' (al-bayyi'an bi'l-khiyar ma lam yatafarraqa).
This hadith is the pivot of the dispute.
4
Analyze the interpretation
Explain how the Hanafis interpret this hadith as referring to khiyar al-shart (stipulated option), not an automatic option. The Shafi'is take it literally: the option exists until separation.
Consider the usul principle: amm (general) vs. ta'wil (interpretation) in light of other evidences.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi says the buyer can be compelled to pay immediately; Shafi'i says the buyer can rescind before leaving the session.
This difference affects everyday transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النموذج الأول: خيار المجلس
يمكن تعريف خيار المجلس بأنه حق العاقدين في إمضاء العقد أو رده، منذ التعاقد إلى التفرق أو التخاير ). فإذا أتم العاقدان عقد البيع من غَيْر أن يتفرقا وَلَمْ يختر أحدٌ مِنْهُمَا اللزوم، فهل العقد لازماً بمجرد هَذَا التمام، أم أن لكلا العاقدين الحق في فسخ العقد ما داما يعتبر في مجلس البيع؟ اختلف الفقهاء في ثبوت هَذَا الحق عَلَى قولين:
الأول: لا يثبت خيار المجلس، والعقد لازم بالإيجاب والقبول، إلا إذا تشارطا أو أحدهما إثبات الخيار.
وبهذا :قَالَ إبراهيم النخعي وأهل الكوفة، وربيعة الرأي وطائفة من أهل المدينة، وَهُوَ قَوْل الثوري في رِوَايَة عَبد الرزاق عَنْهُ.
وإليه ذهب الحنفية ، والمالكية، وأكثر الزيدية).
الثاني: خيار المجلس ثابت للمتعاقدين، ولكل مِنْهُمَا الحق في فسخه مادام المجلس قائماً، ومالم يختر أحدهما اللزوم.
روي هَذَا عن عمر، وعثمان، وابن عمر وابن عَبَّاسٍ، وأبي هُرَيْرَةَ، وأبي برزة الأسلمي () ، وبه قال سعيد بن المسيب، والحسن البصري، وشريح، والشعبي، وعطاء، وطاووس ،والزهري، والأوزاعي، وابن أبي ذئب في طائفة من أهل المدينة، والثوري في "جامعه "، والليث بن سعد، وعبيد الله بن الحسن، وداود الظاهري، وسوّار قاضي البصرة، وسفيان بن عيينة وعبد الله بن المبارك، وابن جريج، ومعمر، و ومسلم بن خالد
Model One: The Option of the Session. The option of the session can be defined as the right of the two contracting parties to confirm the contract or rescind it, from the time of contracting until they separate or mutually choose to confirm. So if the two parties complete the sale contract without separating and neither chooses bindingness, is the contract binding immediately upon this completion, or does each party have the right to rescind the contract as long as they are considered in the session of sale? The jurists differed on the establishment of this right into two opinions: First: The option of the session is not established, and the contract is binding by offer and acceptance, unless they stipulate or one of them stipulates the option. This was the view of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and the people of Kufa, Rabi'a al-Ra'y and a group from the people of Medina, and it is the view of al-Thawri in the narration of 'Abd al-Razzaq from him. The Hanafis, Malikis, and most Zaydis adopted this. Second: The option of the session is established for the two contracting parties, and each of them has the right to rescind it as long as the session is ongoing and as long as neither has chosen bindingness. This was narrated from 'Umar, 'Uthman, Ibn 'Umar, Ibn 'Abbas, Abu Hurayra, Abu Barza al-Aslami, and this was the view of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, al-Hasan al-Basri, Shurayh, al-Sha'bi, 'Ata, Tawus, al-Zuhri, al-Awza'i, Ibn Abi Dhi'b among a group from the people of Medina, al-Thawri in his Jami', al-Layth ibn Sa'd, 'Ubaydullah ibn al-Hasan, Dawud al-Zahiri, Sawwar judge of Basra, Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna, 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak, Ibn Jurayj, Ma'mar, and Muslim ibn Khalid...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage introduces the first case study in the chapter: the dispute over khiyar al-majlis (option of the session). It defines khiyar al-majlis as the right of both parties in a sale to either confirm or rescind the contract from the time of contracting until they physically separate or mutually agree to confirm. The central question is whether the contract becomes immediately binding upon offer and acceptance or remains revocable as long as the parties are still in the session. The author lists two opinions: The first opinion, held by the Hanafis, Malikis, and several early scholars, is that the contract is binding immediately and no such option exists unless stipulated. The second opinion, attributed to many Companions and Successors, including 'Umar and Ibn 'Umar, and later scholars like al-Shafi'i (implicitly), is that the option is established. This dispute stems from the different interpretations of the hadith 'al-bayyi'an bi'l-khiyar ma lam yatafarraqa' (The two parties have the option as long as they have not separated). The Hanafis interpret this as referring to the option of stipulation, not automatic option, and they also consider that the contract becomes binding when mutual consent is expressed.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The right of the contracting parties to confirm or rescind a contract from its formation until they physically separate or mutually choose to confirm.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated by one or both parties in the contract, allowing rescission within a specified period.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarah al-khilaf): The practical consequence or difference in application resulting from a scholarly disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a sale contract become binding?
A) After the parties separate.
B) Immediately upon offer and acceptance, unless a stipulation is made.
C) Only after mutual choice (takhayur).
D) After the option of the session expires.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over khiyar al-majlis illustrates how a single hadith can yield opposite rulings based on usul al-fiqh principles of interpretation (literal vs. contextual).

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Khiyar al-Majlis: Two Positions and Their Usul Implications
Dispute over khiyar al-majlis with positions and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on khiyar al-majlis, state the hadith evidence used by the majority, and analyze the Maliki objection based on the principle that a khabar ahad cannot override established practice (amal).

Core Idea
A single hadith divides schools: Hanafis bind the contract immediately; Shafi'is keep it revocable until separation.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute: is a sales contract immediately binding upon offer and acceptance, or do both parties retain an implied option to rescind as long as they remain in the session? The answer hinges on how we interpret a well-known hadith and weigh it against other sources of law. You will learn the two major positions, their evidence, and a key methodological objection raised by the Malikis.

Guided Path
1
Define the Issue and Identify the Two Positions
State the central legal question: when a sale is concluded without stipulation and before separation, is the contract binding or revocable? Then identify the two positions: (1) Hanafi/Maliki: binding immediately; (2) Shafi'i/Hanbali: each party retains an option during the session.
The Hanafi position is that 'the contract is binding upon offer and acceptance, unless an option is stipulated.'
2
Locate and Analyze the Hadith Evidence
Read the hadith quoted in the passage: 'The two parties in a sale have the option as long as they have not separated, except in the sale of option.' Identify its textual category and wajh al-dalalah (how it proves the majority's position).
The hadith is explicit: it directly grants an option until separation.
3
Understand the Maliki Objection
The Malikis argue the hadith is a khabar ahad that contradicts the established practice (amal) of Medina. In usul, they claim a solitary report cannot override a widespread practice that has the status of tawatur.
This objection raises the methodological issue of prioritising 'amal over khabar ahad.
4
Consider the Hanafi Response (Author's Refutation)
The author begins a refutation of the Maliki view, stating there are three aspects to reject it. Though the text cuts off, this shows the Hanafi engagement with the objection.
The Hanafi school does not rely on Medinan practice; it relies on the general principle of contract binding upon consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في المتن
والأكثرون على تسميته خيار المجلس ومنهم من يسميه «خيار الْمُتَبَايِعَيْنِ». فإذا أتم العاقدان عقد البيع من غَيْر أن يتفرقا وَلَمْ يختر أحدٌ مِنْهُمَا اللزوم، فهل العقد لازماً بمجرد هَذَا التمام، أم أن لكلا العاقدين الحق في فسخ العقد ما داما يعتبر في مجلس البيع؟ اختلف الفقهاء في ثبوت هَذَا الحق عَلَى قولين:
الأول: لا يثبت خيار المجلس، والعقد لازم بالإيجاب والقبول، إلا إذا تشارطا أو أحدهما إثبات الخيار.
وبهذا قَالَ إبراهيم النخعي وأهل الكوفة، وربيعة الرأي وطائفة من أهل المدينة، وَهُوَ قَوْل الثوري في رِوَايَة عَبد الرزاق عَنْهُ. وإليه ذهب الحنفية، والمالكية، وأكثر الزيدية.
الثاني: خيار المجلس ثابت للمتعاقدين، ولكل مِنْهُمَا الحق في فسخه مادام المجلس قائماً، ومالم يختر أحدهما اللزوم.
روي هَذَا عن عمر، وعثمان، وابن عمر وابن عَبَّاسٍ، وأبي هُرَيْرَةَ، وأبي برزة الأسلمي، وبه قال سعيد بن المسيب، والحسن البصري، وشريح، والشعبي، وعطاء، وطاووس، والزهري، والأوزاعي، وابن أبي ذئب في طائفة من أهل المدينة، والثوري في "جامعه"، والليث بن سعد، وعبيد الله بن الحسن، وداود الظاهري، وسوّار قاضي البصرة، وسفيان بن عيينة وعبد الله بن المبارك، وابن جريج، ومعمر، ومسلم بن خالد الزنجي، والدراوردي، ويحيى القطان، وعبد الرَّحْمن بن مهدي، وإسحاق، وأبو عبيد، وأبو ثور.
وإليه ذهب الشافعية، والحنابلة، والظاهرية، والإمامية، وبعض الزيدية.
واستدل الجُمْهُور بأدلة متظافرة كثيرة منها:
ما صَحَّ عن رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أَنَّهُ قَالَ: «الْمُتَبَايِعَانِ كُلِّ واحد مِنْهُمَا بالخيار عَلَى صاحبه، ما لم يتفرقا، إلا بيع الخيار».
وجه الدلالة: أن الْحَدِيث مصرح بأن العقد بَيْنَ الْمُتَبَايِعَيْنِ غَيْر لازم ما لم يحصل التفرق عن مجلس العقد، أو يختار واحد مِنْهُمَا اللزوم.
وأجاب المالكية عن هَذَا الْحَدِيث: بأنه مخالف لعمل أهل المدينة، لذا قَالَ الإمام مالك عقب روايته لهذا الْحَدِيْثِ: «وَلَيْسَ لهذا عندنا حد معروف، ولا أمر معمول فيه».
وَهُوَ خبر آحاد فلا يقوى عَلَى مخالفة عملهم ونستطيع أن نرد قَوْل المالكية هَذَا، من ثلاثة وجوه هي: ...
Chapter Two / Discrepancy in the Text
The majority call it khiyar al-majlis, and some call it 'the option of the two parties'. So when the two contracting parties complete the sale contract without separating and neither chooses bindingness, is the contract binding merely by this completion, or does each of the two parties have the right to rescind the contract as long as they are considered in the sales session? The jurists differed on the establishment of this right into two opinions:
First: Khiyar al-majlis is not established, and the contract is binding upon offer and acceptance, unless they stipulate or one of them stipulates the option.
This was said by Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and the people of Kufa, Rabi'a al-Ra'y and a group from the people of Medina, and it is the statement of al-Thawri in the narration of 'Abd al-Razzaq from him. The Hanafis, Malikis, and most Zaidis adopted this.
Second: Khiyar al-majlis is established for the two contractors, and each has the right to rescind as long as the session continues and neither chooses bindingness.
This was narrated from 'Umar, 'Uthman, Ibn 'Umar, Ibn 'Abbas, Abu Huraira, Abu Barza al-Aslami, and it was said by Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, al-Hasan al-Basri, Shurayh, al-Sha'bi, 'Ata', Tawus, al-Zuhri, al-Awza'i, Ibn Abi Dhi'b among a group from Medina, and al-Thawri in his 'Jami'', al-Layth ibn Sa'd, 'Ubayd Allah ibn al-Hasan, Dawud al-Zahiri, Sawwar the judge of Basra, Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna, 'Abd Allah ibn al-Mubarak, Ibn Jurayj, Ma'mar, Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji, al-Darawardi, Yahya al-Qattan, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi, Ishaq, Abu 'Ubayd, and Abu Thawr.
And the Shafi'is, Hanbalis, Zahiris, Imamis, and some Zaidis adopted this.
The majority adduced numerous supporting proofs, among them:
What is authentically reported from the Messenger of Allah (may Allah bless him and give him peace) that he said: 'The two parties in a sale, each has the option against the other as long as they have not separated, except in the sale of option.'
The indication of this hadith: that the hadith explicitly states that the contract between the two parties is not binding until separation from the session occurs, or one of them chooses bindingness.
The Malikis responded to this hadith: that it contradicts the practice of the people of Medina, so Imam Malik said after narrating this hadith: 'This has with us no known limit, nor is it a practice acted upon.'
And it is a solitary report (khabar ahad), so it cannot override their practice. And we can refute this statement of the Malikis from three aspects: ...
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) in fiqh concerning khiyar al-majlis, the option to rescind a sale while the contracting parties remain in the session. The author first defines the issue: when a sale is concluded without separation, is it immediately binding or revocable? Two opinions are presented. The first opinion, adopted by the Hanafis (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), Malikis, and most Zaidis, holds that once offer and acceptance are exchanged, the contract is binding unless the parties stipulate an option. This view relies on the general principle that contracts are binding upon mutual consent, and the hadith on khiyar al-majlis is interpreted as referring to pre-sale negotiation or is abrogated. The second opinion, held by the Shafi'is, Hanbalis, Zahiris, Imamis, and some Zaidis, affirms khiyar al-majlis based on the explicit hadith: 'The two parties in a sale have the option as long as they have not separated, except in the sale of option.' The hadith is considered clear (zahir) in indicating the contract is not binding until separation. The Malikis, despite being in the first group, offer a different response: they argue the hadith contradicts the established practice ('amal) of Medina, and since it is a solitary report (khabar ahad), it cannot override the prevalent practice. The author then begins a refutation of the Maliki argument, stating there are three aspects to reject it, but the text cuts off before listing them. This passage illustrates how different schools prioritize sources: the majority prioritize the explicit hadith, while the Maliki and Hanafi schools prioritize either Medinan practice (Maliki) or general contractual principles (Hanafi).

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The right of each contracting party in a sale to rescind the contract as long as they remain physically together in the session of the contract.
المتبايعان (al-mutabayi'an): The two parties in a sale transaction, i.e., the buyer and seller.
التفرق (al-tafarruq): Physical separation of the two parties from the place of contract, or by mutual departure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion (Hanafi/Maliki) presented in the passage, when is a sales contract considered binding?
A) Only after the parties physically separate
B) Immediately upon offer and acceptance, unless they stipulate an option
C) After a cooling-off period of three days
D) Only if both parties declare the sale final

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-majlis is not established in the Hanafi school because the contract is binding immediately upon offer and acceptance, unless an option is stipulated.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Refuting the Maliki Objection to Khiyar al-Majlis
Refuting the Maliki condition for accepting khabar ahad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Maliki objection to khiyar al-majlis and apply the Hanafi response using the three refutations from the source.

Core Idea
The Hanafi position defends khiyar al-majlis by refuting the Maliki reliance on 'amal ahl al-Madina over a widely-narrated hadith.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: can a widely-narrated hadith be overridden by the practice of Medina? The author systematically refutes the Maliki argument that khabar ahad cannot oppose 'amal ahl al-Madina, using three powerful counter-arguments. This lesson will walk you through the dispute and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
Recognize that the issue is khiyar al-majlis: the option to rescind a sale contract as long as the parties have not physically separated.
The majority (including Hanafis) accept it; Malikis reject it.
2
Understand the Maliki objection
The Malikis argue that the hadith supporting khiyar al-majlis is khabar ahad and contradicts the established practice of the people of Medina ('amal ahl al-Madina), so it is rejected.
This objection is based on a unique Maliki usul principle.
3
Analyse the first refutation: uniqueness of condition
The author points out that the Maliki condition (khabar ahad must not oppose 'amal ahl al-Madina) is not accepted by other schools, so it only binds Malikis, not the majority.
This refutation highlights that the objection is not a universal criterion.
4
Analyse the second refutation: no genuine consensus
Even if the condition were accepted, the claim of ijma' of Medina against the hadith is false, because many Medinan scholars (e.g., Ibn Umar, Abu Hurayra) affirmed khiyar al-majlis.
The author lists Medinan narrators to undermine the claim of consensus.
5
Analyse the third refutation: hadith is mutawatir
The hadith was narrated by many Companions (the author lists seven), so it is not khabar ahad but reaches the level of tawatur, which overrides 'amal.
The third argument is cut off but the principle is clear: a mutawatir hadith cannot be opposed by 'amal.

الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في المتن
الزنجي، والدراوردي، ويحيى القطان، وعبد الرحمن بن مهدي، وإسحاق، وأبو عبيد، وأبو ثور.
وإليه ذهب الشافعية، والحنابلة، والظاهرية، والإمامية، وبعض الزيدية.
واستدل الجمهور بأدلة متظافرة كثيرة منها:
ما صح عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «المتبايعان كل واحد منهما بالخيار على صاحبه، ما لم يتفرقا، إلا بيع الخيار».
وجه الدلالة من هذا الحديث: أن الحديث مصرح بأن العقد بين المتبايعين غير لازم ما لم يحصل التفرق عن مجلس العقد، أو يختار واحد منهما اللزوم.
وأجاب المالكية عن هذا الحديث: بأنه مخالف لعمل أهل المدينة، لذا قال الإمام مالك عقب روايته لهذا الحديث: «وليس لهذا عندنا حد معروف، ولا أمر معمول فيه».
وهو خبر آحاد فلا يقوى على مخالفة عملهم.
ونستطيع أن نرد قول المالكية هذا، من ثلاثة وجوه هي:
1 - أن اشتراط المالكية للعمل بخبر الآحاد أن لا يكون مخالفاً لعمل أهل المدينة، شرط تفردوا به، فيكون لازماً لهم ولا يلزم غيرهم.
2 - على فرض التسليم - جدلاً - بكون هذا الذي اشترطوه شرطاً للعمل بخبر الآحاد، فما اشترطوه غير متحقق في هذه المسألة، فإنهم نصوا على أن إجماع أهل المدينة إذا عارضه خبر آحاد، قدم الإجماع.
ودعوى إجماع أهل المدينة هنا منقوضة فقد سبق أن نقلنا القول بثبوت خيار المجلس عن عمر وعثمان وابن عمر وأبي هريرة وسعيد بن المسيب والزهري وابن أبي ذئب والدراوردي، وهؤلاء جميعاً من أهل المدينة، فكيف تصح دعوى إجماعهم؟ حتى إن ابن أبي ذئب لما قيل له أن مالكاً لا يعمل بهذا الحديث قال: هذا خبر موطأ في المدينة، يريد أنه منتشر.
3 - وإذا أمعنا في التنزل معهم، والتسليم بأن هذا الشرط الذي اشترطوه صحيح، وأن إجماع أهل المدينة متحقق فإنه يخدش استدلالهم عدم كون الحديث آحادياً، وكيف يكون خبر آحاد وقد رواه من الصحابة عدد غفير، وقفنا على رواية سبعة منهم، هم: أ. سمرة بن جندب وحديثه أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، وأحمد، وابن ماجه، والنسائي، والطحاوي، والبيهقي. ب. عبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص وحديثه عند أحمد، وأبي داود، والترمذي، والنسائي، والدارقطني، والبيهقي، وابن عبد البر
Chapter Two / Disagreement in the Text (Matn). Al-Zanji, al-Darawardi, Yahya al-Qattan, Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi, Ishaq, Abu Ubayd, and Abu Thawr. The Shafi'is, Hanbalis, Zahiris, Imamis, and some Zaydis adopted this view. The majority (jumhur) argued with many supporting proofs, including: what is authentic from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, that he said: 'The two contracting parties each have the option (khiyar) against the other as long as they have not separated, except in the sale of khiyar.' The indication from this hadith: the hadith explicitly states that the contract between the two is not binding until separation from the session occurs, or one of them chooses bindingness. The Malikis responded to this hadith: that it contradicts the practice of the people of Medina, so Imam Malik said after narrating this hadith: 'There is no known limit for this with us, nor any practice acted upon.' It is a solitary hadith (khabar ahad) and thus cannot oppose their practice. We can refute this statement of the Malikis from three aspects: 1 - The Malikis' condition for acting upon a solitary hadith that it not contradict the practice of the people of Medina is a condition they uniquely held, so it is binding on them and not on others. 2 - Even if we hypothetically accept that this condition of theirs is valid, it is not realized in this issue, because they stated that if the consensus (ijma') of Medina contradicts a solitary hadith, the consensus is given precedence. And the claim of consensus of Medina here is refuted, as we have already transmitted the statement affirming the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis) from Umar, Uthman, Ibn Umar, Abu Hurayra, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, al-Zuhri, Ibn Abi Dhi'b, and al-Darawardi, and all of these are from the people of Medina. So how can their claim of consensus be valid? Even Ibn Abi Dhi'b, when it was said to him that Malik does not act upon this hadith, said: 'This is a well-known (mutawa) hadith in Medina,' meaning it is widespread. 3 - If we further concede to them and accept that their condition is correct and that the consensus of Medina is established, then their argument is still undermined because the hadith is not solitary; how can it be a solitary hadith when a large number of Companions narrated it? We have recorded the narration of seven of them: a. Samura ibn Jundub; his hadith is recorded by Ibn Abi Shayba, Ahmad, Ibn Majah, al-Nasa'i, al-Tahawi, and al-Bayhaqi. b. Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As; his hadith is with Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and Ibn Abd al-Barr.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute regarding the validity of the hadith on khiyar al-majlis (option of the session) in sales. The author first lists scholars and schools that affirm khiyar al-majlis, then provides the proof: the well-known hadith 'The two contracting parties have the option as long as they have not separated.' The Malikis object that this hadith contradicts the 'amal (practice) of the people of Medina, and since it is khabar ahad, it cannot override their established practice. The author then refutes the Maliki position with three arguments: (1) The Maliki condition that khabar ahad must not oppose 'amal ahl al-Madina is a unique condition that binds only them, not others. (2) Even if accepted, the claim of consensus (ijma') of Medina against this hadith is false, as many Medinan scholars supported khiyar al-majlis. (3) The hadith is not actually khabar ahad, as it was narrated by many Companions. The third argument is incomplete but lists two of the seven narrators. This dispute illustrates a key usul principle: the weight of khabar ahad versus local practice and consensus.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option for both parties in a sale to annul the contract as long as they have not physically separated.
خبر آحاد (khabar ahad): A hadith that does not reach the level of tawatur, narrated by one or a few chains.
عمل أهل المدينة ('amal ahl al-Madina): The established practice of the people of Medina, considered a source of law by Malikis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The Maliki condition that khabar ahad must not oppose 'amal ahl al-Madina is a condition unique to them, not binding on others.
C) The hadith is weak and cannot be relied upon.
D) The Malikis did not have a complete understanding of the hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on khiyar al-majlis is supported by a mutawatir hadith, and the Maliki objection based on 'amal ahl al-Madina is refuted by showing it is a school-specific condition not accepted by the majority.

Usul Dispute: Forgetful Eating in Fasting
A usul dispute between Hanafi and Maliki schools on the authority of a solitary hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Maliki positions on forgetful eating in fasting by analyzing the role of khabar wahid and general principles in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A solitary hadith may be overridden if it contradicts a general legal principle – but which takes precedence is a core usul dispute.

This passage presents a classic usul disagreement: the Hanafi school relies on an explicit hadith to rule that forgetful eating does not break the fast, while the Maliki school sets aside that hadith because it contradicts a broader principle. Understanding how each school weighs textual evidence against general principles is essential for usul analysis. Let's walk through the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions and their schools
From the text, list which schools hold that the fast remains valid (no qada) and which hold that it is invalidated (qada required).
The first position includes Hanafis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, etc.; the second includes Malikis and a few others.
2
Examine the primary proof: the hadith of Abu Hurayra
Read the hadith carefully. Note its wording: 'If one forgets and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' Identify the key phrase and how it is used as evidence.
The hadith is narrated by many compilers – it is authentic.
3
Analyze the Maliki objection
The Maliki school argues that the hadith is khabar wahid (solitary report) and contradicts a general principle: forgetfulness does not excuse in acts of worship. Understand how this objection is built.
This is a methodological argument, not a criticism of authenticity.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical legal difference between the two positions? Consider a real case where a person forgetfully eats on a Ramadhan day.
Hanafi/Majority: no qada; Maliki: qada required.
5
Reflect on the usul methodology
Compare the two approaches: giving precedence to an explicit text vs. adhering to a general principle. What does this teach about how different usul traditions operate?
This dispute illustrates the tension between nass (text) and qa'idah (principle).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب الحنفية )، والشافعية (۳)، والحنابلة (۳) ، والظاهرية)، والزيدية)، والإمامية). الثاني يفسد صوم من أكل أو شرب ناسياً، وَعَلَيْهِ القضاء، وبه قال ربيعة الرأي ()، والمالكية) ، والقاسمية من الزيدية ) .
الأدلة:
استدل القائلون بالمذهب الأول بأدلة عديدة، مِنْهَا:
ما رَوَاهُ أبو هُرَيْرَةَ - - أن رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «إذا نسي فأكل وشرب فليتم ن صومه، فإنما أطعمه الله وسقاه». رَوَاهُ عَبد الرزاق (۱)، وأحمد (۱۱)، والدارمي (۱۳)، والبخاري(۱۳)، ومسلم(١٤)، وأبو داود(۱۹)، وابن ماجه (۱) ، والترمذي (۱۷)، والنسائي(١٨)، وابن الجارود(۱۹)، وأبو يعلى (۱) ، وابن خزيمة (۳) ، وابن حبان (۳)، والطبراني (4) ، والدارقطني)، والبيهقي)، والبغوي). وجه الدلالة أن النص ظاهر في أن الأكل والشرب بالنسبة للصائم ناسياً لا يؤثر في الصوم، والنص مطلق من حَيْثُ عدم تقييد الصيام بكونه فرضاً أو نفلاً. قَالَ ابن دَقِيْقِ العِيْدِ: «عمدة من لَمْ يوجب القضاء هَذَا الْحَدِيث وما في معناه أو ما يقاربه، فإنه أمر بالإتمام وسمى الذي يتم صوماً، وظاهره حمله على الحقيقية الشرعية، وإذا كَانَ صوماً وقع مجزئاً، ويلزم من ذَلِكَ عدم وجوب القضاء» (۸). ثُمَّ قَالَ: «وإذا دار اللفظ بَيْنَ حمله عَلَى المعنى اللغوي والشرعي، كَانَ حمله عَلَى الشرعي أولى». وأجاب من قَالَ بالمذهب الثاني عن هَذَا الاستدلال بما يأتي: ١. قالوا: هَذَا الْحَدِيث خبر آحاد ، وَقَدْ عارض القاعدة العامة التي تقول: النسيان لا يؤثر في باب المأمورات» (١) . أي لا يؤثر من ناحية براءة ذمة المكلف منه. قَالَ ابن العربي (١١): «أصل مالك في أن خبر الواحد إذا جاء بخلاف القواعد لَمْ يعمل به» (١٢) فما يفسد الصوم بعدمه على وجه العمد، فإنه يفسده على وجه النسيان، كَمَا في الْمَنْثُور فِي الْقَوَاعِدِ لِلزَّرْكَشِيِّ.
The Hanafis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, Zahiris, Zaydis, and Imamis (first opinion) say that the fast of one who eats or drinks forgetfully is not invalidated, and there is no qada upon him. The second opinion says that it invalidates the fast of one who eats or drinks forgetfully, and qada is required upon him; this is the view of Rabi'a al-Ra'y, the Malikis, and the Qasimiyya among the Zaydis.
Evidence:
The proponents of the first opinion adduced numerous proofs, among them:
What Abu Hurayra narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: "If one forgets and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink." This was narrated by Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, al-Darimi, al-Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn al-Jarud, Abu Ya'la, Ibn Khuzayma, Ibn Hibban, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Baghawi.
The aspect of indication is that the text is apparent that eating and drinking for a fasting person who forgets does not affect the fast, and the text is absolute with respect to not restricting the fast to being obligatory or supererogatory. Ibn Daqiq al-'Id said: "The main proof of those who do not require qada is this hadith and what is similar to it, for it commands completion and calls it a fast, and its apparent meaning is to carry it on the legal reality, and if it is a fast, it is sufficient, and from that follows the non-obligation of qada." Then he said: "When a word is ambiguous between carrying it on the linguistic meaning or the legal meaning, carrying it on the legal meaning is preferable."
And those who held the second opinion responded to this proof as follows: 1. They said: This hadith is a khabar wahid, and it contradicts the general principle that forgetfulness does not affect in the realm of commanded acts, i.e., it does not affect the absolution of the responsible person's liability. Ibn al-'Arabi said: "Malik's principle is that when a solitary report comes contrary to the principles, it is not acted upon." So whatever invalidates the fast by its absence intentionally also invalidates it by forgetfulness, as in al-Manthur fi al-Qawa'id by al-Zarkashi.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents a classic fiqh dispute regarding the effect of forgetful eating/drinking on the validity of fasting. The author first lists the two main camps: the first, comprising the majority including Hanafis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, and others, holds that the fast remains valid and no make-up (qada) is required. The second, led by Malik and his school, maintains that the fast is broken and qada is necessary. The key proof for the first group is the well-known hadith of Abu Hurayra in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructs the forgetful person to complete his fast, implying that the fast is not invalidated. Ibn Daqiq al-'Id's commentary emphasizes that the hadith's apparent meaning should be understood in its legal sense, thus the fast is valid. The second group counters by arguing that the hadith is a solitary report (khabar wahid) and conflicts with a general legal principle: forgetfulness does not excuse omissions in acts of worship. According to Malik's methodology, when a solitary report contradicts established principles, it is set aside. They analogize that if intentional eating breaks the fast, forgetful eating does so as well. The passage thus illustrates how different usul methodologies (a'amal with khabar wahid vs. reliance on general principles) lead to divergent fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
خبر آحاد (khabar ahad): A solitary report that does not reach mutawatir status; its transmission is presumptive (zanni).
قاعدة عامة (qa'idah 'ammah): A general legal principle, here: forgetfulness does not affect the obligation of commanded acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person eats forgetfully while fasting?
A) The fast is broken and qada is required.
B) The fast remains valid and no qada is required.
C) The fast is invalidated but no qada is needed.
D) The fast is suspended until the end of the day.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives priority to an explicit hadith over general principles, while the Maliki school does the opposite – a key difference in usul methodology.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
Forgetful Eating in Fasting: Hanafi vs Maliki
Analysing hadith on forgetful break of fast and resultant ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a clear text (nass) overrides qiyas, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument based on qiyas.

Core Idea
A hadith specific to fasting overrides qiyas on prayer because nass takes precedence over analogy.

This lesson examines the classic dispute between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding a fasting person who forgetfully eats or drinks. The Hanafi school relies on a sound hadith that exempts such a person from making up the fast, while the Maliki school uses qiyas (analogy) with prayer, arguing that forgetful breaking of a pillar invalidates the act. The passage illustrates a fundamental usul al-fiqh principle: when a clear text (nass) exists, qiyas cannot override it. You will learn how to identify the operative text, categorize its authority, and understand why the Hanafi school prioritizes the hadith over the analogy.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its primary evidence
From the passage, identify the Hanafi ruling: no qada for forgetful break of fast. Note the hadith cited: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no qada upon him and no expiation.' Recognize that this hadith is a khabar wahid but authentic.
The hadith is specific to Ramadan fasting.
2
State the Maliki position and its qiyas argument
The Maliki school holds that qada is required. Their proof is qiyas with prayer: just as forgetful hadath invalidates wudu and thus prayer, forgetful eating invalidates fasting because both are pillars that must be maintained.
Ibn Daqiq al-Id and al-Qadi Ibn al-Arabi support this position.
3
Identify the usul principle at stake
The core principle is the priority of nass (explicit text) over qiyas. In Hanafi usul, a text that directly addresses a case is independent and cannot be overridden by analogy, even if the analogy seems strong.
Al-Barmawi in Sharh al-Umda states: 'The condition of analogy is non-contradiction of the text.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi refutation of the qiyas
The passage provides three refutations: (1) Qiyas cannot stand against nass. (2) The hadith is specified for Ramadan in some chains. (3) The end of the hadith ('Allah fed him') indicates the action is not attributed to the forgetful person, so the fast remains valid.
Ibn Hajar explains that the hadith is an independent principle for fasting; to reject it by qiyas on prayer would undermine many hadiths.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference is: Hanafi – no qada required, fast valid; Maliki – qada required, fast invalid. In fatwa, the Hanafi mufta bihi position is the former.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في الْمَتْن
النية)، والصيام ركنه الإمساك، فإذا فات الركن في العبادة وجب الإتيان بِهِ، وَقَدْ تعذر هنا، فاقتضى الحكم بفساد صومه. قَالَ ابن دَقِيقِ العِيْدِ: ذهب مالك إلى إيجاب القضاء وَهُوَ القياس، فإن الصوم قَدْ فات ركنه وَهُوَ من باب المأمورات والقاعدة تقتضي أن النسيان لايؤثر في باب المأمورات» () ، وأفاض الْقَاضِي ابن العربي في تأييد مذهب مالك، فَقَالَ: «هَذَا الْحَدِيْثِ صَحِيح مليح ينظر إلى مطلقه دُونَ تثبت جَمِيع فقهاء الأمصار، وقالوا: من أفطر ناسياً
لا قضاء عَلَيْهِ، تعلقاً بقول النبي الهلال في الصَّحِيح: «إن الله أطعمك وسقاك». وتطلع مالك إلى المسألة من طريقها، فأشرف عَلَيْهَا فرأى في مطلعها: أن عَلَيْهِ القضاء؛ لأن الصوم عبارة عن الإمساك عن الأكل، فلا يوجد مَعَ الأكل لأنه ضده، وإذا لَمْ يبق ركنه وحقيقته لَمْ يوجد، وَلَمْ يَكُنْ ممتثلاً ولا قاضياً ما عَلَيْهِ، ألا ترى أن مناقض شرط الصَّلاة وَهُوَ الوضوء: الحدث، إذا وجد سهواً أو عمداً أبطل الصَّلاة؛ لأن الأضداد لا جماع مَعَ أضدادها شرعاً ولاحساً، وَلَيْسَ لهذا الأصل معارض إلا الكلام في الصَّلاة» (۳).

(۲) إحكام الأحكام ۲/ ۲۱۱ - ۲۱۲. (۳) عارضة الأحوذي ١٩٦/٣. (٤) عمدة القاري ١١/ ١٨. (٥) فتح الباري ٤ / ١٥٦ - ١٥٧ . (٦) عارضة الأحوذي ٣/ ١٩٧. (۷) إرشاد الساري ٣٧٢/٣.

الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في الْمَتْن
١٩٥
وكون الْحَدِيث خبر واحد مخالف للقاعدة أمر فِيهِ ،نظر، وعلل هَذَا الحافظ ابن
-
حجر فَقَالَ: «لأَنَّهُ – يعني: الحَدِيث المذكور - قاعدة مستقلة بالصيام فمن عارضه بالقياس عَلَى الصَّلاة أدخل قاعدة في قاعدة وَلَوْ فتح باب رد الأحاديث الصَّحِيحَة بمثل هَذَا لما بقي من الْحَدِيث إلا القليل).
خزيمة (٢)،
(1)
وأما ثانياً: فقد ورد التصريح بتعيين رَمَضَان في بَعْض طرق الْحَدِيث، فأخرج ابن ، ومن طريقه ابن حبان (۳)، وأخرجه الطبراني () والدارقطني () ، والحاكم ) ، ومن طريقه البيهقي ) ، كلهم من طريق مُحَمَّد بن )، ، عن أبي سلمة بن عَبْد الرَّحْمن، عن أبي
(1)
،(۷)
عبد الله الأنصاري، عن مُحَمَّد بن عَمْرو هُرَيْرَةَ، مرفوعاً: «من أفطر في شهر رَمَضَان ناسياً ، فَلاً قضاء عَلَيْه ولا كفارة».
وأما ثالثاً: فإن قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في نهاية الحَدِيث : «فإنما أطعمه الله وسقاه»، دليل عَلَى صحة صومه، فَهُوَ مشعر بأن الفعل الصادر مِنْهُ غَيْر مضاف إليه، والحكم بكونه مفطراً
(۱۱)
يحتاج إِلَى إضافته إلَيْهِ (۱). لذا قَالَ الخطابي (١١): (معناه أن النسيان ضرورة، والأفعال الضرورية غير مضافة في الحكم إلى فاعلها وغير مؤاخذ بها، والقياس مطرد إلا أن يكثر النسيان، فإنه إذا تتابع أخرج العبادة عن حد القربة، وردها إلى حد العدم (۱۲).
Chapter Two: Disagreement in the Text. The intention, and the pillar of fasting is abstention. If the pillar is missed in worship, it must be performed; but it is impossible here, so the ruling necessitates that his fast is invalid. Ibn Daqiq al-Id said: Malik went to requiring qada, and it is the analogy, for the pillar of fasting has been missed and it is among commanded matters, and the principle dictates that forgetfulness does not affect commanded matters. And al-Qadi Ibn al-Arabi elaborated in supporting Malik's school, saying: This hadith is sound and fine, it is considered generally without verification. All jurists of the regions said: Whoever breaks his fast forgetfully has no qada upon him, clinging to the Prophet's statement in Sahih: 'Indeed Allah fed you and gave you drink.' Malik looked at the issue from its avenue, so he considered it and saw at its outset that he must make qada, because fasting is abstention from eating, so it does not exist with eating because it is its opposite. If its pillar and reality remain, it does not exist, and he is neither fulfilling nor making up what is upon him. Do you not see that the opponent of the condition of prayer – that is, wudu (purification) – is hadath (ritual impurity), if it occurs forgetfully or intentionally it invalidates prayer? Because opposites do not combine with their opposites legally or sensually, and this principle has no opponent except speech in prayer. 2. Carrying the hadith to supererogatory fasting, not obligatory, with the argument that the hadith does not specify Ramadan, so it is to be carried as supererogatory... carrying the hadith to the case of the fasting person whose condition is this by completing that day and removal of sin, but he must make it up. 4. Ibn al-Arabi said: This hadith agrees with the principle in removing sin, so it is accepted in that; it does not agree in the survival of worship after the loss of its pillar entirely, so it is not acted upon. And they were answered: Firstly: The mentioned analogy is not sound because it contradicts the text, and there is no ijtihad in the presence of the text. Al-Barmawi mentioned in Sharh al-Umda: The condition of analogy is non-contradiction of the text. And that the hadith is a solitary report conflicting with a principle is a matter of consideration. Hafiz Ibn Hajar explained this, saying: Because it – meaning the mentioned hadith – is an independent principle regarding fasting. Whoever opposes it by analogy with prayer inserts one principle into another. If the door of rejecting authentic ahadith by such means is opened, nothing of hadith would remain except a little. Secondly: The specification of Ramadan has come in some chains of the hadith. Ibn Khuzayma narrated it, and from his route Ibn Hibban, and al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, and from his route al-Bayhaqi, all from the route of Muhammad bin Amr, from Abu Salama ibn Abd al-Rahman, from Abu Abd Allah al-Ansari, from Muhammad bin Amr Hurayra, marfoo: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no qada upon him nor expiation.' Thirdly: The Prophet's statement at the end of the hadith: 'Indeed Allah fed him and gave him drink' is evidence for the validity of his fast, indicating that the action emanating from him is not attributed to him, and the ruling that he is breaking fast requires attribution to him. Hence al-Khattabi said: Its meaning is that forgetfulness is a necessity, and actions of necessity are not attributed in ruling to their doer and are not subject to accountability. Analogy is general except when forgetfulness becomes frequent, for if it continues it removes worship from the limit of devotion and returns it to the limit of non-existence.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi school and the Maliki school regarding a fasting person who eats or drinks forgetfully. The Hanafi position, based on a sound hadith, is that such a person does not need to make up the fast (no qada), and the fast remains valid. The Maliki position, represented by Imam Malik and supported by scholars like Ibn Daqiq al-Id and al-Qadi Ibn al-Arabi, is that the fast is invalidated and qada is required, based on qiyas (analogy) with prayer: just as forgetful impurity invalidates wudu and thus prayer, forgetful eating invalidates fasting. The passage then presents the Hanafi response. First, qiyas cannot override a direct text (nass); there is no ijtihad against explicit revelation. Ibn Hajar explains that the hadith itself is an independent principle for fasting. Second, there are authentic chains specifying that this ruling applies specifically to Ramadan fasting, not just supererogatory. Third, the end of the hadith 'Allah fed him and gave him drink' indicates that the action is not attributed to the fasting person because of forgetfulness, so the fast remains valid. Al-Khattabi adds that forgetfulness is akin to necessity, and necessary actions are not legally attributed to the agent. However, if forgetfulness becomes habitual, it affects the devotion. The Hanafi school therefore holds firmly to the hadith and rejects the qiyas.

Key Terms
نسيان (nisyan): Forgetfulness in acts of worship; the state of being unaware of the prohibition while performing the act.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed or invalidated obligatory worship after its time.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause (illa).
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A solitary hadith reported by one or a few narrators, not reaching tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for someone who eats or drinks forgetfully while fasting in Ramadan?
A) The fast is invalid and qada is required.
B) The fast is valid and no qada is required.
C) The fast is valid but expiation (kaffara) is required.
D) The fast is invalid only if the person ate intentionally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an authentic hadith specific to a worship is an independent principle that takes priority over qiyas derived from a different worship.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
The Three Positions on Abbreviating Hadith
Three positions on abbreviating hadith with a key qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three positions on abbreviating hadith, and apply al-‘Iraqi’s qualification to determine when abbreviation is permitted.

Core Idea
Abbreviating a hadith is permitted if the omitted part is not essential to the meaning.

The transmission of hadith is a sacred trust, but scholars have long debated whether one may narrate only part of a hadith. This lesson examines three major positions—absolute prohibition, absolute permission, and a nuanced middle ground. You will learn how the Hanafi school’s stance on narration by meaning applies to abbreviation and why certain elements like exceptions must be preserved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views on abbreviating hadith as presented in the passage: absolute prohibition, absolute permission, and al-‘Iraqi’s qualified permission.
The third position is not a separate school but a restriction on the second.
2
Understand the basis of prohibition
The first view prohibits abbreviation because it is a form of narration by meaning, which may distort the intended message. The abbreviated version may mislead the listener.
This view is associated with those who insist on verbatim transmission.
3
Analyze the absolute permission
The second view, held by Mujahid and Yahya ibn Ma‘in, allows abbreviation unconditionally. They trust the narrator’s ability to convey the core meaning.
This view relies on the principle that meaning is what matters for legal rulings.
4
Apply al-‘Iraqi’s qualification
Al-‘Iraqi restricts absolute permission: if the omitted part is integrally connected to the narrated part, such as an exception (istithnā’) or a circumstantial clause (ḥāl), deletion spoils the meaning and is not allowed.
This is the critical nuance—preserving the logical structure of the hadith.
5
Connect to Hanafi usul
The Hanafi school generally permits narration by meaning, so abbreviation is allowed under similar conditions: the meaning must be preserved, and any conditional elements must remain intact.
Hanafis would adopt al-‘Iraqi’s restriction as a safeguard.

Source Text

المبحث الثامن
اختلاف الحديث بسبب الاختصار
اختلف الناس في جواز اختصار الحديث، والاقتصار على بعضه، وكانت لهم مذاهب في هذا:
الأول: المنع مطلقاً من اختصار الحديث، بناءً على المنع من الرواية بالمعنى لأن حذف بعض الحديث ورواية بعضه ربما أحدث الخلل فيه، والمختصر لا يشعر.
الثاني: الجواز مطلقاً، وبه قال مجاهد، ويحيى بن معين، وغيرهما. قال الحافظ العراقي: ينبغي تقييد الإطلاق بما إذا لم يكن المحذوف متعلقاً بالمأتي به تعلقاً يخل بالمعنى حذفه كالاستثناء والحال ونحو ذلك، كما سيأتي في
The Eighth Topic: Difference of Hadith Due to Abbreviation. People differed regarding the permissibility of abbreviating the hadith and confining to part of it, and they had schools of thought on this: First: Absolute prohibition of abbreviating hadith, based on the prohibition of narration by meaning, because deleting part of the hadith and narrating part of it may cause defect in it, and the abbreviated (version) does not indicate (the full meaning). Second: Absolute permissibility, and this was held by Mujahid, Yahya ibn Ma'in, and others. Al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi said: It is necessary to restrict the absolute (permission) to cases where the deleted part is not connected to the (narrated) part in a connection that would spoil the meaning if deleted, such as an exception, a circumstantial clause, and the like, as will come later.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage introduces the eighth topic on how differences in hadith texts arise due to abbreviation. It presents two main positions on abbreviating hadith: absolute prohibition and absolute permission. The first view holds that abbreviating is essentially narration by meaning, which is prohibited because it may distort the intended message. The second view, held by well-known scholars like Mujahid and Yahya ibn Ma'in, permits abbreviation. However, al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi qualifies the absolute permission by stating that the omitted portion must not be integrally connected to the preserved part, such that its removal would alter the meaning—for example, dropping an exception or a circumstantial clause. This restriction ensures that the abbreviated version still conveys the correct legal implication.

Key Terms
اختصار الحديث (ikhtisar al-hadith): Abbreviating the hadith by narrating only part of it.
الرواية بالمعنى (al-riwayah bi al-ma'na): Narrating the meaning of a hadith rather than its exact wording.
الاستثناء (al-istithna): An exception clause that modifies a general ruling; if omitted, the meaning changes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi, when is abbreviating a hadith not permissible?
A) When the abbreviation is done by a non-scholar.
B) When the omitted part is essential to the meaning, such as an exception.
C) When the hadith is about legal rulings only.
D) When the narrator does not mention that it is an abbreviation.

Key Takeaway
Abbreviating a hadith is permissible provided that omitted portions are not essential to the legal meaning, especially conditional elements like exceptions.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Effect of Abbreviating Hadith on Juristic Disagreements
Analysis of positions on abbreviating hadith and a case study

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how the abbreviation of a hadith text can lead to juristic differences and apply the Hanafi methodology for evaluating such textual variations.

Core Idea
Abbreviating a hadith can alter its legal implication, leading to juristic disagreement; the full text and context must be considered.

This lesson examines the scholarly rules governing the abbreviation of hadith texts and demonstrates how such abbreviation can directly cause disagreements among jurists. Through the case study of Shu'bah's narration of the wudu hadith, we see how a shortened version versus a longer version can yield different rulings. By understanding the conditions under which abbreviation is permissible and the methods used to detect it, you will gain tools to identify and resolve similar textual issues in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four positions on abbreviating hadith
From the passage, list the third and fourth positions (the first two are implied). Note the conditions each sets for permissibility.
The fourth position, preferred by Ibn al-Salah, is the most widely accepted.
2
Examine the case study: Shu'bah's abbreviation
Read the hadith as narrated by Shu'bah ('No wudu except from sound or smell') and the longer version from other narrators (the condition of being in prayer and the context of doubt).
The longer version adds a specific context that the shorter version omits.
3
Understand Abu Hatim al-Razi's criticism
Note his method: comparing Shu'bah's narration with the majority of Suhayl's companions. This is the standard hadith criticism technique for assessing narrator precision.
This method is used to identify errors (wahm) in narrators.
4
Evaluate counter-arguments by Ibn al-Turkumani and al-Shawkani
They argued that Shu'bah's version might be a separate hadith, not an abbreviation. Consider their reasoning: the general restriction in the first is not present in the second.
They defend Shu'bah based on his reliability and linguistic knowledge.
5
Conclude with the author's methodology
The author supports Abu Hatim by showing that seven narrators contradicted Shu'bah, reinforcing the scientific method of comparing chains.
This illustrates the importance of collecting all available chains before judging.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في الْمَتْن
القول الرابع. فإن كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَمْ يَجُز بلا خلاف، وبه جزم أبو بكر الصيرفي وغيره، وَهُوَ واضح.
الثالث: إن لَمْ يَكُنْ رَوَاهُ عَلَى التمام مرة أخرى هُوَ أَو غيره لَمْ يَجُزْ، وإِن كَانَ رَوَاهُ عَلَى التمام مرة أخرى هُوَ أو غيره جاز.
الرابع: يجوز اختصار الحديث والاقتصار على بعضه إِذا كَانَ فاعل ذَلِكَ عالماً عارفاً، وَكَانَ ما تركه متميزاً عما نقله غَيْر متعلق بِهِ، بِحَيْثُ لا يختل البيان، ولا تختلف الدلالة فيما نقله بترك ما تركه.
وهذا المذهب هُوَ الَّذِي صححه ابن الصلاح وغيره، وعلل ذلك بقوله: «لأن الَّذِي نقله والذي تركه - والحالة هَذِهِ - بمنزلة خبرين منفصلين في أمرين لا تعلق لأحدهما بالآخر».
وَقَدْ ترتب عَلَى اختصار بَعْض الرُّوَاة للأحاديث، خلاف بَيْنَ الفقهاء في بَعْض جزئيات الفقه الإسلامي، ونستطيع أن نمثل ذَلِكَ بِمَا يأتي:
رَوَى شعبة، عن سهيل بن أبي صالح عن أبيه، عن أبي هُرَيْرَةَ عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: لا وضوء إلا من صوت أو ريح. هكذا رَوَى شعبة الْحَدِيث مختصراً، نبّه عَلَى ذَلِكَ حفاظ الْحَدِيث ونقاده، فأبو حاتم الرازي يَقُول: «هَذَا وهم، اختصر شعبة مَتْن هَذَا الْحَدِيْثِ، فَقَالَ: «لا وضوء إلا من صوت أو ريح، ورواه أصحاب سهيل عن سهيل، عن أبيه، عن أبي هُرَيْرَةَ، عن النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «إِذَا كَانَ أحدكم في الصَّلاة فوجد ريحاً من نفسه فلا يخرجن حَتَّى يسمع صوتاً أو يجد ريحاً».
وَقَالَ البَيْهَقِيّ: هَذَا مختصر.
إلا أن الحافظ ابن التركماني قَالَ: «لَوْ كَانَ الْحَدِيْث الأول مختصراً من الثاني لكان موجوداً في الثاني مَعَ زيادة، وعموم الحصر المذكور في الأول لَيْسَ في الثاني، بل هما حديثان مختلفان».
وتابعه عَلَى هَذَا التعليل الشوكاني، فَقَالَ: «شعبة إمام حافظ واسع الرِّوَايَة، وَقَدْ رَوَى هَذَا اللفظ بهذه الصيغة المشتملة عَلَى الحصر، ودينه، وإمامته، ومعرفته بلسان العرب يرد ما ذكره أبو حاتم».
وأيّد هَذَا الشَّيْخ أبو إسحاق الحويني في تحقيقه لـ " منتقى " ابن الجارود.
وَإِذَا ذهبنا نستجلي حقيقة الأمر بطريق البحث العلمي المستند إلى حقائق الأمور وقواعد أصحاب هَذَا الفن، نجد أن أبا حاتم الرازي لَمْ يحكم بهذا الحكم من غَيْر بينة، إِذْ أشار في تضاعيف كلامه إلى أن مستنده في الحكم بوهم شعبة واختصاره للحديث: مخالفته لجمهور أصحاب سهيل، وهذا هو المنهج العلمي الَّذِي يتبعه أَئِمَّة الْحَدِيث مَعْرِفَة ضبط الرَّاوِي، وذلك من خلال مقارنة روايته برواية غيره، وهذا يقتضي جمع الطرق، والحكم عن تثبت لا بالتكهن والتجويز العقلي الخلي عن البرهان والدليل.
وبغية الوصول إلى الحكم الصائب تتبعنا طرق هَذَا الْحَدِيث، فوجدنا سبعة من أصحاب سهيل رووه عن سهيل خالفوا في رواياتهم رِوَايَة شعبة، وهم:
1. جرير بن عَبْد الحميد بن فرط الضبي، عِنْدَ مُسْلِم ، والبيهقي.
2. حماد بن سلمة، عِنْدَ : أَحْمَد ، والدارمي ، وأبي داود.
3. خالد بن عبد الله الواسطي، عِنْدَ ابن خزيمة.
Chapter Two: Disagreement in the Matn
Fourth opinion: If it is like that, it is not permissible without disagreement, and Abu Bakr al-Sayrafi and others asserted it, and it is clear.
Third: If he or someone else did not narrate it in full another time, it is not permissible; but if he or someone else narrated it in full another time, it is permissible.
Fourth: It is permissible to abbreviate a hadith and limit oneself to part of it if the doer is knowledgeable and aware, and what he left out is distinct from what he transmitted and not related to it, such that the explanation is not impaired, and the indication of what he transmitted does not change by leaving out what he left.
This is the view that Ibn al-Salah and others corrected, and he justified it by saying: "Because what he transmitted and what he left out—in this state—are like two separate reports concerning two matters, one not related to the other."
And consequences followed from the abbreviation of some narrators of hadiths, causing disagreement among jurists in some subsidiary matters of Islamic jurisprudence. We can illustrate that with the following:
Shu'bah narrated from Suhayl ibn Abi Salih, from his father, from Abu Hurayrah, from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: "No ablution except from sound or smell." This is how Shu'bah narrated the hadith abbreviated. The hadith memorizers and critics pointed that out. Abu Hatim al-Razi said: "This is a mistake; Shu'bah abbreviated the text of this hadith. He said: 'No ablution except from sound or smell,' while the companions of Suhayl narrated from Suhayl, from his father, from Abu Hurayrah, from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'When one of you is in prayer and feels a wind from himself, he should not leave until he hears a sound or finds a smell.'"
And al-Bayhaqi said: This is abbreviated.
However, al-Hafiz Ibn al-Turkumani said: "If the first hadith were an abbreviation of the second, it would be present in the second with an addition, and the general restriction mentioned in the first is not in the second; rather, they are two different hadiths."
And al-Shawkani followed him in this reasoning, saying: "Shu'bah is an imam, a memorizer, vast in narration. He narrated this wording with this formula that includes restriction. His religion, his imamate, and his knowledge of the Arabic language refute what Abu Hatim mentioned."
And this was supported by Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Huwayni in his editing of "Muntaqa" of Ibn al-Jarud.
And if we go to investigate the reality of the matter through scientific research based on facts and the rules of the masters of this art, we find that Abu Hatim al-Razi did not judge this judgment without evidence, for he indicated in his words that his basis for judging Shu'bah's mistake and abbreviation of the hadith was his contradiction of the majority of Suhayl's companions. This is the scientific method followed by the imams of hadith in knowing the precision of the narrator, by comparing his narration with the narration of others. This requires collecting the chains and judging with certainty, not by conjecture and rational speculation devoid of proof and evidence.
And in order to reach the correct judgment, we have traced the chains of this hadith and found that seven of Suhayl's companions narrated it from Suhayl, contradicting in their narrations the narration of Shu'bah. They are:
1. Jarir ibn 'Abd al-Hamid ibn Fart al-Dabbi, in Muslim and al-Bayhaqi.
2. Hammad ibn Salamah, in Ahmad, al-Darimi, and Abu Dawud.
3. Khalid ibn 'Abd Allah al-Wasiti, in Ibn Khuzaymah.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the permissibility of abbreviating a hadith text (matn) and how such abbreviation can lead to juristic disagreements. The author presents four scholarly positions. The first two are not fully quoted here; the text picks up from the fourth position, which unconditionally prohibits abbreviation if certain conditions are not met. Then the third position links permission to whether the full hadith was narrated elsewhere. The fourth position, preferred by Ibn al-Salah, allows abbreviation if the narrator is competent, the omitted part is distinguished and unrelated, and the meaning is not affected. The author then illustrates this with a famous hadith about wudu' narrated by Shu'bah in an abbreviated form: 'No wudu except from sound or smell.' Critics like Abu Hatim al-Razi considered this a mistake because other narrators from the same teacher (Suhayl) transmitted a longer version that specifies the context of prayer. The author notes a counter-argument by Ibn al-Turkumani and al-Shawkani that Shu'bah's version is a different hadith. The author concludes by supporting Abu Hatim's methodology of comparing narrations to assess a narrator's precision, and provides a list of seven narrators who contradict Shu'bah's version, thus justifying the criticism. The passage demonstrates that differences in the text (matn) of a hadith, whether through abbreviation or addition, can directly cause juristic disagreements, as the legal ruling derived from the hadith may vary depending on which version is relied upon.

Key Terms
اختصار الحديث (ikhtisar al-hadith): Narrating only part of a hadith's text while omitting the rest.
المتن (al-matn): The text of a hadith, as opposed to its chain of transmission (sanad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was the basis of Abu Hatim al-Razi's criticism of Shu'bah's narration?
A) Shu'bah was known to be a weak narrator.
B) Shu'bah's narration contradicted the majority of narrators from Suhayl.
C) Shu'bah intentionally changed the wording of the hadith.
D) The hadith was not authentic.

Key Takeaway
Abbreviation of hadith can cause juristic disagreement when the omitted part contains a condition or context that affects the legal ruling; thus, careful textual comparison is essential.

Resolving Contradictions in Hadith: Error vs. Abbreviation
Critique of Abu Hatim's claim that Shu'bah erred in narrating a hadith about wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the methodology of comparing narrations to determine whether a hadith variant indicates an error or a legitimate abbreviation, and explain the usul principle that the majority narration is preferred while allowing for meaning-based abbreviation.

Core Idea
A narrator's variant may be a valid abbreviation by meaning; comparison with majority narrations is key.

This passage examines a famous ikhtilaf over Shu'bah ibn al-Hajjaj's narration of a hadith on wudu. Abu Hatim al-Razi claimed Shu'bah erred by abbreviating the hadith, but others defended him, arguing that he transmitted the meaning concisely. By analyzing the methodology behind these claims, we learn how hadith scholars resolve apparent contradictions between narrations—a crucial skill for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed hadith variant
Locate the key phrase 'يسمع صوتاً أو يجد ريحاً' in the passage and understand that Shu'bah's version includes an exclusivity (حصر) that the longer version lacks.
The exclusivity is central to the dispute.
2
Understand Abu Hatim's critique
Abu Hatim judged Shu'bah's narration as an error (وهم) based on its contradiction with the majority of Suhayl's students. Recognize that comparing narrations is a valid scientific method.
His basis is the majority's divergence.
3
Examine the opposing defense
Al-Shawkani and al-Huwayni defend Shu'bah, citing his reliability and knowledge of Arabic. The author later supports that the variant may be an abbreviation (اختصار) by meaning, not a mistake.
The defense relies on narrator integrity and linguistic possibility.
4
List the supporting narrators
The passage enumerates seven narrators from Suhayl who contradict Shu'bah. Count them and note their sources.
The majority narration is considered more reliable.
5
Apply the usul principle
Conclude that the majority narration is preferred, but this does not automatically brand Shu'bah an error; the difference can be explained as meaning-based abbreviation, refuting Ibn al-Turkumani's objection.
The principle: comparison of chains, but allowance for ikhtisar.

He hears a sound or finds a smell. Al-Bayhaqi said: This is an abbreviation. Except that al-Hafiz Ibn al-Turkumani said: 'If the first hadith were an abbreviation of the second, it would be present in the second with addition, and the general exclusivity mentioned in the first is not in the second; rather, they are two different hadiths.' Al-Shawkani followed this reasoning, saying: 'Shu'bah is an imam, a memorizer, with vast transmission; he narrated this wording in this form containing exclusivity; his religion, imamate, and knowledge of the Arabic language refute what Abu Hatim mentioned.' This was supported by Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Huwayni in his editing of Ibn al-Jarud's Muntaqa. If we go to investigate the truth of the matter through scientific research based on facts and the rules of the masters of this field, we find that Abu Hatim al-Razi did not rule this without evidence, as he indicated in his speech that his basis for ruling that Shu'bah erred and abbreviated the hadith was his opposition to the majority of Suhayl's students. This is the scientific method followed by the imams of hadith in determining the accuracy of a narrator, by comparing his narration with others, which requires collecting the chains and judging with certainty, not by conjecture and mental speculation devoid of proof and evidence. To reach the correct judgment, we traced the chains of this hadith and found seven of Suhayl's students who narrated from Suhayl and contradicted Shu'bah's narration: (1) 1. Jarir ibn 'Abd al-Hamid ibn Qart al-Dabbi, according to Muslim and al-Bayhaqi. 2. Hammad ibn Salamah, according to Ahmad, al-Darimi, and Abu Dawud. 3. Khalid ibn 'Abd Allah al-Wasiti, according to Ibn Khuzaymah. 4. Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah, according to Abu 'Awanah. 5. 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Muhammad al-Darawardi, according to al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Khuzaymah, and Ibn al-Mundhir. 6. Muhammad ibn Ja'far, according to al-Bayhaqi. 7. Yahya ibn al-Muhallab al-Bajali, according to al-Tabarani in al-Awsat. The narration of the majority is more deserving to be followed and judged free from error. This does not impugn Shu'bah's imamate and religion; this is one matter and that is another, and who does not err? It is not required that the wording of the abbreviated hadith be present in the hadith from which it is abbreviated; rather, the existence of the meaning is sufficient. It may be that the narrator abbreviated the hadith, then narrated the abbreviated wording by meaning, so no link remains between them except the meaning. This is what we find in our hadith, and by this the objection of Ibn al-Turkumani and those who follow him is refuted.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a hadith variation. The author critiques Abu Hatim al-Razi's claim that Shu'bah ibn al-Hajjaj erred by abbreviating a hadith about wudu (hearing sound or finding smell). The author argues that Abu Hatim's reasoning is based on comparing Shu'bah's narration with that of the majority of Suhayl's students, which is a valid scientific method. However, the author also notes that the shorter version may be a legitimate abbreviation of meaning, not necessarily a mistake. The passage lists seven narrators who contradicted Shu'bah, but concludes that the collective narration is more reliable while still upholding Shu'bah's integrity. The key point is that in evaluating a narrator's accuracy, one should compare narrations; but a difference in wording does not automatically indicate an error, as the narrator may have transmitted the meaning concisely.

Key Terms
اختصار الحديث (ikhtisar al-hadith): Abbreviation of a hadith by transmitting the meaning in fewer words, as opposed to verbatim transmission.
وهم (wahm): An error or mistake in a narrator's transmission, often identified by comparison with other narrators.
سهيل (Suhayl): Suhayl ibn Abi Salih, a hadith narrator from the tabi'un, whose students transmitted this hadith.
الحصر (al-hasr): Exclusivity; the linguistic feature indicating restriction, central to the dispute between the two versions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The majority narration is preferred, but a reliable narrator's variant may be a valid abbreviation by meaning, not necessarily an error.

Dispute on Wudu' Breaking by Touching the Penis — A Model Example
Model example of how textual differences affect fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how differences in hadith interpretation lead to differing fiqh rulings on wudu' breaking by touch, and compare the Shafi'i/Zahiri conditions with the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Touching the penis breaks wudu' according to most schools but not Hanafis.

This lesson examines a classic ikhtilaf: whether touching the private parts invalidates wudu. The passage presents the view of the majority (Shafi'is, Zahiris, most Malikis) that it does break wudu, listing numerous Companions and Successors who held this. By studying the conditions set by each school, you will see how differing interpretations of hadith lead to distinct rulings. This model example illustrates the usul principle that textual variants and interpretive choices produce fiqh divergence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core fiqh issue
The central question is: does touching the private part (penis) break wudu? The passage splits jurists into two opinions. Note that the majority opinion is that it does break wudu.
This is a well-known ikhtilaf between Hanafis and the rest.
2
List the schools and their specific conditions
From the passage, extract the Shafi'i and Zahiri conditions for wudu' breaking. Shafi'is require touch with inner palm or inner fingers, without a barrier, and intent irrelevant. Zahiris require touch with any body part except thigh/shin/foot, require intent, and extend to touching another's private part.
Note how each school adds or removes conditions, reflecting their interpretation of the underlying hadith.
3
Contrast with the Hanafi position
Though not explicitly stated in the passage, the Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds that touching the private parts does NOT break wudu. This is based on their interpretation of hadith and their usul principles (e.g., preference for the hadith of Talq ibn 'Ali over the hadith of Busrah).
The Hanafi position is the second opinion implied by the text's 'two opinions'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أثره في اختلاف الفقهاء النموذج الأول: نقض الوضوء بمس الذكر اختلف الفقهاء في من مس ذكره أو ما في معناه، هل ينتقض وضوؤه أم لا؟ وافترقوا على قولين: الأول: إذا مس المتوضئ فرجه انتقض وضوؤه، وعليه الوضوء من جديد، وبه قال جمهور أهل العلم من الصحابة فمن بعدهم، منهم: عمر بن الخطاب، وابنه عبد الله ابن عمر، والبراء بن عازب، وزيد بن خالد الجهني، وجابر بن عبد الله، وأبو أيوب الأنصاري، وزيد بن ثابت وعائشة، وأم حبيبة، وبسرة بنت صفوان، وعطاء بن أبي رباح، وطاووس، وعروة بن الزبير، وسليمان بن يسار وأبان بن عثمان، والزهري، و مجاهد، ومكحول، وجابر بن زيد، والشعبي، وعكرمة، ومصعب بن سعد، ويحيى بن أبي كثير، وهشام بن عروة، وأبو العالية، وجماعة أهل الشام والمغرب، وأكثر أهل الحديث. وإليه ذهب الأوزاعي والليث بن سعد وداود الظاهري وإسحاق بن راهويه والطبري. وَهُوَ رواية عن سعد بن أبي وقاص، وابن عباس وأبي هريرة، وسعيد بن المسيب، وسعيد بن جبير، والحسن. وهو مذهب الشافعية والظاهرية وجمهور المالكية ورواية عن الإمام أحمد، على تفصيل بينهم، نبينه فيما يأتي: الشافعية: إذا مس رجل ذكر نفسه أو ذكر غيره، صغيراً أو كبيراً، حياً أو ميتاً، قريباً أو أجنبياً، وإن كان الذكر مقطوعاً من حي، بشرط أن يكون ببطن الكف أو بطن الأصابع أنقض وضوء اللامس والحكم نفسه بالنسبة للمرأة، وينتقض أيضاً بمس حلقة الدبر في جديد مذهب الشَّافِعِي. ولا ينقض الوضوء مس أنثييه أو إليتيه، أو أعجازه، أو عانته، أو فرج بهيمة، ويشترط في النقض عدم الحائل، ولا يشترط العمد، بَلْ يستوي فيه العامد والساهي. الظاهرية: مَنْ مَسَّ ذَكَرَهُ بأي جزء من بدنه عدا الفخذ والساق أو الرجل هَذَا الحكم إِلَى عامداً انتقض وضوءه، وكذا المرأة إن تعمدت مس فرجها، ويتعدى مس فرج الغير صغيراً أو كبيراً، حياً أو ميتاً مَحْرَمٍ أو غَيْر مَحْرَم، بأي جزء من بدن اللامس، ويشترط في جَمِيع ذَلِكَ عدم الحائل، ولا يشترط وجود اللذة. المالكية: قَالَ ابن عَبْد البر: اضطرب قَوْل مالك في إيجاب الوضوء منه،
Its effect on the difference of jurists. The first model: Breaking wudu' by touching the penis. The jurists differed regarding one who touches his penis or something similar: does his wudu' break or not? They split into two opinions: The first: If the one in wudu' touches his private part, his wudu' breaks and he must perform wudu' anew. This is held by the majority of scholars from the Companions and those after them, including: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, his son 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, al-Bara' ibn 'Azib, Zayd ibn Khalid al-Juhani, Jabir ibn 'Abdillah, Abu Ayyub al-Ansari, Zayd ibn Thabit, 'A'ishah, Umm Habibah, Busrah bint Safwan, 'Ata' ibn Abi Rabah, Tawus, 'Urwah ibn al-Zubayr, Sulayman ibn Yasar, Aban ibn 'Uthman, al-Zuhri, Mujahid, Makhul, Jabir ibn Zayd, al-Sha'bi, 'Ikrimah, Mus'ab ibn Sa'd, Yahya ibn Abi Kathir, Hisham ibn 'Urwah, Abu al-'Aliyah, and the people of Syria and Morocco, and most of the people of hadith. Al-Awza'i, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Dawud al-Zahiri, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and al-Tabari also held this view. It is also a narration from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Ibn 'Abbas, Abu Hurayrah, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, and al-Hasan. This is the school of the Shafi'is, the Zahiris, the majority of Malikis, and a narration from Ahmad, with details among them which we will explain as follows: Shafi'is: If a man touches his own penis or another's, whether young or old, alive or dead, relative or stranger, even if the penis is severed from a living person, provided it is with the inner palm or inner fingers, the toucher's wudu' breaks; the same ruling applies for a woman. Wudu' also breaks by touching the anal ring in the new view of al-Shafi'i. Wudu' does not break by touching his testicles, buttocks, hips, pubic area, or an animal's private part. For breaking, the absence of a barrier is required, and intent is not required; the deliberate and forgetful are equal. Zahiris: Whoever touches his penis with any part of his body except the thigh, shin, or foot — this ruling applies to one who does it deliberately — his wudu' breaks; likewise a woman if she deliberately touches her private part. It also extends to touching another's private part, whether young or old, alive or dead, whether mahram or non-mahram, with any part of the toucher's body. In all this, the absence of a barrier is required, and the presence of pleasure is not required. Malikis: Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said: The statement of Malik fluctuated regarding making wudu' obligatory from it.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the first model example (النموذج الأول) showing how differences in the text (matn) of hadith lead to differences among jurists. The specific issue is whether touching the private parts (specifically the penis) breaks wudu'. The author first states the majority view that it does break wudu', listing numerous Companions, Successors, and later authorities who held this view. This is the position of the Shafi'i, Zahiri, and majority Maliki schools, and a narration from Ahmad. The passage then details the Shafi'i position: wudu' breaks by touching the penis (including a severed one) or the anus (in the new view), with the palm or inner fingers, without a barrier, and intent is irrelevant. The Zahiri position is similar but extends to any body part except thigh and foot, and requires intent. The Maliki position is mentioned as being unsettled, but the text cuts off before completion. The passage demonstrates how different interpretations of hadith (e.g., the hadith of Busrah) lead to different fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
نقض الوضوء (naqd al-wudu): Invalidation or breaking of ritual ablution (wudu); requires renewal of wudu.
مس الذكر (mass al-dhakar): Touching the male genitalia (penis).
بطن الكف (batn al-kaff): The inner part of the palm (not the back) — a condition for breaking wudu in the Shafi'i view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The touch must be deliberate (amdan).
D) The person must be in a state of impurity.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on wudu' breaking by touch demonstrates how different schools' approaches to hadith authenticity, interpretation of commands, and the use of istihsan lead to divergent fiqh rulings.
