Madhhab Positions on Wudu Nullification by Touching the Private Parts
Comparative presentation of madhhab positions on touching private parts nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and compare the conditions set by the Shafi'i, Zahiri, and Maliki schools for wudu nullification upon touching the private parts, and explain the usul al-fiqh basis for their differences.

Core Idea
Touching the private parts: three schools, three sets of conditions — contact area, intention, barrier, pleasure.

This passage presents a detailed comparative analysis of the disagreement among jurists regarding whether touching the private parts nullifies wudu. It provides specific conditions for each school, illustrating how different usul principles lead to varying rulings. By studying this passage, you will learn to identify the key factors that differentiate the Shafi'i, Zahiri, and Maliki positions, and understand the role of textual interpretation and methodological choices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
State the central question: Does touching the private parts nullify wudu? Note that this passage focuses on schools that affirm nullification under certain conditions.
The Hanafi school holds that touching does not nullify, but this passage covers only schools that disagree with that view.
2
List the schools and their general stance
The passage covers Shafi'i, Zahiri, and Maliki schools. Summarize each school's basic ruling and any internal differences.
The Maliki school has multiple narrations, so there is no single Maliki position.
3
Extract conditions for each school
For each school, list the specific conditions required for nullification: required body part, intention, barrier, pleasure, and scope (own vs. others, alive/dead).
Pay attention to details like 'palm of hand' for Shafi'i vs. 'any part except thigh, shin, foot' for Zahiri.
4
Compare the conditions across schools
Identify similarities and differences. For example, all require no barrier for nullification, but differ on intention, pleasure, and the precise body part of the toucher.
This comparison reveals how each school's usul methodology shapes its ruling.
5
Reflect on usul principles
Consider how textual interpretation (amm, haqiqah, etc.) leads to these differences. For instance, Shafi'i's restriction to palm/fingers may stem from a specific hadith interpretation, while Zahiri's broader coverage reflects a literalist approach.
This step connects the rulings to the foundational principles of each school.

Source Text

الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في المنـ
ويحيى بن أبي كثير، وهشام بن عروة، وأبو العالية، وجماعة أهل الشام والمغرب، وأكثر أهل الحديث.
وإليه ذهب الأوزاعي والليث بن سعد وداود الظاهري وإسحاق بن راهويه والطبري.
وَهُوَ رواية عن سعد بن أبي وقاص، وابن عباس وأبي هريرة، وسعيد بن المسيب، وسعيد بن جبير، والحسن.
وهو مذهب الشافعية والظاهرية وجمهور المالكية ورواية عن الإمام أحمد، على تفصيل بينهم، نبينه فيما يأتي:
الشافعية: إذا مس رجل ذكر نفسه أو ذكر غيره، صغيراً أو كبيراً، حياً أو ميتاً، قريباً أو أجنبياً، وإن كان الذكر مقطوعاً من حي، بشرط أن يكون ببطن الكف أو بطن الأصابع انتقض وضوء اللامس والحكم نفسه بالنسبة للمرأة، وينتقض أيضاً بمس حلقة الدبر في جديد مذهب الشافعي. ولا ينقض الوضوء مس أنثييه أو إليتيه، أو أعجازه، أو عانته، أو فرج بهيمة، ويشترط في النقض عدم الحائل، ولا يشترط العمد، بل يستوي فيه العامد والساهي. الظاهرية: من مس ذكره بأي جزء من بدنه عدا الفخذ والساق أو الرجل هذا الحكم إلى عامداً انتقض وضوءه، وكذا المرأة إن تعمدت مس فرجها، ويتعدى مس فرج الغير صغيراً أو كبيراً، حياً أو ميتاً محرم أو غير محرم، بأي جزء من بدن اللامس، ويشترط في جميع ذلك عدم الحائل، ولا يشترط وجود اللذة. المالكية: قال ابن عبد البر: اضطرب قول مالك في إيجاب الوضوء منه، واختلف مذهبه فيه.
والذي وقفت عليه من أقوال المالكية في نقض الوضوء من مس الذكر ما يأتي:
١. قيدها بعضهم وهم: إسماعيل بن إسحاق، وابن بكير، وابن المنتاب، وأبو الفرج، والأبهري، وسائر مالكية بغداد، بوجود اللذة، فإن مسه ملتذاً وجب عليه الوضوء، وإن صلى ولم يتوضأ من مسه فعليه الإعادة سواء كان في الوقت أو بعده. وإن لم يلتذ بمسه فلا شيء عليه.
٢. ذهب أصبغ بن الفرج وعيسى بن دينار منهم إلى إيجاب الوضوء مطلقاً، وإن صلى بعد مسه من غير وضوء فعليه الإعادة في الوقت أو بعده.
٣. ورأى سحنون والعتبي أن لا وضوء عليه مطلقاً، ولا إعادة على من صلى بعد لمسه من غير وضوء، سواء في الوقت أم بعده.
٤. وذهب ابن القاسم وأشهب وابن وهب في رواية إلى أن عليه الوضوء من الذكر، فإن صلى بعد أن مسه من غير وضوء فعليه الإعادة ما دام في الوقت فإن خرج الوقت فلا إعادة عليه.
قال العلامة خليل في مختصره الذي أصبح عمدة المالكية – لا سيما المتأخرون
Chapter Two / Difference in the Text
And Yahya ibn Abi Kathir, Hisham ibn Urwa, Abu al-Aliyah, and the people of Syria and Morocco, and most of the people of hadith.
And to this went al-Awza'i, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Dawud al-Zahiri, Ishaq ibn Rahwayh, and al-Tabari.
And it is a narration from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Ibn Abbas, Abu Hurayra, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, and al-Hasan.
And it is the madhhab of the Shafi'is, the Zahiris, the majority of the Malikis, and a narration from Imam Ahmad, with a detail among them that we will explain below:
The Shafi'is: If a man touches his own penis or the penis of another, young or old, alive or dead, relative or stranger, even if the penis is cut off from a living person, on condition that it is with the palm of the hand or the inside of the fingers, the wudu of the toucher is nullified, and the same ruling applies to the woman. And it is also nullified by touching the anal ring in the new opinion of al-Shafi'i. And wudu is not nullified by touching his testicles or buttocks, or his hips, or his pubic area, or the private part of an animal. And it is required for nullification that there be no barrier, and intention is not required; rather, the intentional and the forgetful are equal. The Zahiris: Whoever touches his penis with any part of his body except the thigh, shin, or foot, this ruling to intentionally, his wudu is nullified. And likewise the woman if she intentionally touches her private part, and it extends to touching the private part of another, young or old, alive or dead, mahram or non-mahram, with any part of the body of the toucher, and it is required in all that there be no barrier, and the presence of pleasure is not required. The Malikis: Ibn Abd al-Barr said: The saying of Malik varied concerning making wudu obligatory from it, and his madhhab differed therein.
And what I have come across of the sayings of the Malikis on the nullification of wudu from touching the penis is as follows:
1. Some of them restricted it, namely: Isma'il ibn Ishaq, Ibn Bukayr, Ibn al-Mintab, Abu al-Faraj, al-Abhari, and the rest of the Malikis of Baghdad, to the presence of pleasure. So if he touches it with pleasure, wudu becomes obligatory upon him, and if he prays without performing wudu from his touch, he must repeat whether within the time or after. And if he does not experience pleasure from his touch, then nothing is upon him.
2. Asbagh ibn al-Faraj and Isa ibn Dinar among them went to making wudu obligatory absolutely, and if he prays after his touch without wudu, he must repeat within the time or after.
3. And Sahnun and al-Utbi held that there is no wudu upon him absolutely, and no repetition upon one who prays after touching without wudu, whether within the time or after.
4. And Ibn al-Qasim, Ashhab, and Ibn Wahb in one narration went to that wudu from the penis is obligatory upon him, and if he prays after touching it without wudu, he must repeat as long as he is within the time, and if the time leaves, then no repetition upon him.
Al-Allamah Khalil said in his Mukhtasar, which became the reliance of the Malikis – especially the later ones
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed comparative discussion of the disagreement (ikhtilaf) among jurists regarding whether touching the private parts (specifically the penis) nullifies wudu. It begins by listing the early scholars and schools who held that it does nullify wudu, including many Companions and Successors, as well as the Shafi'i, Zahiri, and majority of Maliki schools. The text then expounds the precise conditions for each school.

The Shafi'i position is that touching the penis (or the anal ring in the new opinion) with the palm or inside of the fingers breaks wudu, regardless of intention, age of the person touched, or whether the person is alive or dead. There must be no barrier. Touching other body parts like testicles or buttocks does not nullify wudu.

The Zahiri school holds a broader view: touching the penis with any part of the body (except thigh, shin, or foot) nullifies wudu, but only if intentional for the Zahiris (unlike Shafi'i). This applies also to women and to touching others' private parts.

For the Malikis, the text records multiple positions within the school: some require sexual pleasure for nullification (Baghdad Malikis), some make it absolute (Asbagh and Isa), some deny nullification entirely (Sahnun and al-Utbi), and some require repetition only within the time (Ibn al-Qasim et al.). This variety shows the internal ikhtilaf within the Maliki school itself. The passage concludes with a reference to Khalil's Mukhtasar, which standardized the later Maliki position (likely the one requiring repetition only within the time, as is known in the Maliki school).

Key Terms
مس الذكر (mass al-dhakar): Touching the male genitalia
نقض الوضوء (naqd al-wudu): Nullification of ablution; actions that break wudu
الحائل (al-ha'il): Barrier or covering that prevents direct skin contact
اللذة (al-ladhdha): Sexual pleasure or desire

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Shafi'i school as described in the passage, which condition is required for wudu to be nullified by touching the penis?
A) Intentional touch
B) Touch with sexual desire
C) Touch with the palm or inside of the fingers
D) Touch without any barrier

Key Takeaway
Different schools derive varying conditions for wudu nullification from the same basic evidence, reflecting their distinct usul al-fiqh methodologies.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Hanafi Response to Hadith of Busra on Wudu from Touching Private Parts
Dispute over authenticity and implication of a hadith on wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi usul arguments against relying on the hadith of Busra and explain why it is rejected despite its apparent authenticity.

Core Idea
Hanafis reject a hadith if it contradicts a stronger proof and if its transmission is insufficient for a matter of public necessity.

The hadith of Busra bint Safwan—'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu'—is a key proof for those who hold that touching the private parts invalidates wudu. The Hanafi school, however, rejects this hadith based on rigorous usul principles. This passage dissects the Hanafi objections and the counter-arguments, illustrating the deeper methodology of hadith criticism within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hadith in Dispute
Recognize the hadith of Busra: 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu.' It is a clear command (amr) that appears to indicate obligation.
The hadith is cited by the opposing school (Shafi'i) as primary evidence.
2
Understand the Hanafi Objections
The Hanafis criticize the hadith on two main fronts: (1) authenticity (thubut) – the chain includes an unknown narrator; (2) applicability – it is a single narration (ahad) in a matter that afflicts the public generally (ta'umm bihi al-balwa).
Al-Sarakhsi argues that if the ruling were true, it would have been widely transmitted.
3
Apply the Principle of Ta'umm bihi al-Balwa
This principle holds that rulings needed by the general public must be established by widespread transmission (tawatur). An ahad hadith is insufficient for such rulings, regardless of the reliability of its chain.
This is a distinct Hanafi usul principle not shared by the Shafi'i school.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to the Objection on the Chain
The passage also presents a rebuttal showing that other chains of the hadith have Urwa hearing directly from Busra, removing the unknown narrator. This demonstrates that the dispute involves a robust back-and-forth.
The author does not settle the dispute but presents both sides.
5
Derive the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis do not perform wudu after touching their private parts; Shafi'is do. This difference stems directly from the acceptance or rejection of the hadith based on usul principles.
Understanding this dispute highlights how usul methodology shapes fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في الْمَتْنِ
في الروض النضير: «وَهُوَ المحفوظ عن أئِمَّة أهل البيت (). وإليه ذهب الحنفية )، والزيدية(۳)، والإمامية)، وهُوَ رِوَايَة عن الإمام أحمد)، و به جزم ابن المنذر ) ، واختاره العُتقي وسحنون من المالكية).
الأدلة
(1)
استدل من قَالَ بنقض الوضوء من مس الذكر بجملة أدلة، من بينها حَدِيث بسرة بنت صفوان أن النبي الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «مَن مس ذكره فليتوضأ». وَقَدْ رد الحنفية الاستدلال بهذا الْحَدِيث مِن وَجْهَيْنِ:
الأول: الطعن في الْحَدِيث من ناحية الثبوت).
الثاني: الاعتراض عَلَيْهِ من حَيْثُ إن بسرة تفردت بنقله، والفرض أن ينقله عدد كبير؛ لتوافر الدواعي على نقله (۱) ، قَالَ السرخسي (۱۱): (ما بال رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لَمْ يقل هَذَا بَيْنَ يدي كبار الصَّحَابَة، حَتَّى لَمْ ينقله أحد مِنْهُمْ، وإنما قَالَهُ بَيْنَ يدي بسرة؟ وَقَدْ كَانَ رَسُول الله أشد حياءً من العذراء في خدرها (۱۲) .
ويتفرع عن إيرادهم هَذَا الاعتراض بِمَا يأتي: ا ورد في بَعْض الروايات أن مروان بعث شرطياً إلى بسرة، فنقل الْحَدِيث عَنْهَا وسمعه منه عروة، وهذا الشرطي مجهول. فتبين أن سَمَاع عروة عن طريق مجهول، فَلاً تقوم الحجة بإخباره.
٠٢ أن هَذَا الْحَدِيْث يعارض حَدِيث طلق (۱) بن عَلِيّ الحنفي في تَرْك الوضوء من مسه، وَقَدْ قَالَ التِّرْمِذِيّ في حَدِيث طلق «هَذَا الْحَدِيث أحسن شيء روي في الباب (۲).
هَذَا
إِن هَذَا الْحَدِيث حَدِيث آحاد، وَقَد ورد فيما تعم به البلوى، وهذه ريبة توجب التوقف في قبوله.
أنَّهُ تضمن حكماً يختص بالرجال، وَقَدْ روته امرأة.
ونجيب عن هَذِهِ الاعتراضات بِمَا يأتي:
أما الأول: فإنه قد ورد في بَعْض طرق الْحَدِيث التصريح بأن عروة سمعه مباشرة من غَيْر واسطة من بسرة. فأخرج أحمد (۳)، وابن الجارود ()، وابن حبان () ، والدارقطني)، والحاكم، والبيهقي في السنن (۸) ، وفي مَعْرِفَة السنن والآثار )، هَذَا الْحَدِيث وفيه التصريح بسماع عروة من بسرة.
ولنسق رِوَايَة ابن الجارود ليتضح هَذَا، فروى بإسناده عن هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن مروان بن الحكم عن بسرة، أن النبي الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «من مس ذكره فليتوضأ». قَالَ عروة: سألت بسرة فصدقته.
ومن خلال التتبع للطرق الَّتِي روي بِهَا الْحَدِيث، نقف على ثلاث طرق للحديث من طريق عروة، هي:
۱ عروة، عن مروان عن بسرة.
He said: Chapter Two / Disagreement in the Text (Matn). In al-Rawd al-Nadhir: 'And it is what is preserved from the Imams of Ahl al-Bayt. The Hanafis, Zaydis, Imamis, and one narration from Imam Ahmad adopted it. Ibn al-Mundhir affirmed it, and al-Utqi and Sahnun from the Malikis chose it.' The proofs: (1) Those who said that wudu is broken by touching the penis used a set of proofs, among them the hadith of Busra bint Safwan that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu.' The Hanafis rejected using this hadith as evidence in two ways: First: criticizing the hadith in terms of its authenticity (thubut). Second: objecting to it because Busra alone transmitted it, whereas many should have transmitted it due to the widespread motive for its transmission. Al-Sarakhsi said: 'What is the matter that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not say this in the presence of the senior Companions, so that none of them transmitted it, but he said it only in the presence of Busra? And the Messenger of Allah was more shy than a virgin in her seclusion.' From this objection branches the following: first, in some narrations Marwan sent a policeman to Busra, and he transmitted the hadith from her, and Urwa heard it from him, but this policeman is unknown (majhul). So it becomes clear that Urwa's hearing was through an unknown chain, so proof is not established by his report. Second, this hadith contradicts the hadith of Talq ibn Ali al-Hanafi on leaving wudu from touching it. Al-Tirmidhi said about the hadith of Talq: 'This hadith is the best thing reported on the topic.' Third, this is an ahad hadith and it deals with a matter of public necessity (ta'umm bihi al-balwa), and this is a suspicious matter that requires hesitation in accepting it. Fourth, it includes a ruling specific to men, and a woman narrated it. We respond to these objections as follows: As for the first: it has been reported in some chains of the hadith that Urwa heard it directly without an intermediary from Busra. Ahmad, Ibn al-Jarud, Ibn Hibban, al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, and al-Bayhaqi in al-Sunan and in Ma'rifat al-Sunan wa al-Athar narrated this hadith, and in it is the explicit statement that Urwa heard from Busra. Let us present the narration of Ibn al-Jarud to clarify this: he narrated with his chain from Hisham ibn Urwa, from his father, from Marwan ibn al-Hakam, from Busra, that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu.' Urwa said: 'I asked Busra and she confirmed it.' Through tracing the chains by which the hadith is narrated, we find three chains from Urwa: (1) Urwa, from Marwan, from Busra.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the hadith of Busra concerning wudu from touching the private parts. The author first notes that many schools—Hanafis, Zaydis, Imamis, and a narration from Ahmad—hold that wudu is not broken by touching the penis. The opposing view (typically Shafi'i and some others) relies on the hadith of Busra. The Hanafis object on two grounds: 1) The hadith's authenticity is weak because Busra alone transmitted it, and the occasion called for widespread transmission. Al-Sarakhsi argues that if the Prophet had said this publicly, senior Companions would have transmitted it. 2) The hadith is contradicted by the hadith of Talq, which al-Tirmidhi considers the best on the topic. Additionally, the Hanafis note the hadith is ahad and pertains to a public need (ta'umm bihi al-balwa), and it was narrated by a woman, casting doubt. The author then presents the response: other chains show Urwa heard directly from Busra, so the chain is sound. This demonstrates the Hanafi usul principle that hadith in matters of public necessity require tawatur, and that mursal hadith or those with unknown narrators are not accepted.

Key Terms
ثبوت (thubut): The authenticity or reliability of a hadith's chain of transmission.
آحاد (ahad): A hadith narrated by a small number of narrators, not reaching tawatur.
تعم به البلوى (ta'umm bihi al-balwa): A matter that afflicts the general public frequently, requiring widespread transmission if it is a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hadith contradicts the Quran.
B) The hadith is weak in its chain of transmission (thubut).
C) The hadith is about women, not men.
D) The hadith is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rejection of Busra's hadith exemplifies how usul principles—thubut, ahad, ta'umm bihi al-balwa, and conflict with stronger evidence—determine the operative ruling.

The Mashhur Hadith and Naskh: A Case Study in Wudu Nullification
Concluding analysis of authenticity and abrogation of conflicting hadiths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi use of the mashhur hadith category and evaluate how the naskh argument affects the ruling on wudu nullification.

Core Idea
A mashhur hadith is acted upon in matters of general need even if not mutawatir.

When two authentic hadiths conflict, the scholar must determine how to reconcile them. In this passage, the author explains that despite the authenticity of both Busra's and Talq's hadiths, the Hanafi school defends its position by citing the mashhur nature of the evidence and the principle that wudu does not break without clear proof. The majority, however, see Busra's hadith as abrogating Talq's. Let's dissect the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting hadiths
Locate the two hadiths mentioned: Busra's hadith (touching the private part nullifies wudu) and Talq's hadith (it is merely a part of you, no nullification). Note their sources and the dispute over authenticity.
Both hadiths are accepted as authentic by the author, but they give opposite rulings.
2
Understand mashhur in Hanafi usul
Read the phrase 'مجموعها يكون في أقل أحواله مشهورًا' (their totality in the least of its states is mashhur). Define mashhur as a hadith narrated by multiple chains, below tawatur, but sufficient for action in matters of general need (umum al-balwa). Recognize its epistemic weight in Hanafi thought.
Hanafis argue the ruling from the Companions constitutes a mashhur evidence.
3
Analyze the naskh argument
Identify the majority view that Busra's hadith abrogates Talq's. Note that this requires certainty of chronology and that Busra's hadith is later. The text cites several scholars supporting this.
The majority consider abrogation as the only reconciliation.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response via Ibn Abd al-Barr
Examine Ibn Abd al-Barr's principle: wudu does not break except by consensus or an unambiguous sunna. This allows the Kufi (Hanafi) view to stand despite the naskh claim. Note that the Hanafi position is supported by Companion practice.
This is a methodological defense: the original principle of wudu integrity is not easily overturned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَيّاً مَا يَكُن الْأَمْر فَإِن هَذَا الْحُكْم قَدْ رُوِيَ عَنْ ثَمَانِيَةٍ مِنْ الصَّحَابَةِ، بَعْضُ طُرُقِهِمْ صَحِيحَةٌ، وَبَعْضُهَا قَابِلٌ لِلْاعْتِضَادِ، فَمَجْمُوعُهَا يَكُونُ فِي أَقَلِّ أَحْوَالِهِ مَشْهُورًا، وَالْمَشْهُورُ يُعْمَلُ بِهِ عِنْدَهُمْ فِيمَا تَعُمُّ بِهِ الْبَلْوَى.
أَمَّا الرَّابِعُ، وَهُوَ كَوْنُ الْحَدِيثِ مِمَّا يَخْتَصُّ حُكْمُهُ بِالرِّجَالِ، وَقَدْ نَقَلَتْهُ امْرَأَةٌ، فَقَوْلٌ مَرْدُودٌ، فَقَدْ مَضَى بِنَا فِي عَرْضِ الْآرَاءِ أَنَّ جُمْهُورَ مَنْ يَرَى النَّقْضَ مِنْ مَسِّ الْفَرْجِ يُسَوِّي فِي الْحُكْمِ بَيْنَ الرَّجُلِ وَالْمَرْأَةِ، ثُمَّ إِنَّ الْحَدِيثَ قَدْ رَوَاهُ عَدَدٌ مِنْ رِجَالِ الصَّحَابَةِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ.
ثُمَّ إِنَّ دَيْدَنَ الصَّحَابَةِ كَانَ قَبُولَ أَخْبَارِ النِّسَاءِ فِي أَحْكَامٍ تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالرِّجَالِ فَقَبِلُوا خَبَرَ أُمِّ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ عَائِشَةَ - رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنْهَا - فِي الْتِقَاءِ الْخِتَانَيْنِ وَنُسِخَ بِهِ: «الْمَاءُ مِنَ الْمَاءِ»)، وَقَدْ خَاطَبَ اللهُ تَعَالَى نِسَاءَ رَسُولِهِ بِقَوْلِهِ جَلَّ ذِكْرُهُ: ﴿ وَاذْكُرْنَ مَا يُتْلَى فِي بُيُوتِكُنَّ مِنْ آيَاتِ اللهِ ﴾ (٨)، وَهَذَا أَمْرٌ لَهُنَّ بِالْبَيَانِ، وَفِي ضِمْنِ ذَلِكَ أَحْكَامٌ قَدْ تَخْتَصُّ بِالرِّجَالِ (٩).
وَبَعْدَ هَذَا النِّقَاشِ الطَّوِيلِ، فَإِنَّ الْمُحَصِّلَةَ النِّهَائِيَّةَ كَانَتْ صِحَّةَ حَدِيثَيْ بُسْرَةَ وَطَلْقٍ، فَكَيْفَ نَعْمَلُ فِيهِمَا؟
قَالَ ابْنُ عَبْدِ الْبَرِّ: (وَالْأَصْلُ أَنَّ الْوُضُوءَ الْمُجْتَمَعَ عَلَيْهِ لَا يَنْتَقِضُ إِلَّا بِإِجْمَاعٍ أَوْ سُنَّةٍ ثَابِتَةٍ غَيْرِ مُحْتَمَلَةٍ لِلتَّأْوِيلِ، فَلَا عَيْبَ عَلَى الْقَائِلِ بِقَوْلِ الْكُوفِيِّينَ؛ لِأَنَّ إِيجَابَهُ عَنِ الصَّحَابَةِ لَهُمْ فِيهِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ ذِكْرُهُ).
وَالْجُمْهُورُ عَلَى أَنَّ حَدِيثَ بُسْرَةَ نَاسِخٌ لِحَدِيثِ طَلْقٍ، وَبِهِ قَالَ ابْنُ حِبَّانَ (٢) وَالطَّبَرَانِيُّ (٣) وَابْنُ حَزْمٍ (٤) وَالْبَيْهَقِيُّ (٥) وَالْحَازِمِيُّ (٦) وَغَيْرُهُمْ.
And however the matter may be, this ruling has been narrated from eight of the Companions, some of their chains are authentic, and some are capable of being supported. Their totality in the least of its states is mashhur, and the mashhur is acted upon according to them in matters of general necessity. As for the fourth [objection], which is that the hadith is among those whose ruling is specific to men, yet a woman transmitted it, it is a rejected statement. Indeed, in the presentation of opinions, the majority of those who hold that wudu is nullified by touching the private part make no distinction in ruling between man and woman. Moreover, the hadith was narrated by a number of male Companions as previously mentioned. Furthermore, the practice of the Companions was to accept reports from women regarding rulings pertaining to men. They accepted the report of the Mother of the Believers Aisha - may Allah be pleased with her - concerning the meeting of the two circumcisions and abrogated by it the [hadith] 'water from water'. And Allah the Exalted addressed the wives of His Messenger saying: 'And remember what is recited in your houses of the verses of Allah' (33:34), and this is a command for them to convey, and within that are rulings that may be specific to men. After this lengthy discussion, the final result is the authenticity of the two hadiths of Busra and Talq. So how do we act upon them? Ibn Abd al-Barr said: 'The principle is that wudu agreed upon does not become nullified except by consensus or by an established sunna that is not open to interpretation. So there is no blame upon the one who holds the view of the Kufans, because their requiring it from the Companions has what was previously mentioned.' The majority holds that the hadith of Busra abrogates the hadith of Talq, and this was stated by Ibn Hibban, al-Tabarani, Ibn Hazm, al-Bayhaqi, al-Hazimi, and others.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage concludes a lengthy discussion on the ruling of wudu nullification from touching the private part. The author first establishes that the ruling (nullification) is narrated from eight Companions, and while some narrations are weak, the overall body of narrations reaches the level of mashhur (well-known) – a category that is acted upon in matters of general need according to Hanafis. He then refutes the objection that the hadith is specific to men but narrated by a woman, by noting that the majority of jurists who hold nullification make no gender distinction, and the hadith was also narrated by male Companions. He further supports this by referencing the acceptance of Aisha's report on the meeting of the two circumcisions (which abrogated the earlier 'water from water' rule) and the Quranic command to the Prophet's wives to convey what is recited in their homes. After establishing the authenticity of both Busra's and Talq's hadiths, the author presents two approaches: Ibn Abd al-Barr's defense of the Kufi (Hanafi) view that the established principle of wudu breaks only by consensus or unambiguous sunna, and the majority view that Busra's hadith abrogates Talq's – thus the nullification ruling stands. The passage demonstrates how differing assessments of textual strength and abrogation lead to different fiqh positions.

Key Terms
مَشْهُور (mashhur): A hadith narrated by a number of chains that does not reach the level of tawatur but is well-known and acted upon in matters of general need.
نَسْخ (naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later revelation or hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of the majority (al-jumhur) regarding the hadiths of Busra and Talq?
A) Both hadiths are invalid for wudu rulings.
B) The hadith of Busra abrogates the hadith of Talq.
C) The hadith of Talq abrogates the hadith of Busra.
D) The two hadiths are reconciliable without abrogation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends its ruling that wudu does not break by touching the private part using the mashhur category and the principle that wudu's default integrity requires clear evidence to overturn.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Definition and Conditions of Mudtarib Hadith
Definition of mudtarib hadith with linguistic and technical analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mudtarib hadith linguistically and technically, identify its conditions, and explain the Hanafi position that disruption from a single narrator suffices.

Core Idea
A mudtarib hadith is weak due to irreconcilable differences; disruption from even one narrator suffices.

This lesson introduces the concept of al-Idtirab (disruption) in hadith, a key tool for evaluating narrations in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Understanding what makes a hadith mudtarib allows you to identify weak narrations that cannot serve as primary proofs for legal rulings. The passage defines the term, addresses common misconceptions, and explores scholarly refinements by al-Zarkashi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic root
Recognize that mudtarib derives from idtirab meaning movement/conflict, as in waves striking each other. Distinguish the common active-participle form from the technically correct noun-of-place form (mudtarab).
The linguistic origin sets the conceptual framework.
2
Understand the technical definition and Ibn al-Salah's formulation
Ibn al-Salah defines mudtarib as a hadith whose narration differs among narrators, each conflicting. Note that this definition focuses on multiple narrators.
3
Consider al-Zarkashi's two objections
Al-Zarkashi objects that the definition might exclude disruption from a single narrator, which is more common. He also insists that only differences 'in a manner that affects' (i.e., irreconcilable) should count.
The Hanafi position accepts both objections, making the definition more inclusive and practical.
4
Apply the condition of irreconcilability
Not every difference condemns a hadith. Only when the conflicting versions cannot be harmonized and the narrator is shown to lack mastery in that specific hadith does it become mudtarib and thus weak.
This is the key condition for classification.
5
Derive the practical implication for Hanafi fiqh
A mudtarib hadith is not acceptable as a primary proof (dalil) for a hukm, but may be used for secondary purposes like istihsan or as support.
Connects hadith classification to legal methodology.

تمهيد
لما كَانَ الاختلاف أمراً وارداً في الحَدِيث النبوي الشريف؛ وَذَلِكَ للاختلاف في مقدار تيقظ الرواة وقوة قرائحهم، وَكَذَلِكَ بسبب اختلاف بعضهم عن بغض في مدى اهتمامهم بمروياتهم وَكَذَلِكَ أمور أُخْرَى تكون أسباباً للاختلاف فرغنا من ذكرها في الفصل الأول. وَقَد بينا آنذاك أنَّ الاختلاف يَكُون في المثن والسَّنَد فَهُوَ لَيْسَ قاصراً عَلَى المَثْن حسب بَلْ هُوَ يشمل كليهما. لذا رأيت أن أذكر في هذا الفصل أنواع الاختلافات الَّتِي تَكُون في السَّنَد والمَثْن. وَقَدْ قسمته على أحد عشر مبحثاً.
المبحث الأول
الاضطراب
الاضطراب في الحَدِيث سنداً ومتناً أمرٌ حاصل وواقع بسبب اختلاف المواهب وما إلى غَيْر ذَلِكَ من الأسباب التي تجعل اضطراباً في المتون والأسانيد، والاضطراب يحصل من رادٍ واحدٍ ويحصل من عدة رواة)، والاضطراب يَكُون في الأعم الأغلب في المدارس المتأخرة ويندر جداً في المدارس المتقدمة، وَذَلِكَ أن المدارس المتأخرة شأنها التعدد زيادة على بعد الزمان وتقاصر الهمم.
من
المطلب الأول
تعريف المضطرب لغة واصطلاحاً
الحَدِيث المضطرب (۳) أحد أنواع علم الحديث، والمضطرب: اسم فاعل من اضطرب، مأخوذ لغةً من الاضطراب بمعنى الحركة والاختلاف، يقال: اضطرب الموج، أي: ضرب بعضه بعضاً، فَهُوَ مضطرب.
وأود التنبيه عَلَى أن الشائع تسميته بـ «المضطرِب عَلَى وزن اسم الفاعل، هُوَ من باب الإسناد المجازي ()، لأن الاضطراب واقع فِيهِ لا مِنْهُ، إذ إنَّه اسم مكان، فيظهر فِيهِ اضطراب الرَّاوِي أو الرواة، فَهُوَ عَلَى الحقيقة : مضطرب بفتح الراء- وَلَوْ سمي كَذلِكَ لكان أظهر في المَعْنَى الاصطلاحي والمضطرب من الحَدِيث اصطلاحاً: هُوَ الذي تَخْتَلِف الرّوَايَة فِيهِ، فيرويه بعضهم على وجه وبعضهم على وجه آخر مخالف لَهُ.
هكذا عرفه الحافظ ابن الصلاح (۳) ، وَقَدْ استدرك عَلَيْهِ الإمام الزَّرْكَشِيَ بقوله: «قَدْ يخرج ما لو حصل الاضطراب من رادٍ واحدٍ. وَقَدْ يقال فِيهِ : نبنيه على دخوله من باب أولى، فإنه أولى بالرد من الاختلاف بَيْنَ راويين» (٤) . قُلْتُ : وهَذَا اعتراض متجة، لأن الاضطراب في الأعم الأغلب يحصل من راءٍ واحد، وَهُوَ الَّذِي يوجه الغلط فِيهِ لِمَنْ اضطرب فِيهِ. أما الاضطراب من راويين فَهُوَ أقل، وَكَذَلِكَ قَدْ يوجه الاضطراب لأحد الراويين أو للشيخ، وربما كَانَ قَدْ حدث بالوجهين.
وللزركشي اعتراض آخر فَقَدْ قَالَ: «وينبغي أن يقال: (عَلَى وجه يؤثر) ليخرج ما لَوْ روي الحَدِيث عن رَجُل مرة، وعن آخر أخرى ...»( ).
قُلْتُ: وَهُوَ اعتراض متجة أيضاً، لأن لَيْسَ كُلّ اختلاف قادحاً، بَلْ القادح الَّذِي لا يحتمل التوفيق والجمع، بمعنى أن الرَّاوِي لَمْ يضبط الحَدِيث فَهُوَ وَإِن كَانَ ثِقَة إِلا أَنَّهُ ضَعِيف في هَذَا الحَدِيث خَاصَّة.
المطلب الثاني
شرط الاضطراب
سبق أن ذكرت أن لَيْسَ كُلّ اختلاف اضطراباً، بَلْ شرط الاضطراب أمران:
Introduction
Since difference is an occurring matter in the noble Prophetic hadith, and that is due to difference in the degree of alertness of narrators and strength of their intellects, and likewise due to difference of some of them from others in the extent of their care for their narrations, and likewise other matters that are causes for difference, we have finished mentioning them in the first chapter. And we have explained then that difference occurs in the text and the chain, so it is not limited to the text alone, but rather it includes both. Therefore I saw that I should mention in this chapter the types of differences that occur in the chain and the text. And I have divided it into eleven discussions.

First Discussion
Al-Idtirab (Disruption)
Disruption in hadith, chain and text, is an occurring and real matter due to difference in abilities and other such causes that cause disruption in texts and chains. Disruption occurs from a single narrator and occurs from multiple narrators, and disruption occurs in the vast majority of cases in later schools and very rarely in early schools, and that is because the later schools are characterized by multiplicity in addition to distance in time and weakness of aspirations.

First Sub-topic
Definition of Mudtarib Linguistically and Technically
The mudtarib hadith is one of the types of hadith sciences. Mudtarib: is the active participle from idtaraba, derived linguistically from idtirab meaning movement and difference. It is said: the waves idtaraba, meaning some struck others, so it is mudtarib.
And I wish to point out that the common name is 'mudtarib' on the pattern of the active participle, and it is from the chapter of metaphorical attribution, because the disruption is located in it, not from it, since it is a noun of place. So the narrator's or narrators' disruption appears in it. So in reality it is mudtarab (with fath of the ra'), and if it were called that, it would be more apparent in the technical meaning. And mudtarib from hadith technically: it is that in which the narration differs, so some narrate it in one way and others in another way contrary to it.
Thus defined it al-Hafiz Ibn al-Salah, and Imam al-Zarkashi objected to him saying: 'It may exclude what if the disruption occurred from a single narrator. And it could be said about it: we build its inclusion by priority, for it is more deserving of rejection than the difference between two narrators.' I say: This objection is valid, because disruption in the vast majority of cases occurs from a single narrator, and it is he who directs the error to the one who is confused about it. As for disruption from two narrators, it is less, and likewise the disruption may be directed to one of the two narrators or to the shaykh, and perhaps he had narrated with both versions.
And al-Zarkashi has another objection, for he said: 'It should be said: (in a manner that affects) to exclude what if the hadith is narrated from one man once and from another another time...'
I say: This objection is also valid, because not every difference is condemnatory, but rather the condemnatory one is that which does not admit of reconciliation and harmonization, meaning that the narrator did not master the hadith, so even if he is trustworthy, he is weak in this particular hadith.

Second Sub-topic
Condition of Disruption
I have previously mentioned that not every difference is disruption, but rather the condition of disruption is two matters:
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an introductory discussion on the topic of 'al-idtirab' (disruption) in hadith as part of the study of differences in chain and text. The author begins by stating that differences in hadith are natural due to varying levels of narrator alertness and memory. He notes that differences can be in both the text (matn) and chain (sanad), and he will cover eleven types of differences. The first type is 'al-idtirab', which occurs in later schools more frequently.

Then in the first sub-topic, the author defines 'mudtarib' linguistically as 'movement and difference', giving the example of waves striking each other. He clarifies a common naming issue: though it is often called 'mudtarib' (active participle), it should actually be 'mudtarab' (passive or noun of place) because the disruption is located in the hadith itself. Technically, following Ibn al-Salah, it is a hadith narrated in conflicting ways. Al-Zarkashi objects that this definition might exclude disruption from a single narrator; the author agrees that disruption from one narrator is more common. Another objection is that the definition should include 'in a manner that affects' because not every difference is harmful; only irreconcilable differences cause weakness. This leads to the second sub-topic (incomplete) on conditions.

The discussion sets up that for a hadith to be deemed mudtarib, the differences must be irreconcilable. This is important for Hanafi fiqh because the classification of hadith affects which ahkam (rulings) are derived. A mudtarib hadith is considered weak and cannot be used as a primary proof, though it may be considered for istihsan or other secondary proofs. The author's reliance on Ibn al-Salah and al-Zarkashi shows a synthesis of hadith sciences with usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الاضطراب (al-Idtirab): A type of difference in hadith where narration conflicts irreconcilably
المضطرب (al-Mudtarib): A hadith narrated with conflicting versions that cannot be reconciled

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a hadith to be considered mudtarib according to the passage?
A) The hadith must be narrated with two conflicting versions by two different narrators.
B) The difference must be irreconcilable and the narrator lacking in mastery of that specific hadith.
C) The hadith must be from a weak narrator.
D) The contradiction must occur in the chain only.

Key Takeaway
A mudtarib hadith is weak due to irreconcilable differences; the Hanafi school accepts disruption from a single narrator as sufficient for this classification.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Definition and Conditions of Mudtarib Hadith
Definition and conditions of mudtarib hadith with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a hadith is classified as mudtarib and distinguish mudtarib from cases where preponderance or reconciliation applies.

Core Idea
A hadith is mudtarib only when conflicting versions are equal and cannot be reconciled.

The classification of a hadith as mudtarib is a critical tool in hadith criticism, directly affecting its usability as evidence in fiqh. This lesson unpacks the precise conditions that trigger this label, based on the definitions of major hadith scholars like Ibn al-Salah and Ibn Daqiq al-'Id.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Identify the term being defined: mudtarib (مضطرب). Its linguistic meaning is 'confused' or 'disturbed'.
In hadith terminology, it refers to a specific defect that weakens the hadith.
2
Identify the cause (sabab)
The cause of idtirab is the existence of conflicting narrations (ikhtilaf al-riwayat) that cannot be harmonized.
Other defects like mursal have different causes.
3
Identify the conditions (shurut)
For a hadith to be called mudtarib, the conflicting narrations must be equal in strength (tasawi) and reconciliation (jam‘) must be impossible according to the rules of hadith scholars.
If the narrations are not equal, one is preponderant and the hadith is not mudtarib.
4
Identify the preventers (mawani)
Two things prevent idtirab: (1) preponderance (tarjih) of one narration over another, and (2) the possibility of reconciliation between the conflicting versions.
If either exists, the hadith is not mudtarib.
5
Identify the effect (athar)
When idtirab is established, the hadith is judged to be mudtarib, meaning its authenticity is suspended and it is not used as proof until further clarification.
This affects its usability in fiqh istinbat.

Source Text

الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن

أحدهما استواء وجوه الاختلاف، فمتى رجح أحد الأقوال قدم ولا يعل الراجح بالمرجوح عند أهل النقد.
ثانيهما: أن يتعذر - مَعَ الاستواء - الجمع بينها عَلَى قواعد المُحَدِّثِينَ، ويغلب عَلَى الظن أن ذَلِكَ الحافظ لَمْ يضبط ذَلِكَ الحَدِيث بعينه فحينئذ يحكم عَلَى تِلْكَ الرّوَايَة وحدها بالاضطراب، ويتوقف عن الحكم بصحة ذَلِكَ الحَدِيث لِذلِكَ السبب (۱). وعلى هَذَا المَعْنَى يدور قَوْل الحافظ ابن الصَّلاح: «وإنما نسميه مضطرباً إذا تساوت الروايتان (۲) ، أما إذا ترجحت إحداهما بحيث لا تقاومها الأخرى، بأن يكون راويها أحفظ أو أكثر صحبة للمروي عَنْهُ، أو غَيْر ذَلِكَ من وجوه الترجيحات المعتمدة، فالحكم للراجحة، وَلا يطلق عَلَيْهِ حينئذ وصف المضطرب، وَلا له حكمه»(۳). وَقَدْ أكد هَذَا المفهوم الإمام ابن دقيق العيد فَقَالَ: «أشار بغض الناس إلى أن اختلاف الرواة في ألفاظ الحَدِيث مِمَّا يمنع الاحتجاج بِهِ ... فنقول هَذَا صَحِيح لكن بشرط تكافؤ الروايات أو تقاربها، أما إذا كَانَ الترجيح واقعاً في بعضها: إما لأن رواته أكثر أو أحفظ، فينبغي العَمَل بِهَا، إذ الأضعف لا يكون مانعاً من العمل بالأقوى، والمرجوح لا يدفع التمسك بالراجح ) . ويفهم مِمَّا سبق أن أحد الوجوه المختلفة إن كَانَ مروياً من طريق ضَعِيف والآخر من طريق قوي فَلاَ اضطراب، والعمل بالطريق القوي، وإن لَمْ يَكُنْ كَذلِكَ، فإن أمكن الجمع بَيْنَ تِلْكَ الوجوه بحيث يمكن أن يكون المتكلم باللفظين الواردين أراد مَعْنًى واحداً فَلا إشكال أيضاً؛ مِثْل أن يَكُون في أَحَد الوَجْهَيْنِ: عَن رَجُل وَفِي الوجه الآخر يُسَمَّى هَذَا الرجل فَقَدْ يَكُون هَذَا المسمى هُوَ ذَلِكَ الْمُبْهَم، فَلاَ اضطراب إذن ولا تعارض، وإن لَمْ يَكُنْ كَذلِكَ بأن يُسَمَّى مثلاً الرَّاوِي باسم معين في رِوَايَة وَيُسَمَّى باسم آخر في رِوَايَة أُخْرَى فهذا محل نظر؛ إذ يتعارض فيه أمران: أحدهما : أَنَّهُ يجوز أن يَكُون الحَدِيث عن الرجلين معاً.
والثاني: أن يغلب عَلَى الظن أن الرَّاوِي واحد واختلف فيه. فههنا لا يخلو أن يَكُون الرجلان معاً ثقتين أولا، فإن كَانَا ثقتين فههنا لا يضر الاختلاف عِنْدَ الكثير؛ لأن الاختلاف كَيْفَ دار فَهُوَ عن ثِقَة، وبعضهم يَقُول : هَذَا اضطراب يضر، لأَنَّهُ يدل عَلَى قلة الضَّبط ) .
قَالَ:
ولخص هَذَا التفصيل الحافظ العراقي في منظومته المسماة"التبصرة والتذكرة" إذ
مُضطَرِبُ الحَدِيثِ: مَا قَدْ وَرَدَا مُخْتَلِفاً مِنْ وَاحِدِ فَأَزْيَدَا في مَتْنِ اوْ) في سَنَدِ إِن صَحَّ فِيهِ تَسَاوِي الْخُلْفِ، أَمَّا إِنْ رَجَحْ بَعْضُ الوُجُوهِ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُضْطَرِبًا وَالحُكْمُ للرَّاحِحِ مِنْهَا وَجَبَا ) ويمكننا أن نقدّم مثالاً تطبيقياً عَلَى مَا لَا يُعَدُّ مُضْطَرِباً لِرُجْحَانِ بَعْضِ وُجُوهِ مروياته عَلَى بَعْضٍ فَقَدْ مَثَّلَ ابن الصَّلاح للاضطراب الواقع في السَّنَد قائلاً: ومن أمثلته: ما رويناه عن إسماعيل بن أمية (٤) ، عن أبي عَمْرو بن مُحَمَّد بن حريث (٥) ، عن جده حريث (٦) ، عن أبي هُرَيْرَة عن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم في المُصَلِّي: «إذا لَمْ يجد عصاً ينصبها بَيْنَ يديه فليخط خطاً» فرواه بشر بن المفضل (۷) ، وروح بن القاسم (۸) ، عن إسماعيل هكذا، ورواه سُفْيَان الثَّوْرِيّ (۱۱) عَنْهُ، عن أبي عمرو بن حريث، عن

الهوامش:

(۱۰) حريث العذري، اختلف في اسم أبيه، فقيل سليم أو سليمان أو عمارة، مختلف في صحبته. تهذيب الكمال (۱۱۵۸۸۸/۲)، وميزان الاعتدال ٤٧٥/١، والتقريب (١١٨٣).

Chapter Three: Difference in Sanad and Matn. First: Equality of the aspects of difference. So whenever one of the statements is preponderant, it is given precedence and the preponderant is not weakened by the outweighed according to the critics. Second: That it becomes impossible, with equality, to reconcile them according to the rules of the hadith scholars, and it becomes predominant in opinion that that memorizer did not precisely preserve that particular hadith. Then at that time, that single narration is judged to be confused (mudtarib), and one suspends judgment on the authenticity of that hadith for that reason. And upon this meaning revolves the statement of al-Hafiz Ibn al-Salah: 'We only call it mudtarib if the two narrations are equal. But if one of them is preponderant to the extent that the other does not resist it—such as its narrator being more memorizing or having more companionship with the one narrated from, or other such accepted factors of preponderance—then the ruling is for the preponderant, and at that time the description of mudtarib is not applied to it, nor does it have its ruling.' And Imam Ibn Daqiq al-'Id emphasized this concept, saying: 'Some people pointed out that the difference of narrators in the wording of a hadith prevents using it as proof... We say: This is correct, but on condition that the narrations are equivalent or close. However, if preponderance occurs in some of them—either because their narrators are more numerous or more memorizing—then one should act upon it, for the weaker does not prevent acting upon the stronger, and the outweighed does not repel clinging to the preponderant.' And it is understood from the above that if one of the different versions is narrated through a weak chain and the other through a strong chain, then there is no confusion, and one acts upon the strong chain. And if it is not like that, then if reconciliation between those versions is possible such that it can be that the speaker of the two uttered expressions intended one meaning, then there is also no problem. For example, if in one version it is: 'from a man' and in the other version that man is named, then that named person might be the same as the ambiguous one. So there is no confusion or contradiction. And if it is not like that—for example, the narrator is named with a specific name in one narration and with another name in another narration—then this is a matter for consideration, for two things conflict: first, that it is possible that the hadith is from both men; second, that it becomes predominant in opinion that the narrator is one and there is disagreement concerning him. Here, it is not free from the possibility that the two men are both trustworthy or not. If they are both trustworthy, then the difference does not harm according to many, because the difference, however it rotates, is from a trustworthy person. And some say: This is a harmful confusion, because it indicates lack of precision. He said: Al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi summarized this detail in his poem called 'al-Tabsira wa al-Tadhkira': 'Mudtarib al-hadith: what has come differing from one or more in matn or sanad, if the correctness of it involves equality of the conflict. But if some of the aspects are preponderant, it is not mudtarib, and the ruling for the preponderant of them is obligatory.' And we can present a practical example of what is not considered mudtarib due to the preponderance of some aspects of its narrations over others. Ibn al-Salah gave an example of confusion occurring in the sanad, saying: Among its examples is what we narrated from Isma'il ibn Umayyah, from Abu 'Amr ibn Muhammad ibn Huraith, from his grandfather Huraith, from Abu Hurairah, from the Messenger of Allah (SAW) concerning the one praying: 'If he does not find a stick to set up in front of him, let him draw a line.' Bishr ibn al-Mufaddal and Rawh ibn al-Qasim narrated from Isma'il like this, and Sufyan al-Thawri narrated from him from Abu 'Amr ibn Huraith, from... [footnotes follow]
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of mudtarib (confused) hadith, a key term in hadith criticism that has direct implications for fiqh, as unclear narrations affect legal deduction. The author first outlines two scenarios: when one narration is preponderant (rajih), it is accepted; when narrations are equal (tasawi) and cannot be reconciled, the hadith is ruled mudtarib. He then quotes Ibn al-Salah, who emphasizes that mudtarib applies only when opposing narrations are equal in strength; if one is preponderant, the term does not apply. Ibn Daqiq al-'Id reinforces this, stating that difference alone does not prevent use of a hadith as evidence; only when narrations are equivalent or close does it become problematic. If one is clearly stronger, it must be followed. The author then explains further nuances: if a weak chain conflicts with a strong chain, there is no confusion; if reconciliation is possible (e.g., a named narrator could be the same as an unnamed one), there is also no confusion. However, if the same narrator is given different names, it becomes problematic, and scholars differ: many accept it if both are trustworthy, but others consider it harmful confusion. Al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi's poetic summary is given, and an example follows: the hadith of drawing a line for prayer, where Isma'il ibn Umayyah's narration has a conflict in sanad between Bishr and Rawh versus Sufyan al-Thawri. The passage is essential for understanding how technical hadith rulings affect the strength of legal evidence in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مضطرب (mudtarib): الحديث الذي اختلفت رواياته مع تساويها وعدم إمكان الجمع بينها
اضطراب (idtirab): اختلاف الروايات مع تساويها وعدم إمكان الجمع بينها
ترجيح (tarjih): تقديم إحدى الروايات على الأخرى بسبب قوة الحفظ أو كثرة الصحبة وغيرها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is a hadith considered mudtarib according to the passage?
A) When there is any difference between narrations.
B) When one narration is clearly stronger than the other.
C) When conflicting narrations are equal in strength and cannot be reconciled.
D) When the narrator is unknown.

Key Takeaway
A hadith is classified as mudtarib only when conflicting versions are equal in strength and cannot be reconciled; preponderance or reconciliation prevents this classification.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Idtirab: Ibn al-Salah vs Ibn Hajar on Narrator Name Variation
Debate on idtirab and narrator name variation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a variation in a hadith chain constitutes idtirab and apply the Hanafi principle of reconciliation (jam') to distinguish harmless naming differences from defects.

Core Idea
Disagreement over a narrator's name does not automatically weaken a hadith; only irreconcilable contradictions that undermine reliability constitute idtirab.

In this passage, we explore a classic dispute in hadith criticism: does a difference in how narrators name a person in the chain render the hadith weak (mudtarib)? Ibn al-Salah and al-Iraqi said yes; Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani argued no, because the disagreement is only over the name, and if the person is reliable, the hadith remains sound. The Hanafi school favours reconciliation (jam') and careful weighing of proofs, so Ibn Hajar's view is preferred. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating hadith evidence in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core variation
Note the different names given for one narrator in the hadith chain: Huraith, Huraith ibn Sulaym, Huraith ibn Ammar, etc. Also note whether he narrates from his father, grandfather, or directly from Abu Huraira.
The variation is only in the name and the exact relationship, not in the text of the hadith.
2
Contrast the two positions
Ibn al-Salah and al-Iraqi considered these variations as idtirab, weakening the hadith. Ibn Hajar rejected this, arguing that idtirab requires a disagreement that affects the reliability of the narration.
Ibn Hajar's definition: 'Idtirab is a disagreement that constitutes a defect.'
3
Analyze Ibn Hajar's reasoning
Ibn Hajar states that the disagreement is only over the name or kunya of a single person. If that person is trustworthy (thiqa), his name difference does not harm. If he is weak, the hadith is weak due to his weakness, not due to the disagreement.
This reasoning shifts the focus from the mere existence of variation to the underlying reliability of the narrator.
4
Extract the definition of idtirab
From the passage, idtirab is a defect ('illa) that occurs when narrators contradict each other irreconcilably. If the contradictions can be reconciled or one version given preference (tarjih), idtirab is removed.
Hanafi usul emphasizes seeking jam' (reconciliation) before declaring idtirab.
5
Apply the principle to the hadith
The author points out that Huraith is actually unknown (majhul), so the hadith's weakness (if any) stems from his unknown status, not from the name variation. Thus, citing this hadith as an example of idtirab is incorrect.
This illustrates the importance of checking narrator reliability before applying the concept of idtirab.

الرواة في اسم رَجُل لا يؤثر؛ ذَلِكَ لأَنَّهُ إِن كَانَ ذَلِكَ الرجل ثِقَة فَلا ضير، وإِن كَانَ غَيْر ثقة فضعف الحَدِيث إنما هُوَ من قبل ضعفه لا من قبل اختلاف الثقات في اسمه فتأمل ذَلِكَ كله فالطرق الَّتِي ذكرها ابن الصلاح، ثُمَّ شَيْخُنَا قابلة لترجيح بعضها

(٤)

الخط.
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. (4) (1) 225 ... his father, from Abu Huraira. And Hamid ibn al-Aswad narrated it ... from Ismail, from Abu Amr ibn Muhammad ibn Huraith ibn Sulaym, from his father, from Abu Huraira. And Wahib narrated it ... and Abd al-Warith ... from Ismail, from Abu Amr ibn Huraith, from his grandfather Huraith. And Abd al-Razzaq said, from Ibn Jurayj: I heard Ismail, from Huraith ibn Ammar, from Abu Huraira. There is more disorder in it than what we have mentioned, and Allah knows best. Al-Hafiz al-Iraqi elaborated on the aspects of disagreement in this hadith, following Ibn al-Salah in considering it disorder. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani refuted both Ibn al-Salah and al-Iraqi, saying: All who narrated from Ismail ibn Umayya about this man only differed on his name or kunya, and whether he narrated from his father, his grandfather, or directly from Abu Huraira. If this is realized, there is no real disorder, because disorder is a disagreement that constitutes a defect. Disagreement among narrators about a man's name does not affect; because if that man is reliable, no harm; if unreliable, the weakness of the hadith is due to his weakness, not due to the disagreement of reliable narrators over his name. So consider all this; the chains that Ibn al-Salah and our shaykh mentioned can be weighted, and the stronger ones can be reconciled, thus eliminating disorder entirely. I say: Ibn Hajar's words are correct, because the correct view is not to cite this hadith as an example, since Huraith is unknown. Even if we assume his companionship, making him reliable, the narrator from him is unknown—only Ismail ibn Umayya narrated from him. So Ibn Hajar's words are correct: their disagreement was over the name of a single person; if he is reliable, the disagreement over his name does not harm; if unreliable, the weakness is for other than disorder. And the case here is similar. Upon our verification of al-Hafiz al-Iraqi's book 'Sharh al-Tabsira wa al-Tadhkira', we found a note on the margin of one manuscript: 'This hadith was declared authentic by Imam Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, and others from Abu Huraira, and they considered this disorder not harmful.' I say: Imam Ahmad's authentication is reported by Ibn Abd al-Barr. As for Ibn Hibban, he included it in his Sahih. Likewise Ibn Khuzayma and Ali ibn al-Madini authenticated it. Ibn Hajar said: 'It is good.' Others weakened it, including Ibn Uyayna, and al-Sarakhsi said: ...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a specific hadith chain dispute. It begins by presenting multiple chains of a hadith, all going back to Abu Huraira, but with variations in the narrator's name (Huraith, Huraith ibn Sulaym, Huraith ibn Ammar, etc.). Ibn al-Salah and al-Hafiz al-Iraqi considered these variations as idtirab (disorder) that weakens the hadith. Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani rebuts this, arguing that the disagreement is only about the name and whether the narrator reported from his father, grandfather, or directly. He defines idtirab as a disagreement that causes a defect in the hadith. A disagreement over the name of a narrator does not itself cause a defect; if the narrator is reliable, the hadith remains sound; if he is unreliable, the weakness comes from his unreliability, not from the disagreement. The author agrees with Ibn Hajar, adding that Huraith is actually unknown (majhul), so the hadith's weakness (if any) is due to that, not to idtirab. The author then cites a marginal note stating that Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Ibn Hibban, and others declared the hadith authentic, implying they did not consider the variation harmful. This shows that differences in the chain do not always constitute idtirab; a hadith may still be sound if the variation can be reconciled or if the narrator is reliable.

Key Terms
اضطراب (idtirab): اختلاف الرواة الذي يؤثر قدحاً في الحديث (disagreement among narrators that constitutes a defect in the hadith)
سند (sanad): سلسلة الرواة (chain of narrators)
متن (matn): نص الحديث (text of the hadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Hajar, what is the main reason why the variation in the chain of this hadith does not constitute idtirab?
A) The hadith is fabricated, so idtirab is irrelevant.
B) The disagreement is only over the name or kunya of a single narrator, which does not affect the hadith's reliability if the narrator is trustworthy.
C) The hadith is mutawatir, so idtirab cannot apply.
D) All narrators are unknown, making the hadith weak regardless.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement over a narrator's name does not automatically cause idtirab; the key is the narrator's reliability and the possibility of reconciliation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Hadith Disagreement and Its Fiqh Impact: The Sutrah Case
Analyzing a hadith dispute and its fiqh implications regarding sutrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of a hadith dispute (narrator reliability, idtirab) and explain how they affect the fiqh ruling on sutrah.

Core Idea
Disagreement over a hadith's authenticity directly shapes fiqh rulings — a weak hadith cannot establish a sunnah.

This passage takes you inside the workshop of a Hanafi usuli, examining how a single hadith about drawing a line during prayer triggered a heated debate among scholars. You will see how the reliability of narrators, the concept of idtirab (disturbance), and the classification of a hadith as shadhdh all feed into a clear fiqh outcome: whether a line can serve as a sutrah. The lesson will sharpen your ability to trace the path from hadith criticism to legal ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed hadith and its ruling
Locate the hadith about drawing a line as sutrah when no physical object is available. What is the intended ruling (instruction) from that hadith?
The hadith is from Abu Hurayrah, transmitted through Hurayth and Isma'il ibn Umayyah.
2
Analyze the sanad for weaknesses
Examine the narrators: Hurayth and the unknown narrator from him. Why do scholars like Ibn Hajar consider this chain weak? Focus on the concept of 'unknown' (majhul) and how it affects thubut.
Hurayth is unknown (majhul), and his student is also unknown — this is a double weakness.
3
Evaluate the scholars' positions
List the scholars who authenticated the hadith (Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, Ibn Khuzaymah, Ali ibn al-Madini, Ibn Hajar as hasan) and those who weakened it (Sufyan ibn Uyaynah, al-Shafi'i, al-Baghawi, al-Nawawi, al-Qadi Iyad). Note their reasoning.
Al-Sarakhsi called it shadhdh — a hadith contradicting more reliable narrations.
4
Trace the fiqh consequence
Connect the hadith's authenticity to the sutrah ruling: those who accept the hadith permit drawing a line; those who reject it require a physical object. Identify which schools fall on each side.
Hanafis and Shafi'is (new view) reject the line; Ahmad and old Shafi'i accept it.
5
Apply Hanafi usul principles
Use Hanafi hadith methodology: because the hadith is weak, it cannot be used as a basis for a sunnah ruling. The Hanafi requirement of a physical sutrah (e.g., a stick) is based on strong ahadith, so the line is insufficient.
Hanafis also consider that the line contradicts the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) who used a spear or stick.

الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن

الرواة في اسم رَجُل لا يؤثر؛ ذَلِكَ لأَنَّهُ إِن كَانَ ذَلِكَ الرجل ثِقَة فَلا ضير، وإِن كَانَ غَيْر ثقة فضعف الحَدِيث إنما هُوَ من قبل ضعفه لا من قبل اختلاف الثقات في اسمه فتأمل ذَلِكَ كله فالطرق الَّتِي ذكرها ابن الصلاح، ثُمَّ شَيْخُنَا قابلة لترجيح بعضها عَلَى بَعْض، والراجحة مِنْها يمكن التوفيق بينها فينتفي الاضطراب أصلاً ورأساً. أقول: كلام الحافظ ابن حجر صواب، إذ إن الأصح عدم التمثيل بهذا الحَدِيث؛ لأن حريثاً مَجْهُول لا يعرف، وعلى فرض التسليم بصحبته – فيكون عدلاً - فإن الرَّاوِي عَنْهُ مَجْهُول لَمْ يرو عَنْهُ غَيْر إسماعيل بن أمية، لذا فإن كلام الحافظ ابن حجر صواب، فاختلافهم كَانَ في تسمية ذات وَاحِدَة فإن كَانَ ثِقَة لَمْ يضره الاختلاف في اسمه، وإن كَانَ غَيْر ثِقَة فَقَدْ ضعف لغير الاضطراب، والحال هنا كَذلِكَ. وعند تحقيقنا لكتاب " شرح التبصرة والتذكرة " للحافظ العراقي وقفنا على تعليقة جاءت في حاشية إحدى النسخ نصها: «هَذَا الحَدِيث صححه الإمام أحمد، وابن حبان، وغيرهما من حديث أبي هُرَيْرَة، وكأنهم رأوا هَذَا الاضطراب لَيْسَ قادحاً». أقول: تصحيح الإمام أحمد نقله عَنْهُ ابن عبد البر، أما تصحيح ابن حبان فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ خرجه في صحيحه، وصححه كَذلِكَ ابن خزيمة، وعلي بن المديني، وَقَالَ ابن حجر: «هُوَ حَسَن». عَلَى أن آخرين قد ضعفوا هَذَا الحَدِيث مِنْهُمْ ابن عُيَيْنَةَ، وَقَالَ السرخسي: هَذَا الحَدِيث شاذ. قَالَ ابن حجر أشار إلى ضعفه سُفْيَان بن عيينة، والشَّافِعِي، والبَغَوِيّ، وغيرهم. وَقَالَ الْقَاضِي عِيَاض: (وإن كَانَ جاء بِهِ حَدِيث وأخذ بِهِ أحمد بن حَنْبَل فَهُوَ ضَعِيف). وضعفه كَذلِكَ النَّوَوِيّ.

وَقَدْ ترتب عَلَى حكم من حكم باضطراب الحَدِيث، اختلاف فقهي في حكم سترة المصلي، فالشترة -بالضم - مأخوذة من السِّتْر وَهِيَ في اللغة: ما استترت بِهِ من شيء كائناً مَا كَانَ، وكذا الستار والستارة، والجمع الستائر والستر. وفي الاصطلاح الشرعي: هِيَ مَا يغرز أو ينصب أمام المصلي من عصا أو غَيْر ذَلِكَ، أو ما يجعله المصلي أمامه لمنع المارين بَيْنَ يديه.
والسترة في الصَّلاة مشروعه لمنع المارين، قَالَ ابن عبد البر: «السترة في الصَّلاة سنة مسنونة معمول بها»، وقد وردت أحاديث صَحِيحَة بِهَا، وَقَدِ اختلف أهل العِلْمِ وَقَدْ فيمن ليس لديه شيء يجعله سترة لَهُ هَلْ يشرع له أن يخط خطا؟ فَقَدْ ذهب الأوزاعي، وسعيد بن جبير، والإمام أحمد، والشافعي في القديم، وأبو
Chapter Three: Disagreement in Sanad and Matn. The narrators concerning the name of a man do not affect [the hadith]; that is because if that man is trustworthy, then no harm; and if he is not trustworthy, then the weakness of the hadith is only due to his weakness, not due to the disagreement of trustworthy [narrators] in his name. So ponder all of that. The paths mentioned by Ibn al-Salah, then our shaykh, are open for preferring some over others, and the preponderant among them can be reconciled, so the disturbance is negated entirely and completely. I say: The words of al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar are correct, because the most correct [view] is not to cite this hadith as an example; because Hurayth is unknown and not known, and if we assume his [status as] Companion – then he would be 'adl – the narrator from him is unknown; none narrated from him except Isma'il ibn Umayyah. So the words of al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar are correct; their disagreement was about naming one person: if he was trustworthy, disagreement about his name does not harm him; and if he was not trustworthy, then he is weak due to something other than disturbance (idtirab). The situation here is like that. When we verified the book 'Sharh al-Tabsirah wa al-Tadhkirah' by al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi, we came across an annotation in the margin of one copy whose text was: 'This hadith was authenticated by Imam Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, and others from the hadith of Abu Hurayrah, and it seems they considered this disturbance not detrimental.' I say: The authentication of Imam Ahmad was transmitted from him by Ibn 'Abd al-Barr; as for the authentication of Ibn Hibban, it is that he included it in his Sahih; and likewise Ibn Khuzaymah and 'Ali ibn al-Madini authenticated it. Ibn Hajar said: 'It is hasan.' However, others weakened this hadith, among them Ibn 'Uyaynah, and al-Sarakhsi said: 'This hadith is shadhdh.' Ibn Hajar said: 'Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah, al-Shafi'i, al-Baghawi, and others pointed to its weakness.' Al-Qadi 'Iyad said: 'Even if a hadith comes [through other routes] and Ahmad ibn Hanbal acted upon it, it is weak.' Likewise, al-Nawawi weakened it.

The Effect of This Hadith on the Disagreement of Jurists
[Ruling: The Covering of the Worshipper with a Line When He Does Not Find Something to Set Up]. There followed, from the ruling of whoever ruled that the hadith is disturbed, a juristic disagreement on the ruling of the sutrah of the worshipper. Sutrah – with a dammah – is derived from 'sitr'; it is in language: that by which you screen yourself from something, whatever it may be; also 'sitar' and 'sitarah'; the plural is 'sata'ir' and 'sutur'. In legal terminology: it is what is planted or set up in front of the worshipper, such as a stick or the like, or what the worshipper places in front of him to prevent people passing in front of him. The sutrah in prayer is legislated to prevent passers-by. Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said: 'The sutrah in prayer is a well-established sunnah that is practiced.' There are authentic ahadith about it. The people of knowledge differed over whether, for one who does not have something to make a sutrah, it is legislated for him to draw a line. Al-Awza'i, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, Imam Ahmad, al-Shafi'i in his old opinion, and Abu
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis demonstrating how disagreement over the authenticity of a hadith (due to sanad and matn issues) can lead to juristic differences. The author begins by discussing a hadith concerning a man's name in the chain, arguing that disagreement among trustworthy narrators about a name does not harm the hadith unless the narrator himself is weak. He then examines the specific hadith about drawing a line as a sutrah (screen) during prayer, noting that scholars differed on its authenticity due to the unknown narrator Hurayth and another unknown narrator. Some scholars like Ahmad and Ibn Hibban authenticated it, while others like al-Shafi'i and al-Nawawi weakened it. This dispute directly affects the fiqh ruling: whether it is permissible for a praying person, when lacking a physical object, to simply draw a line on the ground as a sutrah. Those who consider the hadith authentic (the old view of al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and others) permit it; those who consider it weak (the new view of al-Shafi'i) do not. The passage thus illustrates the practical consequence of hadith criticism on fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
السترة (as-sutrah): What is set up in front of a praying person to prevent passers-by from crossing in front of him.
الاضطراب (al-idtirab): A defect in hadith where differing versions cannot be reconciled, causing weakness.
الشاذ (ash-shādh): A hadith that contradicts a more reliable narrator after reconciliation attempts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All scholars agree that a line suffices as sutrah.
B) Hanafis require a physical object, while some other scholars permit a line.
C) The hadith is authentic, so the line is obligatory.
D) The disagreement has no effect on the ruling.

Key Takeaway
A single hadith's authenticity, evaluated through rigorous sanad and matn analysis, can create a real fiqh divergence between schools.

Dispute on Drawing a Line as Sutra
Analyzing juristic difference on drawing a line as a screen when no sutra is available

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific hadith that triggers the dispute on drawing a line as a sutra and evaluate the Hanafi response based on hadith criticism.

Core Idea
The ruling on drawing a line as a sutra turns entirely on the authenticity of one hadith.

Scholars differ on what to do if you have no physical object to use as a sutra in prayer. Some say draw a line; others say pray without any screen. This dispute is rooted in a single hadith and the principles of hadith authentication. Understanding how the Hanafi school evaluates this hadith will sharpen your skills in textual analysis and juristic disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
What is the legal question at hand? Determine the specific action under dispute and the schools involved.
The issue is whether a praying person without a physical sutra should draw a line on the ground.
2
Examine the evidence for the permissive view
Which hadith do the opposing schools rely on? Understand why they consider it authentic and how it leads to their ruling.
The hadith commands drawing a line; those who accept it as sahih adopt the practice.
3
Analyze the Hanafi objection
Why does the Hanafi school reject this hadith? Identify the basis of their critique: isnad weakness or textual contradiction.
Hanafis consider the hadith mudtarib (disturbed) and therefore not reliable as evidence.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
How does this difference affect the actual prayer? What does each school require from the worshipper?
Hanafis: pray without any sutra or line; others: draw a line if no object is found.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preponderance)
Which position is stronger according to the text? Note the author's neutral presentation but the Hanafi stance is clear.
The Hanafi position is based on rejecting the hadith's authenticity; the text does not explicitly prefer one side.

أثر هذا الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء
حكم استتار المصلي بالخط إذا لم يجد ما ينصبه
وَقَدْ ترتب عَلَى حكم من حكم باضطراب الحَدِيث، اختلاف فقهي في حكم سترة المصلي، فالشترة -بالضم - مأخوذة من السِّتْر وَهِيَ في اللغة: ما استترت بِهِ من شيء كائناً مَا كَانَ، وكذا الستار والستارة، والجمع الستائر والستر). وفي الاصطلاح الشرعي: هِيَ مَا يغرز أو ينصب أمام المصلي من عصا أو غَيْر ذَلِكَ، أو ما يجعله المصلي أمامه لمنع المارين بَيْنَ يديه ).
والسترة في الصَّلاة مشروعه لمنع المارين، قَالَ ابن عبد البر: «السترة في الصَّلاة سنة مسنونة معمول بها» ، وقد وردت أحاديث صَحِيحَة بِهَا ، وَقَدِ اختلف أهل العِلْمِ فيمن ليس لديه شيء يجعله سترة لَهُ هَلْ يشرع له أن يخط خطا؟ فَقَدْ ذهب الأوزاعي وسعيد بن جبير والإمام أحمد والشافعي في القديم وأبو ثور إلى أن المصلي إذا لَمْ يجد ما يستتر بِهِ يخط خطاً.
والحجة لَهمُ الحَدِيث السابق، قَالَ ابن عَبْد البر: «هَذَا الحَدِيث عِنْدَ أحمد بن حَنْبَل، ومن قَالَ بقوله حَدِيث صحيح، وإليه ذهبوا، ورأيت أن علي بن المديني كَانَ يصحح هَذَا الحَدِيث ويحتج بِهِ». وذهب آخرون إلى عدم مشروعية الخط في الصَّلاة، مِنْهُمُ : الليث بن سعد والإمام مالك، وَقَالَ: «الخط باطل». والإمام أبو حَنِيفَة وأصحابه، والإمام الشافعي بمصر، وَقَدْ قَالَ: «لا يخط بين يديه خطاً إلا أن يَكُون في ذَلِكَ حَدِيث ثابت فيتبع».
The effect of this hadith on the difference of jurists: The ruling on the praying person's screening himself by a line if he does not find something to set up as a screen. And a juristic difference resulted from the ruling of whoever ruled the hadith to be disturbed regarding the ruling of the sutra of the praying person. As-sutra – with dammah – is derived from satr, and in language it is: what you screen yourself with from something, whatever it may be, likewise al-sitar and al-sitarah, plural al-sata'ir and al-sutr. In shar'i terminology: it is something that is implanted or set up in front of the praying person, like a stick or the like, or what the praying person places in front of him to prevent passersby from passing in front of him. And the sutra in prayer is legislated to prevent passersby. Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said: 'The sutra in prayer is a well-established sunnah, practiced upon.' And authentic ahadith have come concerning it. And the people of knowledge differed regarding one who does not have something to make as a sutra for himself: is it legislated for him to draw a line? So al-Awza'i, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, Imam Ahmad, al-Shafi'i in his old view, and Abu Thawr went to the view that if the praying person does not find something to screen himself with, he draws a line. Their proof is the previous hadith. Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said: 'This hadith – according to Ahmad ibn Hanbal and those who follow his view – is a saheeh hadith, and they adopt it, and I saw that 'Ali ibn al-Madini used to declare this hadith saheeh and used it as evidence.' And others went to the view of the non-legitimacy of drawing a line in prayer, among them: al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Imam Malik – he said: 'The line is invalid.' – and Imam Abu Hanifah and his companions, and Imam al-Shafi'i in Egypt. He said: 'He should not draw a line in front of him unless there is a proven hadith about it, then it should be followed.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis showing how the ruling on drawing a line (khatt) as a sutra in prayer divides scholars based on their acceptance or rejection of the relevant hadith. The author first defines the sutra linguistically and technically, then notes that sutra is a sunnah. The dispute is whether one may draw a line if no physical object is available. One group, including Imam Ahmad and al-Shafi'i in his old opinion, accepts the hadith about drawing a line, declaring it authentic, and thus permits it. The other group, including Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Malik, rejects the line, either considering the hadith weak or lacking sufficient proof. The Hanafi position is that drawing a line is not legislated; the prayer's validity is not conditioned upon a sutra. This is a well-known ruling in the Hanafi school. The author presents the arguments neutrally, showing that the difference stems from a textual disagreement.

Key Terms
السترة (al-sutra): Something set up in front of the praying person to prevent passersby from passing directly in front.
الخط (al-khatt): A line drawn on the ground as a substitute for a physical sutra.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on drawing a line as a sutra if no object is available?
A) It is recommended (mustahabb).
B) It is permissible (mubah) but not established from the Prophet.
C) It is not legitimate; one prays without any sutra.
D) It is obligatory (wajib).

Key Takeaway
The difference on drawing a line as a sutra is rooted in the authenticity of a single hadith, demonstrating how hadith criticism directly affects fiqh rulings.

Drawing a Line During Prayer: A Usul Dispute
Hanafi usul against drawing a line in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on drawing a line in prayer and evaluate how hadith authenticity influences the dispute.

Core Idea
Drawing a line in prayer is rejected by Hanafis because the supporting hadith is not deemed authentic.

This passage examines a classical usul dispute over a sunnah practice: drawing a line as a sutra. The disagreement hinges on the authenticity of a hadith narrated by Thawr. You will analyse how each school weighs the evidence and the implications for fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
What is the specific act under dispute? Is it drawing a line as a substitute for a sutra, or is it the ruling itself?
The issue is whether the line is legally valid (mashru') or not.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their proofs
List the schools on each side: those who permit (Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Ali ibn al-Madini) and those who reject (Abu Hanifa, Malik, Shafi'i in Egypt). What evidence does each side cite?
The key proof for the permitting side is the hadith of Thawr.
3
Apply usul principles to the proof
How does the Hanafi school evaluate the hadith? Consider categories of authenticity (sahih/hasan/da'if) and the principle that a weak hadith cannot establish a ruling. Does the text mention any other evidence?
The Hanafi position relies on the weak status of the hadith; they also may cite other proofs not stated here.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in fiqh? How does this affect the prayer of a Hanafi vs. a Hanbali?
Hanafis do not instruct drawing a line; Hanbalis may permit or recommend it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن ثور إلى أن المصلي إذا لَمْ يجد ما يستتر بِهِ يخط خطاً. والحجة لَهمُ الحَدِيث السابق، قَالَ ابن عَبْد البر: «هَذَا الحَدِيث عِنْدَ أحمد بن حَنْبَل، ومن قَالَ بقوله حَدِيث صحيح، وإليه ذهبوا، ورأيت أن علي بن المديني كَانَ يصحح هَذَا الحَدِيث ويحتج بِهِ». وذهب آخرون إلى عدم مشروعية الخط في الصَّلاة، مِنْهُمُ : الليث بن سعد والإمام مالك، وَقَالَ: «الخط باطل». والإمام أبو حَنِيفَة وأصحابه، والإمام الشافعي بمصر، وَقَدْ قَالَ: «لا يخط بين يديه خطاً إلا أن يَكُون في ذَلِكَ حَدِيث ثابت فيتبع».
Chapter Three: Disagreement in Sanad and Matn. Thawr [reported] that the praying person, if he does not find something to screen himself, draws a line. Their evidence is the previous hadith. Ibn Abd al-Barr said: 'This hadith, according to Ahmad ibn Hanbal and those who follow his view, is a sound hadith, and they adhered to it, and I saw that Ali ibn al-Madini authenticated this hadith and used it as evidence.' Others went to the impermissibility of drawing a line in prayer, among them: al-Layth ibn Sa'd and Imam Malik, and he said: 'The line is invalid.' And Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions, and Imam al-Shafi'i in Egypt, and he said: 'He should not draw a line before him unless there is a confirmed hadith about it, then he follows it.'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a classical fiqhi dispute regarding the ruling of drawing a line (khatt) on the ground to mark the place of prayer when no sutra (screen) is available. The text first mentions that Thawr (likely Sufyan al-Thawri) transmitted a hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructed a person who could not find a sutra to draw a line. Some scholars, including Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Ali ibn al-Madini, considered this hadith sound and acted upon it. However, other prominent imams—namely al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Imam Malik, Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions, and Imam al-Shafi'i (in his later Egyptian school)—rejected the practice. Malik declared the line invalid. Abu Hanifa and his school likewise held it impermissible. al-Shafi'i's statement indicates caution: he would only accept drawing a line if a confirmed hadith existed. The Hanafi position is that the hadith in question is not sufficiently authentic or is abrogated, hence they rely on other evidence to negate the practice.

Key Terms
الخط (al-khatt): A line drawn on the ground to mark the place of prostration or as a sutra
الاستتار (al-istitar): Screening oneself from passersby during prayer
ثور (Thawr): A narrator (likely Sufyan al-Thawri) who transmitted the hadith about drawing a line

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on drawing a line during prayer when no sutra is available?
A) It is recommended.
B) It is impermissible.
C) It is obligatory.
D) It is optional.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rejection of drawing a line in prayer exemplifies how hadith authenticity (thubut) is a fundamental usul principle that directly shapes fiqh rulings.
